Dialectic -- Jadal

The Fourth Type of Sahaba Disagreement: Ijtihad and Jadal
Author describes the fourth type of difference among Companions: divergence in opinion and ijtihad using qiyas and maslaha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the tools of ijtihad used by the Companions (qiyas and maslaha) and describe the characteristics of their jadal (brief, evidence-driven).

Core Idea
The Companions' disagreements were rooted in genuine ijtihad, not speculation, and were resolved quickly by evidence.

After covering the first three types of differences among the Companions, the author now describes the fourth: divergence due to ijtihad. When no clear text from Quran or Sunnah was found, the Companions used analogy (qiyas) and public interest (maslaha) to derive rulings. Their debates over these rulings were simple and centered on evidence, with each Companion ready to change their opinion once the proof became clear. This passage gives us a window into the disciplined, evidence-based nature of early Islamic jadal.

Guided Path
1
Locate the fourth type
Find the sentence that explicitly states the reason for this type of difference: 'اختلافهم بسبب تباين الرأي والاجتهاد'.
The author has enumerated three previous types; this is the fourth.
2
Extract the tools of ijtihad
Identify the two primary tools the Companions used when no text was available: 'القياس' (qiyas) and 'المصلحة' (maslaha).
3
Identify the examples given
Note the two specific issues mentioned: killing of a group by one person (قتل الجماعة بالواحد) and marriage during the 'idda period (الزواج بمن دخل بها وهي في عدة الطلاق).
These examples illustrate real cases where qiyas and maslaha were applied.
4
Understand the nature of their jadal
Read the description of their debates: they were simple (بسيطا), did not last long, and each was eager to follow the evidence (حريصًا على اتباع الدليل).
This contrasts with later lengthy theological debates.
5
Connect jadal to evidence
Notice the cause-effect: when evidence appeared, they would change their opinion. This shows that the goal was truth, not victory.
This principle is essential for proper adab al-bahth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
4 - اختلافهم بسبب تباين الرأي والاجتهاد فهم إذا لم يجدوا نصا في حكم : الحادثة من القرآن أو نصا من السنة اجتهدوا في البحث عن الحكم باستعمال وسائل الاجتهاد وكانوا أكثر ما يعتمدون فيه على القياس والمصلحة، وذلك ما أدى إلى اختلافهم في حكم بعض المسائل ؛ كاختلافهم في قتل الجماعة بالواحد (2) وحكم الزواج بمن دخل بها وهي في عدة الطلاق(3)، وغيرها من ما المسائل . سبق ؛ فإن الاختلاف بين الصحابة لم يكن مستفحلا كثيرًا، لأن ومع أقضيتهم وفتاواهم كانت بقدر ما ينزل من الحوادث، فهم «لم يكونوا يتوسعون في تقرير المسائل والإجابة عنها، بل كانوا يكرهون ذلك، ولا يبدون رأيًا في شيء حتى يحدث، فإن حدث اجتهدوا في استنباط حكمه، ولذلك كان ما ينقل عن كبار الصحابة من الفتوى قليلا» (4) ومع ذلك فقد جرى الجدل بين الصحابة في هذه المسائل المختلف فيها، وإن كان جدلاً بسيطا لا يطول كثيرًا، لأن كلا منهم كان حريصًا على اتباع الدليل، لذلك ما إن يظهر أمامه حتى يسارع إلى تغيير رأيه إن كان هذا الرأي مخالفا للدليل، وهذا ما نلمسه من خلال استقراء وقائع الجدل في الفروع في هذه المرحلة
4 - Their difference due to divergence of opinion and ijtihad. For when they did not find a text in the ruling of an incident from the Quran or a text from the Sunnah, they made ijtihad to search for the ruling by using the means of ijtihad, and they mostly relied on qiyas (analogy) and maslaha (public interest). That led to their difference in the ruling of some issues, like their difference in the killing of a group by one person (2) and the ruling on marriage with a woman who was entered during her 'idda (waiting period) (3), and other issues. Previously [mentioned], the difference among the Companions was not very widespread, because their judicial decisions and fatwas were only to the extent of events that occurred. They 'would not expand in setting issues and answering about them; rather they disliked that, and would not give an opinion on anything until it happened. If it happened, they would exert ijtihad to derive its ruling. Therefore, what is transmitted from the senior Companions of fatwas is little' (4). Despite that, jadal (debate) occurred among them in these disputed issues, although it was simple and did not last long, because each of them was keen to follow the evidence. Therefore, as soon as evidence appeared before him, he would hasten to change his opinion if that opinion contradicted the evidence. This is what we discern through surveying the incidents of jadal in subsidiary matters in this stage.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth type of difference among the Companions: differences arising from ijtihad when no explicit text is found. The Companions primarily used qiyas (analogy) and maslaha (public interest) as tools of ijtihad. Examples given include the case of a group jointly killing one person (whether they are all liable for qisas) and the case of marriage with a woman who was entered during her 'idda. The author notes that such differences were not extensive because the Companions did not speculate on hypotheticals; they only addressed real events. Moreover, their jadal (debate) was brief and evidence-centered: each was eager to follow the proof, and if it became clear that his opinion was contrary to evidence, he would quickly change his view. The passage concludes by stating that surveying the records of jadal in subsidiary matters confirms this characteristic. This passage is a general description of the nature of early Islamic jadal.

Key Terms
القياس
المصلحة
الجدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Qiyas and maslaha
C) Ijma' and qiyas
D) Ra'y and istihsan

Key Takeaway
The Companions' differences in opinion were due to ijtihad using qiyas and maslaha, and their debates were evidence-centered and brief.

The Simple, Evidence-Based Jadal of the Companions
Author describes Companions' jadal with the al-musharaka example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the causes of differences among the Companions and describe the nature of their jadal as reflected in the al-musharaka case.

Core Idea
Companions' jadal was simple because they prioritized evidence over opinion.

In this passage, the author explains why the Companions sometimes differed in legal rulings and how their debates (jadal) played out. The key is that their disagreements stemmed from sincere ijtihad when no explicit text was available, and their debates were brief because everyone was committed to following the truth once it became clear. The famous inheritance case of al-musharaka serves as a concrete example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example of jadal
Locate the inheritance case known as al-musharaka. Note the hypothetical objection ('Suppose their father was a donkey') and Umar's response to rule equally among siblings.
This case is also called al-himariyya or al-hajariyya due to the analogies used.
2
Extract the cause of difference
According to the author, differences arose when no explicit text from Quran or Sunnah existed. Companions then used ijtihad, relying on qiyas and maslaha, leading to varying conclusions.
Ijtihad here is the effort to derive a ruling, not yet formalized as in later usul.
3
Analyze the nature of their jadal
The author describes the jadal as 'simple' and 'not long-lasting'. Each Companion was keen to follow evidence, so when it appeared, they changed their opinion quickly.
This contrasts with later structured jadal (munazara) which has formal rules.
4
Connect to evidence-based reasoning
The key takeaway is that the Companions' debates were guided by dalil. Their commitment to truth over personal opinion made the jadal productive and short.
This principle is foundational for Islamic legal debate ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجدل هو العدل يشارك الأضعف، ولما قال له زيد: هب أن أباهم كان حماراً، أو قال أحدهم: هب أن أبانا كان حجراً في اليم، رأى عمر أن التشريك بينهم في الثلث يقسم بينهم بالسوية ذكورهم كإناثهم وسميت هذه الفريضة بالمشركة أو المشتركة أو الحمارية أو الحجرية أو اليمية. اختلافهم بسبب تباين الرأي والاجتهاد فهم إذا لم يجدوا نصاً في حكم الحادثة من القرآن أو نصاً من السنة اجتهدوا في البحث عن الحكم باستعمال وسائل الاجتهاد وكانوا أكثر ما يعتمدون فيه على القياس والمصلحة، وذلك ما أدى إلى اختلافهم في حكم بعض المسائل؛ كاختلافهم في قتل الجماعة بالواحد وحكم الزواج بمن دخل بها وهي في عدة الطلاق، وغيرها من المسائل. ومع ذلك فقد جرى الجدل بين الصحابة في هذه المسائل المختلف فيها، وإن كان جدلاً بسيطاً لا يطول كثيراً، لأن كلاً منهم كان حريصاً على اتباع الدليل، لذلك ما إن يظهر أمامه حتى يسارع إلى تغيير رأيه إن كان هذا الرأي مخالفاً للدليل، وهذا ما نلمسه من خلال استقراء وقائع الجدل في الفروع في هذه المرحلة
Jadal is [the case of] the 'adl [inheritance share] that shares with the weaker [heir]. When Zayd said to him: 'Suppose their father was a donkey,' or one of them said: 'Suppose our father was a stone in the sea,' Umar saw that sharing among them in the third [of the estate] is divided equally among them, males like females, and this obligatory share was called al-musharaka, al-mushtaraka, al-himariyya, al-hajariyya, or al-yamiyya. Their differences were due to divergence of opinion and ijtihad. So when they did not find a text regarding a ruling of an incident from the Quran or a text from the Sunnah, they exerted ijtihad in searching for the ruling using the means of ijtihad, and they relied most on qiyas and maslaha. That led to their differences in the ruling of some issues, like their difference regarding the killing of a group for one person, the ruling on marriage with one who entered [the marriage] while she was in the 'idda of divorce, and other issues. And despite that, jadal occurred among the Companions regarding these disputed issues, though it was simple jadal that did not last long, because each of them was keen to follow evidence. Therefore, as soon as evidence appeared before him, he hastened to change his opinion if that opinion contradicted the evidence. This is what we perceive through surveying the incidents of jadal in subsidiary issues in this stage.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of jadal among the Companions. It first gives an example of a famous inheritance case known as al-musharaka, where Umar ruled equally among siblings despite a hypothetical objection about the father. This illustrates how differences arose from ijtihad. The author then explains that when no explicit text existed, Companions used ijtihad methods, primarily qiyas and maslaha, leading to differing rulings on issues like capital punishment for a group killing one person, or marriage during 'idda. Yet, their jadal was simple and short because they prioritized evidence; whenever the truth became clear, they changed their stance. This reflects their commitment to following the dalil.

Key Terms
الجدل (al-jadal): Dialectic or argumentation; here it refers to the simple, evidence-based disputes among the Companions that were quickly resolved. [Note: In later usul works, jadal becomes a structured discipline with rules, whereas here it is informal.]
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Exertion of personal reasoning to derive rulings when no explicit text exists; the Companions relied on qiyas and maslaha. [Note: Ijtihad later becomes formalized with conditions (mujtahid qualifications), but here it simply means seeking the ruling.]
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning; one of the main tools the Companions used in ijtihad. [Note: Qiyas is defined more rigorously in usul al-fiqh; here it is a general method.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the primary reason for differences among the Companions in legal rulings?
A) Disagreement over the authenticity of hadith
B) Lack of knowledge of the Quran
C) Divergence in ijtihad using qiyas and maslaha when no text was found
D) Personal enmity between the Companions

Key Takeaway
The Companions' jadal was a healthy, evidence-based process that quickly resolved differences because they prioritized dalil.

Analyze a given hypothetical jadal between two Companions on a fiqhi issue: identify the cause of difference and how evidence would resolve it.
?

The Spread of Companions and Its Impact on Jadal in the Tabi'un Era
Author describes the rise of Tabi'un jurists and the impact of Sahabi dispersal on methodological differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the geographical dispersion of the Companions led to methodological diversity in jadal among the Tabi'un, and identify regional schools using the example of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib.

Core Idea
The Companions' dispersal planted the seeds of regional jadal schools.

This lesson explores the historical context of jadal in the generation of the Tabi'un (Followers). After the Companions spread across the Islamic empire, each region developed its own methodological approach to legal reasoning and debate, influenced by the Companion(s) who taught there. Understanding this diversity is key to appreciating later development of usul al-fiqh and jadal traditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim about Tabi'un jurists
The passage says that Tabi'un were trained by the Companions and took up ijtihad, fatwa, and judiciary. Notice that the author emphasizes continuity: the Tabi'un 'followed their methods' in knowledge.
This indicates transmission of jadal practices from the Companions to the Tabi'un.
2
Trace the causal link: Companions' dispersal → regional schools
The author quotes Ibn Qayyim naming four key Companions: Ibn Mas'ud (Kufa), Zayd ibn Thabit (Madina), Abdullah ibn Umar (Madina), and Abdullah ibn Abbas (Makkah). People of each city learned from the Companion(s) who settled there, creating distinct methodological schools.
This is the mechanism for methodological differences in jadal.
3
Recognize the effect on jadal
Each region adhered to the methods of their teachers, leading to different argumentative strategies and priorities in debate. The example of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib and his circle shows that even within a region, scholars gave precedence to their own transmitted knowledge.
This regional loyalty naturally created distinct debating positions and styles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : الجدل في عصر التابعين تربى فقهاء التابعين تحت رعاية من سبقوهم من الصحابة رضوان الله عليهم، وأخذوا عنهم العلم والفقه، وقد حدث ذلك حين انتشر الصحابة في أمصار الدولة الإسلامية وتنقلوا إليها لتعليم الناس أمور دينهم، وقد أنشأوا حركة علمية في كل مصر نزلوا فيه، ولدى كل واحد منهم من العلم ما قد لا يكون لدى الآخر، وكونوا مدارس منهجية في تعليمهم وكان لهم تلاميذ ينقلون عنهم العلم، فتخرج عليهم التابعون ونهجوا في العلم مناهجهم وهؤلاء هم اضطلعوا بما كان يضطلع به علماء وفقهاء الصحابة من اجتهاد وفتوى وقضاء يقول ابن القيم رحمه الله: «والدين والفقه انتشرا في الأمة عن طريق أصحاب ابن مسعود وأصحاب زيد بن ثابت وأصحاب عبد الله بن عمر وأصحاب عبد الله بن عباس، فعلم الناس عامته عن أصحاب هؤلاء الأربعة، أما أهل المدينة فعلمهم أصحاب زيد بن ثابت وعبد الله بن عمر، وأما أهل مكة فعلمهم من أصحاب عبد الله بن عباس، وأما أهل الكوفة فعلمهم من أصحاب عبد الله بن مسعود» أ - أثر انتشار الصحابة في اختلاف مناهج التابعين : ولقد كان من نتائج انتشار الصحابة وتفرقهم على الأمصار، وكذا تفقه أهل كل مصر على من حل بينهم من الصحابة؛ أن تأثر أهل كل مصر بمناهج الصحابة الذين نزلوا فيه في استنباط الأحكام واستفادتها من مظانها. وكان من الطبيعي أن يلتزم أهل كل مصر من تلك الأمصار بما وصل إليهم عن طريق فقهائهم ومعلميهم من الصحابة رضي الله عنهم وتقديمهم على غيرهم، لذلك وجدنا سعيد بن المسيب وأصحابه كانوا يرون أن علماء الحرمين
Second Section: Jadal in the Era of the Followers. The jurists of the Followers were raised under the care of those who preceded them from the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, and they took knowledge and fiqh from them. That occurred when the Companions spread throughout the cities of the Islamic state and moved to them to teach people the matters of their religion. They established a scientific movement in every city they settled in, and each one of them had knowledge that another might not have. They formed methodological schools in their teaching and had students who transmitted knowledge from them. The Followers graduated under them and followed their methods in knowledge. These [Followers] undertook what the scholars and jurists of the Companions undertook of ijtihad, fatwa, and judiciary. Ibn Qayyim, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'Religion and fiqh spread in the ummah through the companions of Ibn Mas'ud, the companions of Zayd ibn Thabit, the companions of Abdullah ibn Umar, and the companions of Abdullah ibn Abbas. Most of the people's knowledge came from the companions of these four. As for the people of Madina, their knowledge came from the companions of Zayd ibn Thabit and Abdullah ibn Umar. As for the people of Makka, their knowledge came from the companions of Abdullah ibn Abbas. As for the people of Kufa, their knowledge came from the companions of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud.' A - The Effect of the Spread of the Companions on the Differences in the Methods of the Followers: Among the results of the spread of the Companions and their dispersal across the cities, and likewise the fact that the people of each city learned from the Companions who settled among them, was that the people of each city were influenced by the methods of the Companions who had settled there in deriving rulings and obtaining them from their sources. It was natural that the people of each city adhered to what had reached them through their jurists and teachers from the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, and gave them precedence over others. Therefore, we found that Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab and his companions used to hold that the scholars of the two sanctuaries...
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage introduces jadal in the Tabi'un era. It explains that Tabi'un jurists were trained by the Companions who spread across the Islamic territories. The Companions established centers of learning, each with unique knowledge, creating methodological schools. The Tabi'un inherited their methods and carried on ijtihad, fatwa, and judiciary. The author quotes Ibn Qayyim to show how the diffusion of knowledge from four key Companions (Ibn Mas'ud, Zayd ibn Thabit, Abdullah ibn Umar, Abdullah ibn Abbas) shaped the transmission of fiqh. Then, the passage discusses the effect of this dispersal: each region adhered to the methods of the Companions who settled there, leading to distinct regional schools. For example, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib and his circle regarded the scholars of the Haramayn (Makkah and Madinah) as authoritative, illustrating how regional loyalty influenced jadal and legal methodology.

Key Terms
التابعون
المناهج
المدارس

Key Takeaway

Ra'y vs Hadith: Origins of Jadal in Usul al-Fiqh
Characteristics of Ra'y and Hadith schools and resulting dialectical debates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the methodological differences between the Ra'y and Hadith schools and explain how these differences led to the rise of dialectical debate (jadal) among the usuliyyin.

Core Idea
The Ra'y school's speculation and the Hadith school's traditionalism ignited the first great jadal in Islamic legal theory.

This passage describes the two main legal schools that emerged in the early Islamic period—the School of Ra'y in Iraq and the School of Hadith in the Hijaz. Their contrasting methodologies—one speculative and analogy-driven, the other tradition-bound—sparked intense debate. Understanding this conflict is key to appreciating how jadal became a formal discipline in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two schools and their regions
Locate which school arose in Iraq and which in the Hijaz. The text mentions 'fuqaha al-Hijaz wa fuqaha al-Iraq' and contrasts their approaches.
2
List the characteristics of the Ra'y school
Three traits: extensive branching into subsidiary issues (even hypothetical), few hadith narrations, and strict conditions on hadith acceptance.
The text says 'kathrat al-tafri' lil furu' 'and 'qillat riwayatihim li al-hadith'.
3
List the characteristics of the Hadith school
Three traits: dislike of questioning and hypothetical cases, esteem for hadith, and adherence to transmitted traditions.
The text says 'karahiyyat al-su'al wa al-masa'il' and 'al-i'tidad bi al-hadith'.
4
Trace the result of these differences
The text says scholarly debate intensified, debates were held, and jadal was conducted vigorously. Each side attacked the other's method and cast doubt on its rulings.
This is where the dialectical tradition emerges.
5
Identify the role of evidence in the debate
Each imam, mujtahid, or debater tried to support his opinion with scientific and rational proofs and evidences. This shows jadal was evidence-based, not mere polemic.
The phrase 'bi al-adilla wa al-barahin al-'ilmiyya wa al-'aqliyya' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - ظهور مدرسة الرأي ومدرسة الحديث ونشأة الجدل بينهما : ولقد تطور ذلك التنوع في الرأي واختلاف النظرة إلى مصادر الأحكام ومواردها ليتحول إلى نزاع ظاهر تميز به هذا العصر كان يشتد بين فقهاء الحجاز وفقهاء العراق في الأخذ بالرأي فمدرسة أهل الرأي التي نشأت بالعراق قد تميزت بكثرة التفريع للفروع لكثرة ما يعرض لهم من الحوادث، بل وحتى فرضها قبل وقوعها في كثير من الأحيان، كما تميزت أيضًا بقلة روايتهم للحديث واشتراطهم فيه شروطا لا يسلم معها إلا القليل (2) . أما مدرسة أهل الحديث التي نشأت بالحجاز وفي المدينة المنورة خصوصا، فقد تميزت بكراهية السؤال وفرض المسائل وتشعيب القضايا، وكذا الاعتداد بالحديث والوقوف عند الآثار (1) ، وقد اشتد النقاش العلمي بين المدرستين وعقدت المناظرات، وقام الجدل على قدم وساق وطعن كل منهما بالآخر وعاب طريقته وتشكك فيما وصل إليه من أحكام، وكان كل إمام أو مجتهد أو مناظر يحاول أن يدعم رأيه بالأدلة والبراهين العلمية والعقلية (2) .
B - The emergence of the School of Ra'y and the School of Hadith and the rise of dialectic between them: This diversity of opinion and difference in perspective on the sources and resources of rulings developed into an apparent conflict that characterized this era, intensifying between the jurists of Hijaz and the jurists of Iraq regarding the adoption of ra'y. The School of Ra'y, which arose in Iraq, was distinguished by extensive branching out into subsidiary issues due to the many events that occurred to them, and even presuming them before their occurrence in many cases. It was also distinguished by their few narrations of hadith and their imposition of conditions on it that only a few could meet. As for the School of Hadith, which arose in Hijaz and especially in Medina, it was distinguished by its dislike of questioning and of imposing hypothetical issues and subdividing cases, as well as by its esteem for hadith and adherence to transmitted traditions. The scholarly debate between the two schools intensified, debates were held, and dialectic was conducted vigorously. Each group attacked the other, criticized its method, and cast doubt on the rulings it arrived at. Every imam, mujtahid, or debater tried to support his opinion with scientific and rational proofs and evidences.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage explains the characteristics of the two main schools of legal thought and how their differences led to open dialectical debate. The School of Ra'y in Iraq is described as being more speculative: they frequently derived new rulings for hypothetical scenarios even before they occurred, and they were less reliant on hadith, imposing strict conditions on its acceptance. In contrast, the School of Hadith in the Hijaz was cautious about speculative reasoning: they disliked asking hypothetical questions and subdividing cases, and they held firmly to the transmitted traditions. The result was intense debate (jadal) between the two, with each side criticizing the other's methodology and trying to support their positions with rational and textual evidence. This passage is important for understanding the social and intellectual context of jadal among the usuliyyin.

Key Terms
مدرسة الرأي
مدرسة الحديث
الجدل

D) Use of rational proofs and evidences to support opinions.

The rivalry between the Ra'y and Hadith schools, rooted in their contrasting approaches to sources, gave rise to a vigorous dialectical tradition that shaped Islamic legal theory.

Five Factors Worsening Juristic Differences
Lists five new factors that intensified juristic disagreements in this era.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five new factors that intensified juristic differences in this era and explain how each contributed to the widening of disagreement.

Core Idea
Juristic differences were intensified by intellectual, political, and social factors beyond pure methodology.

This passage from a course on dialectical methods among usulis (jadal al-usuliyyin) identifies five key factors that worsened juristic disagreements in a certain era. Understanding these factors helps us see how the dialectical landscape shifted, making consensus harder and debates more contentious. Let's explore each factor analytically.

Guided Path
1
Locate the five factors in the Arabic
Scan the Arabic passage and identify the five numbered points. Note that the first factor is unnumbered but begins with 'افتتان الناس'. List them in order.
The Arabic enumeration starts with 1 implied, then 2, 3, 4, 5 explicitly.
2
Analyze factor 1: deep delving into meanings
Factor 1 is 'al-ghaws 'ala al-ma'ani' (delving into meanings). This intellectual approach, combined with diverse backgrounds (ahl al-ra'y vs. ahl al-hadith), naturally led to divergent conclusions.
This reflects the tension between rationalist and traditionalist methods.
3
Examine factor 2: differences in taking the Sunnah
Political and sectarian divisions (Shia, Kharijites) caused different attitudes toward the Sunnah. Each group's method of accepting hadith varied, leading to disagreements in rulings.
This shows how external factors can influence legal methodology.
4
Assess factor 3: impossibility of ijma'
The text says 'ta'adhdhur tahqiq al-ijma'' (impossibility of achieving consensus) due to sectarian distrust. Each sect no longer considered the opinions of other scholars, making ijma' unattainable.
This undermines a key source of law in usul al-fiqh.
5
Consider factors 4 and 5: widespread hadith narration and fabrication
Factor 4: 'shuyu' riwayat al-ahadith' (spread of hadith narration) meant more material, hence more potential contradictions. Factor 5: 'harakat al-wad' (fabrication movement) introduced false hadiths, further confusing matters.
These factors highlight the problem of hadith authenticity in legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج - عوامل جديدة زادت من حدة الاختلاف : ومما زاد من حدة الاختلاف وتوسيع شقته في هذا العهد أكثر من ذي قبل وبروز الاختلافات في الاجتهاد ومناهج الاستنباط وفي الأحكام والفتاوى ؛ ظهور عوامل جديدة ميزت هذا العهد عما سبقه، ومن هذه العوامل : افتتان الناس في هذا العهد بالغوص على المعاني والتعمق فيما وراء النصوص، ومن الطبيعي أن ينشأ ذلك الاختلاف فيما يتوصلون إليه من نظرا لتباين أفكارهم وخلفياتهم الفكرية وتنوع مدارسهم بين أهل الرأي وأهل الحديث . 2 - اختلاف طرقهم في الأخذ بالسنة ، فإنه نتيجة للاختلافات السياسية وظهور الفرق المذهبية والكلامية من شيعة وخوارج ؛ اختلفت مواقفهم من السنة ومن العمل بها . 3 - تعذر تحقق الإجماع، نظرا لحدوث ،الفرقة ولأن كل فرقة سحبت ثقتها من سائر علماء الفرق الأخرى، فلم تعد تعتد بشيء من قولهم وافقوا أو خالفوا. 4 - شيوع رواية الأحاديث والسنن بعد أن لم تكن كذلك. 5 - ظهور حركة الوضع في الأحاديث (3)
C - New factors that increased the intensity of differences: Among what increased the intensity of disagreement and widened its gap in this era more than before, and the emergence of differences in ijtihad, methods of deduction, and in rulings and fatwas: the appearance of new factors that distinguished this era from its predecessors. Among these factors: 1. People's fascination with delving into meanings and going deep into what lies beyond texts. It is natural that this disagreement arises in what they arrive at due to the diversity of their ideas and intellectual backgrounds and the variety of their schools between the people of ra'y and the people of hadith. 2. The difference in their methods of taking the Sunnah, as a result of political differences and the emergence of sectarian and theological groups from Shia and Kharijites; their positions regarding the Sunnah and acting upon it differed. 3. The impossibility of achieving consensus, due to the occurrence of division, and because each sect withdrew its trust from the scholars of other sects, so they no longer considered anything of their statements, whether they agreed or disagreed. 4. The prevalence of narrating hadith and sunan after they had not been like that. 5. The emergence of the movement of fabricating hadith.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage enumerates five factors that exacerbated the differences among jurists in this period. First, people began to delve deeply into meanings and beyond texts, which naturally led to divergent conclusions due to different intellectual backgrounds. Second, differences in how to take the Sunnah arose from political and sectarian divisions (Shia and Kharijites), affecting their attitudes towards the Sunnah. Third, the difficulty of achieving consensus (ijma') because each sect no longer trusted the scholars of other sects, making their opinions irrelevant. Fourth, the widespread transmission of hadith and sunan, which had not been the case before, led to more material and potential contradictions. Fifth, the emergence of hadith fabrication further complicated matters. These factors show that the dialectical context was shaped not only by methodological preferences but also by political and social changes.

الوضع في الأحاديث

D) The emergence of hadith fabrication.

Key Takeaway
The five factors show that juristic differences were exacerbated by a combination of intellectual deepening, political-sectarian divisions, loss of trust, proliferation of hadith, and forgery.

The Dynamic Nature of Jadal: Why Foundational Rules Are Debated
Author reflects on the nature of jadal, stating it becomes clearer and naturally debates the fundamental rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the foundational rules of jadal themselves become subjects of debate, using the author's reasoning.

Core Idea
Jadal's rules are not fixed; they evolve through debate because they are the root of all legal differences.

This passage offers a meta-reflection on the discipline of jadal. The author observes that jadal grows clearer over time, and that it is natural for scholars to debate its very foundations. Understanding this dynamic helps us appreciate why jadal is not a rigid method but a living tradition of rigorous intellectual exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about jadal's development
The author asserts that jadal 'grows and becomes clearer' over time. This implies that the methods and rules of jadal are refined through generations of scholars, not static.
2
Identify the second claim about debate on rules
The author says it is natural for debate to revolve around the rules themselves because they are the basic cause of differences in the branches built upon them. This is a meta-argument: the rules of argumentation are themselves arguable.
3
Connect the two claims
The development of jadal is driven by debates over its foundational rules. Clarity increases precisely because scholars question and refine the principles. Thus, the dynamic nature of jadal is a healthy sign of intellectual rigor.
This step shows that the author sees dialectical growth as organic and positive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنمو وتزداد وضوحًا كلما تقدم بهم الزمن، ولقد كان طبيعيا وبديهيا أن يدور الجدل والنقاش في هذه القواعد ذاتها، لأنها هي السبب الأساس للاختلاف في الفروع التي تنبني عليها
It grows and becomes clearer as time progresses for them, and it was natural and intuitive that debate and discussion would revolve around these very rules, because they are the basic cause of difference in the branches that are built upon them.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a reflective statement on the evolutionary nature of jadal (dialectic). The author observes that jadal does not remain static; rather, it develops and becomes more manifest (‘clearer’) over time as scholars engage with it. Furthermore, it is inevitable that the fundamental rules of jadal themselves become subjects of debate, because those rules are the root cause of disagreement in the subsidiary issues (furū‘) that depend on them. In other words, the premises and principles of argumentation in jadal are not immune from scrutiny; they are the foundations upon which all dialectical reasoning rests, and so they are naturally contested.

Key Terms
الجدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Because jadal is a modern invention.

Key Takeaway
Jadal is a dynamic discipline: its rules evolve through debate because they are the root cause of differences in all subsidiary legal matters.

The Intensification of Jadal Between Ahl al-Hadith and Ahl al-Ra'y
Author describes the intensification of jadal between two schools

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key characteristics of the Ahl al-Hadith and Ahl al-Ra'y schools that led to their intense dialectical conflict.

Core Idea
Jadal between the two schools was fueled by opposing epistemological priorities: transmitted text vs. reasoned opinion.

This lesson explores the historical context of the fierce debate between two major schools in early Islamic legal theory: the School of Hadith (Ahl al-Hadith) in the Hijaz and the School of Opinion (Ahl al-Ra'y) in Iraq. Understanding their conflicting approaches to jurisprudence is essential for grasping the development of jadal in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two schools
In the passage, find the names of the two schools and their geographic bases. Note that the conflict is between the Hadith school in Hijaz and the Opinion school in Iraq.
2
Contrast their strengths and weaknesses
Read the descriptions: Ahl al-Ra'y were strong in reasoning but weak in hadith transmission; Ahl al-Hadith were strong in memorization but weak in dialectical skill.
3
Analyze al-Razi’s quote
Imam al-Razi criticizes the hadith scholars as incapable of reasoning and dialectic. Reflect on how this quote summarizes the intellectual imbalance.
4
Understand the consequences
See how the conflict turned into enmity. The methodological differences directly fueled the jadal tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الأول : اشتداد الجدل بين مدرسة الحديث ومدرسة الرأي فقد اشتد النزاع الذي نشأ بين مدرسة الحديث في الحجاز ومدرسة الرأي في العراق خلال هذا العهد واستفحل أثره حتى تحول إلى عداء وخصومة، إذ كان أهل الرأي على جانب عظيم من قوة البحث والنظر، وإن كانوا على قلة الحديث والأثر، لأسباب ذكرناها من قبل، فكانوا يحتاطون في الرواية، ويعتنون باستنباط المعاني من النصوص لبناء الأحكام عليها، فأكثروا من القياس ومهروا فيه وقدموه على الحديث الصحيح إذا خالفه من كل وجه، وكذلك ردوا الحديث إذا كان في واقعة تعم بها البلوى وأسرفوا في الطعن على أهل الحديث ومنهجهم، وانتقصوا من قدرهم وقيمتهم وعابوا عليهم الإكثار من الرواية التي هي مظنة لقلة التدبر والفهم وكان أهل الحديث على كثرة روايتهم وحفظهم للحديث ومتنه ودرايتهم برجاله وسنده على جانب من الخمول والكسل، عاجزين عن الجدال والنظر، كما قال الإمام الرازي فيهم: «أما أصحاب الحديث فكانوا حافظين لأخبار رسول الله إلا أنهم كانوا عاجزين عن النظر والجدل وكلما أورد عليهم أحد من أصحاب الرأي سؤالاً أو إشكالا أسقطوا في أيديهم عاجزين متحيرين غير قادرين على الرد على خصومهم والانتصار لطريقتهم ... وكانوا يعيبون على أهل الرأي طريقتهم ويرمونهم بأنهم يأخذون في دينهم بالظن، ويقدمون القياس الجلي على خبر الواحد ويردون الحديث إن خالف القياس
First Requirement: The Intensification of Dialectic between the School of Hadith and the School of Opinion. The conflict that arose between the Hadith school in the Hijaz and the Opinion school in Iraq intensified during this era, and its effect became severe until it turned into enmity and hostility. For the people of opinion had great strength in investigation and reasoning, although they were few in hadith and tradition, for reasons we mentioned earlier. They were cautious in narration and devoted themselves to deriving meanings from texts to build rulings upon them. They extensively used analogical reasoning and excelled in it, and they gave it precedence over the authentic hadith if it contradicted it in every respect. Likewise, they rejected the hadith if it pertained to an incident that was widespread in affliction. They exaggerated in attacking the people of hadith and their methodology, belittled their status and value, and criticized them for excessive narration, which is a cause for lack of reflection and understanding. The people of hadith, despite their abundant narration and memorization of hadith and its text and their knowledge of its narrators and chain, were characterized by lethargy and laziness, incapable of dialectic and reasoning. As Imam al-Razi said about them: 'As for the hadith scholars, they were memorizers of the reports of the Messenger of God, but they were incapable of reasoning and dialectic. Whenever one of the people of opinion presented a question or problem to them, they were frustrated, confused, and unable to answer their opponents or defend their methodology... They criticized the people of opinion for their methodology and accused them of taking their religion by conjecture, giving precedence to clear analogy over a single-narrator report, and rejecting the hadith if it contradicted analogy.'
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a historical description of the intensification of debate (jadal) between two major schools in early Islamic legal theory: the School of Hadith (based in Hijaz) and the School of Opinion (based in Iraq). The author describes how the conflict escalated from mere disagreement to outright hostility. He characterizes the Ahl al-Ra'y as being strong in investigation and reasoning but weak in hadith transmission, leading them to prioritize analogical reasoning (qiyas) over authentic hadith when there was a conflict. They were also critical of the Ahl al-Hadith for their heavy reliance on narration without deep reflection. In contrast, the Ahl al-Hadith are described as memorizers of hadith but lacking in dialectical skill and intellectual vigor, as quoted from Imam al-Razi. Al-Razi's quote emphasizes that the hadith scholars were unable to respond to objections from the Ahl al-Ra'y, and that the Ahl al-Ra'y criticized the Ahl al-Hadith for using weak reasoning. This passage sets the stage for understanding the methodological differences that fueled the jadal tradition in usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
الجدل
القياس
أهل الرأي
أهل الحديث

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) They relied excessively on narration without deep reflection.
C) They rejected authentic hadith.
D) They were weak in memorization.

Key Takeaway
The intensification of jadal between the two schools arose from their opposing methodological commitments: narration vs. reasoning.

Methodological Foundations of Jadal in Usul al-Fiqh
Author describes methodological differences fueling jadal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Ahl al-Ra'y's reliance on qiyas and the Ahl al-Hadith's prioritization of narration created tension.

Core Idea
The methodological divide between qiyas and hadith transmission was the core of the jadal.

This lesson dives deeper into the methodological differences between the two schools as described in the passage. Understanding how each school approached legal evidence is key to grasping the rules of jadal in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key methods
In the passage, find the two primary methods: qiyas (analogical reasoning) used by Ahl al-Ra'y, and riwaya (narration) emphasized by Ahl al-Hadith.
2
Describe how Ahl al-Ra'y used qiyas
Note that they extensively used qiyas, excelled in it, and even prioritized it over authentic hadith if there was a contradiction.
3
Describe how Ahl al-Hadith focused on narration
They were memorizers and transmitters of hadith, but were criticized for lacking reflection and dialectical skill.
4
Explain the conflict
The tension arose because each school elevated its preferred method. This led to disputes over the hierarchy of evidence.

First Requirement: The Intensification of Dialectic between the School of Hadith and the School of Opinion. The conflict that arose between the Hadith school in the Hijaz and the Opinion school in Iraq intensified during this era, and its effect became severe until it turned into enmity and hostility. For the people of opinion had great strength in investigation and reasoning, although they were few in hadith and tradition, for reasons we mentioned earlier. They were cautious in narration and devoted themselves to deriving meanings from texts to build rulings upon them. They extensively used analogical reasoning and excelled in it, and they gave it precedence over the authentic hadith if it contradicted it in every respect. Likewise, they rejected the hadith if it pertained to an incident that was widespread in affliction. They exaggerated in attacking the people of hadith and their methodology, belittled their status and value, and criticized them for excessive narration, which is a cause for lack of reflection and understanding. The people of hadith, despite their abundant narration and memorization of hadith and its text and their knowledge of its narrators and chain, were characterized by lethargy and laziness, incapable of dialectic and reasoning. As Imam al-Razi said about them: 'As for the hadith scholars, they were memorizers of the reports of the Messenger of God, but they were incapable of reasoning and dialectic. Whenever one of the people of opinion presented a question or problem to them, they were frustrated, confused, and unable to answer their opponents or defend their methodology... They criticized the people of opinion for their methodology and accused them of taking their religion by conjecture, giving precedence to clear analogy over a single-narrator report, and rejecting the hadith if it contradicted analogy.'

This passage is a historical description of the intensification of debate (jadal) between two major schools in early Islamic legal theory: the School of Hadith (based in Hijaz) and the School of Opinion (based in Iraq). How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Based on the passage, write a short argument from the perspective of an Ahl al-Ra'y scholar defending the use of qiyas over a hadith that contradicts it.
?
Write a counter-argument from the perspective of an Ahl al-Hadith scholar criticizing the Ahl al-Ra'y for relying on conjecture.

The Debate Between Hadith and Ra'y Schools
Author describes the intense debate between Hadith and Ra'y schools, quoting al-Razi's critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the key points of the debate between the Hadith and Ra'y schools as described by al-Razi, and identify the methodological differences between them.

Core Idea
The Hadith school had memorization but lacked jadal; the Ra'y school had qiyas but was accused of conjecture.

This passage plunges you into the heart of one of early Islam's most consequential intellectual conflicts: the clash between the School of Hadith in Hijaz and the School of Opinion (Ra'y) in Iraq. Understanding this debate is essential for grasping how later legal theory (usul al-fiqh) developed. Here, the author describes the strengths and weaknesses of each side, culminating in Imam al-Razi's sharp critique of the Hadith school's dialectical incapacity. As you work through this lesson, you will learn to dissect a historical dialectical exchange using the tools of jadal (argumentation theory).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two schools and their geographical centers
Locate the two schools mentioned: the School of Hadith in Hijaz and the School of Opinion (Ra'y) in Iraq. Note the author's description of each: the Ra'y school is strong in investigation and reasoning but weak in hadith; the Hadith school is strong in narration and memorization but weak in argumentation.
This sets the stage for the dialectical conflict.
2
Extract the methodological claims of each school
From the passage, list what each school prioritizes. The Ra'y school: uses qiyas extensively, prioritizes it over authentic hadith, rejects hadith in cases of 'umum al-balwa. The Hadith school: criticizes the Ra'y school for relying on conjecture and for giving precedence to qiyas over khabar al-wahid.
These are the core epistemic positions in the debate.
3
Analyze al-Razi's critique of the Hadith school
Read al-Razi's quoted statement carefully. He says the hadith scholars were 'preservers of reports' but 'incapable of reasoning and debate'. When challenged by the Ra'y school, they were humiliated and unable to respond. Identify what specific weakness al-Razi is highlighting: lack of dialectical skill (jadal).
This critique is a key move in the debate: it defines the Hadith school's deficiency.
4
Reconstruct the debate structure using jadal terminology
Frame the exchange as a dialectical encounter. The Ra'y school's position (mudda'i): qiyas can override khabar al-wahid. The Hadith school's objection (mu'tarid): that is conjecture and contradicts prophetic tradition. According to al-Razi, the Hadith school failed to provide an effective response (jawab), thus losing the debate by the rules of jadal.
This step applies the debate_map tool to the historical account.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الأول : اشتداد الجدل بين مدرسة الحديث ومدرسة الرأي من رواية فقد اشتد النزاع الذي نشأ بين مدرسة الحديث في الحجاز ومدرسة الرأي في العراق خلال هذا العهد واستفحل أثره حتى تحول إلى عداء وخصومة، إذ كان أهل الرأي على جانب عظيم من قوة البحث والنظر، وإن كانوا على قلة الحديث والأثر، لأسباب ذكرناها من قبل، فكانوا يحتاطون في الرواية، ويعتنون باستنباط المعاني من النصوص لبناء الأحكام عليها، فأكثروا من القياس ومهروا فيه وقدموه على الحديث الصحيح إذا خالفه من كل وجه، وكذلك ردوا الحديث إذا كان في واقعة تعم بها البلوى وأسرفوا في الطعن على أهل الحديث ومنهجهم، وانتقصوا من قدرهم وقيمتهم وعابوا عليهم الإكثار من الرواية التي هي مظنة لقلة التدبر والفهم وكان أهل الحديث على كثرة روايتهم وحفظهم للحديث ومتنه ودرايتهم برجاله وسنده على جانب من الخمول والكسل، عاجزين عن الجدال والنظر، كما قال الإمام الرازي فيهم : «أما أصحاب الحديث فكانوا حافظين لأخبار رسول الله إلا أنهم كانوا عاجزين عن النظر والجدل وكلما أورد عليهم أحد من أصحاب الرأي سؤالاً أو إشكالا أسقطوا في أيديهم عاجزين متحيرين قادرين على الرد على خصومهم والانتصار لطريقتهم ... وكانوا يعيبون على أهل الرأي طريقتهم ويرمونهم بأنهم يأخذون في دينهم بالظن، ويقدمون القياس الجلي على خبر الواحد ويردون الحديث إن خالف القياس .
The First Topic: The Intensification of Dialectical Dispute between the School of Hadith and the School of Opinion. The dispute that arose between the School of Hadith in Hijaz and the School of Opinion in Iraq during this era intensified and its effect became grave until it turned into enmity and hostility. For the people of opinion were extremely powerful in investigation and reasoning, although they were lacking in Hadith and reports, due to reasons we mentioned before. So they were cautious in narration, and concerned with deriving meanings from texts to build rulings upon them. They greatly used analogical reasoning (qiyas) and excelled in it, and they prioritized it over authentic hadith if it conflicted with it in every aspect. Likewise, they rejected the hadith if it pertained to an incident of widespread affliction, and they went to excess in attacking the people of hadith and their methodology, belittling their status and worth, and criticizing them for excessive narration, which is a cause for lack of reflection and understanding. The people of hadith, despite their abundant narration and memorization of hadith, its text, and their knowledge of its narrators and chain, were on a side of inertia and laziness, incapable of argumentation and reasoning, as Imam al-Razi said regarding them: 'As for the hadith scholars, they were preservers of the reports of the Messenger of God, but they were incapable of reasoning and debate. Whenever one of the people of opinion posed a question or difficulty to them, they were humiliated, incapable, and bewildered, unable to respond to their opponents and defend their own methodology... And they used to criticize the people of opinion for their methodology and accuse them of relying on conjecture in their religion, and of giving precedence to clear analogical reasoning over a solitary report (khabar al-wahid), and of rejecting the hadith if it contradicted analogical reasoning.'
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage describes the historical conflict between two major schools of thought in early Islamic legal theory: the School of Hadith (centered in Hijaz) and the School of Opinion (centered in Iraq). The author first outlines the characteristics of the Ra'y school: they were strong in reasoning and deduction but weak in hadith transmission. They relied heavily on qiyas (analogical reasoning) and would prioritize it over hadith if there was conflict. They also rejected hadith regarding widely prevalent matters. They criticized the Hadith school for excessive narration without reflection. In contrast, the Hadith school had abundant memorization but were incapable of dialectical argumentation (jadal). The author then quotes Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, who confirms that the hadith scholars were preservers of reports but unable to engage in reasoning and debate, and were easily refuted by the people of opinion. Al-Razi also notes that the hadith scholars would criticize the people of opinion for relying on conjecture and prioritizing qiyas over sound hadith. This passage sets the stage for understanding the methodological differences that led to intense debates between the two schools.

Key Terms
القياس
الخبر الواحد
الجدل

A) Consensus (ijma')
B) Analogical reasoning (qiyas)
C) The opinion of the Companion
D) The apparent meaning of the Qur'an

Key Takeaway
The conflict between the two schools revolved around the authority of qiyas versus the authority of hadith, and the competence in dialectical argumentation was a decisive factor in the debate.

Analysing the Debate on Raising Hands between Abu Hanifa and al-Awza'i
Dialectical exchange between Abu Hanifa and al-Awza'i on raising hands in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical exchange between Abu Hanifa and al-Awza'i, identify the grounds of each argument, and evaluate who wins by the rules of jadal.

Core Idea
In jadal, the strength of an argument depends not only on the chain but on the juristic knowledge of the narrators.

This lesson presents a classical debate between the imams of two major schools: Abu Hanifa (Ra'y school) and al-Awza'i (Hadith school). They differ on the practice of raising hands in prayer. Each presents a hadith. But the debate turns on a deeper methodological point: should we prefer a chain based on the fiqh of its narrators? Let's analyse this exchange step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions
Al-Awza'i claims that the Prophet raised hands at opening, bowing, and rising. Abu Hanifa claims that the Prophet only raised hands at the opening of prayer.
2
Extract the evidence
Al-Awza'i cites his chain: al-Zuhri from Salim from Ibn 'Umar. Abu Hanifa cites his chain: Hammad from Ibrahim from 'Alqama and al-Aswad from Ibn Mas'ud.
Both chains are from Companions but differ in content.
3
Understand the objection
Al-Awza'i questions why Abu Hanifa would prefer his chain over one that includes a Companion (Ibn 'Umar). This is a challenge to the strength of Abu Hanifa's evidence.
4
Analyse Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa argues that his narrators are more jurisprudentially knowledgeable (afqah) than al-Awza'i's: Hammad > al-Zuhri, Ibrahim > Salim, and 'Alqama and al-Aswad are not inferior to Ibn 'Umar in virtue.
This introduces the criterion of fiqh al-ruwat.
5
Assess the debate outcome
The author notes that both agreed on acting upon hadith, but Abu Hanifa prioritised narrators' fiqh. No clear winner is declared; the debate is described as innocent, each seeking truth. The key takeaway is the methodological tool of evaluating narrators' juristic knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وظهر المتعصبون من كلا الفريقين فاشتد الخلاف واحتدم النزاع وأخذ كل فريق ينتصر لطريقته ويدافع عن مذهبه بكل ما أوتيه من حجة، وأسرفوا في الغلو على بعضهم البعض، وإن كان هذا الغلو لم يتخط دائرة الأدب ولم يدخل دائرة التكفير والتفسيق والاتهام بالابتداع ويمكننا أن ننقل هنا واحدة من المجادلات التي جرت بين علماء المدرستين . لنرى كيف أن اختلاف الطرق وتشعب الأنظار، أوجد الاختلاف في الفروع. اجتمع أبو حنيفة والأوزاعي في دار الخياطين بمكة المكرمة، فقال الأوزاعي لأبي حنيفة : ما لكم لا ترفعون أيديكم عند الركوع وعند الرفع منه، فقال أبو حنيفة : لأجل أنه لم يصح عن رسول الله الله أنه كان يرفع يديه إذا افتتح الصلاة وعند الركوع وعند الرفع، قال : كيف؟ وقد حدثني الزهري سالم عن أبيه عن رسول الله الا الله أنه كان يرفع يديه إذا افتتح الصلاة وعند الركوع وعند الرفع، فقال أبو حنيفة : حدثنا حماد عن إبراهيم عن علقمة والأسود عن ابن مسعود عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كان لا يرفع يديه إلا عند افتتاح الصلاة، ولا يعود إلى شيء من ذلك، فقال الأوزاعي : أحدثك عن الزهري عن سالم عن أبيه، وتقول حماد عن إبراهيم، فقال : أبو حنيفة : كان حماد أفقه من الزهري، وكان إبراهيم أفقه من سالم، وعلقمة ليس بدون ابن عمر، وإن كان لابن عمر صحبة أو له فضل صحبة، فالأسود له فضل كثير .. فالاثنان متفقان على العمل بالحديث، ولكن أبا حنيفة لاحظ أولا فقه الرواة، والمناظرة هنا بريئة لا يقصد بها كل منهما إلا إحقاق الحق
And the extremists from both factions appeared, so the disagreement intensified and the dispute heated up. Each faction began to support its own method and defend its school with every argument it had. They went to excess in exaggeration against each other, although this exaggeration did not transgress the bounds of decency and did not enter the realm of declaring disbelief or sin or accusation of innovation. And we can narrate here one of the debates that occurred between scholars of the two schools, to see how the difference in methods and divergence of views created disagreement in subsidiary matters. Abu Hanifa and al-Awza'i met in the tailors' market in Mecca. Al-Awza'i said to Abu Hanifa: 'Why do you not raise your hands at the time of bowing and at rising from it?' Abu Hanifa said: 'Because it has not been authentically reported from the Messenger of God that he used to raise his hands at the opening of prayer, at bowing, and at rising.' Al-Awza'i said: 'How can that be? For al-Zuhri narrated to me from Salim from his father from the Messenger of God that he used to raise his hands at the opening of prayer, at bowing, and at rising.' Abu Hanifa said: 'Hammad narrated to us from Ibrahim from 'Alqama and al-Aswad from Ibn Mas'ud from the Messenger of God that he did not raise his hands except at the opening of prayer and did not return to that again.' Al-Awza'i said: 'I narrate to you from al-Zuhri from Salim from his father, and you say Hammad from Ibrahim?' Abu Hanifa said: 'Hammad was more jurisprudentially knowledgeable (afqah) than al-Zuhri, and Ibrahim was more knowledgeable than Salim, and 'Alqama is not less than Ibn 'Umar, and even if Ibn 'Umar had companionship or virtue of companionship, al-Aswad has great virtue... So both are agreed upon acting upon hadith, but Abu Hanifa first noted the fiqh of the narrators, and this debate is innocent; each of them only intended to establish the truth.'
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage provides a concrete example of a dialectical exchange (jadal) between two prominent scholars from the two schools: Abu Hanifa (representing the Ra'y school) and al-Awza'i (representing the Hadith school). The debate took place in Mecca regarding the practice of raising hands during prayer. Al-Awza'i presented a hadith from al-Zuhri through Salim from Ibn 'Umar that the Prophet raised his hands at opening, bowing, and rising. Abu Hanifa countered with a hadith from Hammad through Ibrahim from 'Alqama and al-Aswad from Ibn Mas'ud that the Prophet only raised hands at the opening. When al-Awza'i questioned the precedence of his narration, Abu Hanifa argued that the narrators in his chain (Hammad, Ibrahim, 'Alqama, al-Aswad) were more knowledgeable in fiqh than those in al-Awza'i's chain (al-Zuhri, Salim, Ibn 'Umar). Abu Hanifa did not reject the hadith but prioritized the understanding (fiqh) of the narrators. The author comments that both were acting on hadith but Abu Hanifa considered the juristic acumen of the narrators. This illustrates the methodological difference: the Ra'y school evaluated narrators' fiqh, while the Hadith school focused on the chain's authenticity. The debate is described as innocent, each seeking truth. This passage directly demonstrates the conflict described earlier.

Key Terms
المدرسة
فقه الرواة
مناظرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His chain was more authentic in terms of transmission
B) The narrators in his chain were more trustworthy
C) The narrators in his chain were more jurisprudentially knowledgeable (afqah)
D) Al-Awza'i's chain was broken

Key Takeaway
In jadal, a scholar may prefer one hadith over another by evaluating the juristic knowledge (fiqh) of the narrators, not just the chain's authenticity.

The Structure of Jadal in the Abu Hanifa–al-Awza'i Debate
Author narrates a civil dialectical exchange between two scholars on a fiqh issue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key elements of a jadal exchange (claim, evidence, objection, response) in the debate between Abu Hanifa and al-Awza'i.

Core Idea
Jadal is a structured method of inquiry where each party seeks truth through claim, evidence, and critique.

This passage presents a live example of jadal (dialectical debate) between two towering scholars of early Islam. By analysing their exchange, you will see how fiqh disagreements are rooted in methodological principles, not mere obstinacy. The debate follows a clear structure: a question, a hadith citation, a counter-citation, and a methodological justification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim (mudda'i)
Al-Awza'i asks Abu Hanifa why his school does not raise hands during prayer at certain points. This question implies a claim that raising hands is sunna. Note the implicit position.
The claim is not stated directly but inferred from the question.
2
Extract the evidence (dalil) from each side
Al-Awza'i cites a hadith from al-Zuhri → Salim → Ibn 'Umar. Abu Hanifa counters with a hadith from Hammad → Ibrahim → 'Alqama and al-Aswad → Ibn Mas'ud. Both use prophetic reports.
Both rely on hadith, not personal opinion.
3
Analyse the objection (mu'arada) and response (jawab)
Al-Awza'i objects to Abu Hanifa's chain: 'I narrate to you from al-Zuhri... and you say Hammad from Ibrahim?' meaning his chain seems stronger. Abu Hanifa responds by evaluating the juristic knowledge (fiqh) of the narrators, arguing that his narrators were more juristically learned.
This is the crux: Abu Hanifa introduces a non-chain criterion.
4
Determine the outcome by jadal rules
The author notes both intended truth, and the debate remained civil. No winner is declared; instead, the methodological difference is highlighted. According to rules of jadal, the debate ends when one party accepts the other's evidence, but here it remains unresolved, illustrating a valid methodological divergence.
In later munazara, the burden of proof would require the respondent (Abu Hanifa) to defend his position, which he does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وظهر المتعصبون من كلا الفريقين فاشتد الخلاف واحتدم النزاع وأخذ كل فريق ينتصر لطريقته ويدافع عن مذهبه بكل ما أوتيه من حجة، وأسرفوا في الغلو على بعضهم البعض (1) ، وإن كان هذا الغلو لم يتخط دائرة الأدب ولم يدخل دائرة التكفير والتفسيق والاتهام بالابتداع ويمكننا أن ننقل هنا واحدة من المجادلات التي جرت بين علماء المدرستين . لنرى كيف أن اختلاف الطرق وتشعب الأنظار، أوجد الاختلاف في الفروع. اجتمع أبو حنيفة والأوزاعي في دار الخياطين بمكة المكرمة، فقال الأوزاعي لأبي حنيفة : ما لكم لا ترفعون أيديكم عند الركوع وعند الرفع منه، فقال أبو حنيفة : لأجل أنه لم يصح عن رسول الله الله أنه كان يرفع يديه إذا افتتح الصلاة وعند الركوع وعند الرفع، قال : كيف؟ وقد حدثني الزهري سالم عن أبيه عن رسول الله الا الله أنه كان يرفع يديه إذا افتتح الصلاة وعند الركوع وعند الرفع، فقال أبو حنيفة : حدثنا حماد عن إبراهيم عن علقمة والأسود عن ابن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كان لا يرفع يديه إلا عند افتتاح الصلاة، ولا يعود إلى شيء من ذلك، فقال الأوزاعي : أحدثك عن الزهري عن سالم عن أبيه، وتقول حماد عن إبراهيم، فقال : أبو حنيفة : كان حماد أفقه من الزهري، وكان من سالم، وعلقمة ليس بدون ابن عمر، وإن كان لابن عمر صحبة أو له فضل صحبة، فالأسود له فضل كثير .. فالاثنان متفقان على العمل بالحديث، ولكن أبا حنيفة لاحظ أولا فقه الرواة، والمناظرة هنا بريئة لا يقصد بها كل منهما إلا إحقاق الحق
And the fanatics from both groups appeared, so the conflict intensified and the dispute heated up, and each group began to support its way and defend its school with all the argument it possessed, and they went to extremes in exaggeration against each other, though this exaggeration did not exceed the bounds of courtesy and did not enter the realm of declaring disbelief, corruption, or accusation of innovation. And we can here transmit one of the debates that took place between the scholars of the two schools, so that we may see how the difference in methods and the branching of views created difference in branches. Abu Hanifa and al-Awza'i met in the house of the tailors in Mecca. Al-Awza'i said to Abu Hanifa: 'Why do you not raise your hands at bowing and when rising from it?' Abu Hanifa said: 'Because it has not been reliably transmitted from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) that he used to raise his hands when opening the prayer, at bowing, and when rising from it.' [Al-Awza'i] said: 'How is that? Al-Zuhri narrated to me from Salim from his father from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) that he used to raise his hands when opening the prayer, at bowing, and when rising from it.' Abu Hanifa said: 'Hammad narrated to us from Ibrahim from 'Alqama and al-Aswad from Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used not to raise his hands except when opening the prayer, and he would not return to that.' Al-Awza'i said: 'I narrate to you from al-Zuhri from Salim from his father, and you say Hammad from Ibrahim?' Abu Hanifa said: 'Hammad was more juristically learned than al-Zuhri, and Ibrahim was better than Salim, and 'Alqama is not inferior to Ibn 'Umar, and if Ibn 'Umar had companionship [of the Prophet], 'Alqama has great merit... So the two agreed on acting on hadith, but Abu Hanifa first considered the fiqh of the narrators, and the debate here is innocent, each of them intending only to establish the truth.'
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (jadal) between Abu Hanifa and al-Awza'i, illustrating how differences in methodological principles (usul) lead to differing rulings (furu'). The author first notes the emergence of partisanship among followers of the two schools (likely the Kufan and Medinan schools) but emphasizes that civility was maintained. The debate centers on the practice of raising hands during prayer (raf' al-yadayn). Al-Awza'i cites a hadith from al-Zuhri → Salim → Ibn 'Umar that the Prophet used to raise hands at the opening takbir, before bowing, and when rising from bowing. Abu Hanifa responds with a contradictory hadith via Hammad → Ibrahim → 'Alqama and al-Aswad → Ibn Mas'ud that the Prophet only raised hands at the opening takbir. When al-Awza'i challenges the strength of Abu Hanifa's chain, Abu Hanifa replies by evaluating the juristic knowledge (fiqh) of the narrators: Hammad was more learned than al-Zuhri, Ibrahim better than Salim, and 'Alqama equal to Ibn 'Umar in merit, though Ibn 'Umar had the advantage of companionship. Abu Hanifa's argument is that the fiqh (legal understanding) of the narrators is a valid criterion for preferring one hadith over another. The author concludes that both agreed on acting on hadith, but Abu Hanifa prioritized the juristic acumen of the transmitters. The debate is presented as a model of civil disagreement, each seeking truth.

Key Terms
المناظرة
الرفع

D) Al-Zuhri's hadith contradicts the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
The debate shows that fiqh disagreements often stem from different methodological principles in evaluating evidence, not from disregard for hadith.

Fiqh al-Ruwat: The Hanafi Criterion for Hadith Preference
The principle is articulated in Abu Hanifa's response to al-Awza'i's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Abu Hanifa's criterion of fiqh al-ruwat (juristic understanding of narrators) functions as a tool for preferring one hadith over another.

Core Idea
Fiqh al-ruwat prioritizes narrators' legal comprehension over mere transmission continuity.

In the debate, Abu Hanifa introduces a non-chain criterion: the juristic knowledge of the narrators. This principle, known as fiqh al-ruwat, became a distinctive feature of Hanafi methodology. By examining his argument, you will see how a narrator's understanding of fiqh can outweigh apparent chain strength.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle being invoked
Abu Hanifa says: 'Hammad was more juristically learned than al-Zuhri, and Ibrahim was better than Salim...' He is evaluating the narrators' fiqh, not just their reliability in transmission.
This is fiqh al-ruwat: the idea that a narrator's depth of fiqh makes his hadith more likely correct.
2
Understand the rationale behind the principle
A narrator with deeper fiqh is more likely to have understood the Prophet's action correctly, especially when reporting a practice that might have been abrogated or subject to interpretation.
This aligns with the Hanafi emphasis on 'reason' within revelation.
3
Contrast with al-Awza'i's implicit criterion
Al-Awza'i appears to prioritize the strength of the sanad (chain) and the status of Companions (suhba). He objects to Abu Hanifa's chain because it includes non-Companions like 'Alqama.
This reflects different schools' weighting of criteria.
4
Evaluate the applicability of fiqh al-ruwat
Ask: does this principle lead to subjective preferences? Later Hanafi scholars systematized it, but it remains a point of contention with other schools.
It is a valid methodological tool, but its use requires careful examination of each narrator's fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وظهر المتعصبون من كلا الفريقين فاشتد الخلاف واحتدم النزاع وأخذ كل فريق ينتصر لطريقته ويدافع عن مذهبه بكل ما أوتيه من حجة، وأسرفوا في الغلو على بعضهم البعض (1) ، وإن كان هذا الغلو لم يتخط دائرة الأدب ولم يدخل دائرة التكفير والتفسيق والاتهام بالابتداع ويمكننا أن ننقل هنا واحدة من المجادلات التي جرت بين علماء المدرستين . لنرى كيف أن اختلاف الطرق وتشعب الأنظار، أوجد الاختلاف في الفروع. اجتمع أبو حنيفة والأوزاعي في دار الخياطين بمكة المكرمة، فقال الأوزاعي لأبي حنيفة : ما لكم لا ترفعون أيديكم عند الركوع وعند الرفع منه، فقال أبو حنيفة : لأجل أنه لم يصح عن رسول الله الله أنه كان يرفع يديه إذا افتتح الصلاة وعند الركوع وعند الرفع، قال : كيف؟ وقد حدثني الزهري سالم عن أبيه عن رسول الله الا الله أنه كان يرفع يديه إذا افتتح الصلاة وعند الركوع وعند الرفع، فقال أبو حنيفة : حدثنا حماد عن إبراهيم عن علقمة والأسود عن ابن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كان لا يرفع يديه إلا عند افتتاح الصلاة، ولا يعود إلى شيء من ذلك، فقال الأوزاعي : أحدثك عن الزهري عن سالم عن أبيه، وتقول حماد عن إبراهيم، فقال : أبو حنيفة : كان حماد أفقه من الزهري، وكان من سالم، وعلقمة ليس بدون ابن عمر، وإن كان لابن عمر صحبة أو له فضل صحبة، فالأسود له فضل كثير .. فالاثنان متفقان على العمل بالحديث، ولكن أبا حنيفة لاحظ أولا فقه الرواة، والمناظرة هنا بريئة لا يقصد بها كل منهما إلا إحقاق الحق
And the fanatics from both groups appeared, so the conflict intensified and the dispute heated up, and each group began to support its way and defend its school with all the argument it possessed, and they went to extremes in exaggeration against each other, though this exaggeration did not exceed the bounds of courtesy and did not enter the realm of declaring disbelief, corruption, or accusation of innovation. And we can here transmit one of the debates that took place between the scholars of the two schools, so that we may see how the difference in methods and the branching of views created difference in branches. Abu Hanifa and al-Awza'i met in the house of the tailors in Mecca. Al-Awza'i said to Abu Hanifa: 'Why do you not raise your hands at bowing and when rising from it?' Abu Hanifa said: 'Because it has not been reliably transmitted from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) that he used to raise his hands when opening the prayer, at bowing, and when rising from it.' [Al-Awza'i] said: 'How is that? Al-Zuhri narrated to me from Salim from his father from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) that he used to raise his hands when opening the prayer, at bowing, and when rising from it.' Abu Hanifa said: 'Hammad narrated to us from Ibrahim from 'Alqama and al-Aswad from Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used not to raise his hands except when opening the prayer, and he would not return to that.' Al-Awza'i said: 'I narrate to you from al-Zuhri from Salim from his father, and you say Hammad from Ibrahim?' Abu Hanifa said: 'Hammad was more juristically learned than al-Zuhri, and Ibrahim was better than Salim, and 'Alqama is not inferior to Ibn 'Umar, and if Ibn 'Umar had companionship [of the Prophet], 'Alqama has great merit... So the two agreed on acting on hadith, but Abu Hanifa first considered the fiqh of the narrators, and the debate here is innocent, each of them intending only to establish the truth.'
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (jadal) between Abu Hanifa and al-Awza'i, illustrating how differences in methodological principles (usul) lead to differing rulings (furu'). The author first notes the emergence of partisanship among followers of the two schools (likely the Kufan and Medinan schools) but emphasizes that civility was maintained. The debate centers on the practice of raising hands during prayer (raf' al-yadayn). Al-Awza'i cites a hadith from al-Zuhri → Salim → Ibn 'Umar that the Prophet used to raise hands at the opening takbir, before bowing, and when rising from bowing. Abu Hanifa responds with a contradictory hadith via Hammad → Ibrahim → 'Alqama and al-Aswad → Ibn Mas'ud that the Prophet only raised hands at the opening takbir. When al-Awza'i challenges the strength of Abu Hanifa's chain, Abu Hanifa replies by evaluating the juristic knowledge (fiqh) of the narrators: Hammad was more learned than al-Zuhri, Ibrahim better than Salim, and 'Alqama equal to Ibn 'Umar in merit, though Ibn 'Umar had the advantage of companionship. Abu Hanifa's argument is that the fiqh (legal understanding) of the narrators is a valid criterion for preferring one hadith over another. The author concludes that both agreed on acting on hadith, but Abu Hanifa prioritized the juristic acumen of the transmitters. The debate is presented as a model of civil disagreement, each seeking truth.

Key Terms
فقه الرواة
الصحبة

Key Takeaway
Fiqh al-ruwat is a methodological principle that prioritizes a narrator's legal understanding, illustrating that hadith evaluation in Hanafi usul goes beyond chain criticism to incorporate substantive fiqh reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given two hadith on the same topic: one narrated by a Companion known for fiqh (Ibn 'Abbas) and another by a Companion less known for fiqh (Anas). Using fiqh al-ruwat, which would Abu Hanifa prefer? Explain your reasoning.
?
Critique the principle: does fiqh al-ruwat risk subjectivity? What safeguards are needed? Write a short paragraph taking either the position of a Hanafi or a critic.

Methodological Grouping of the Four Imams
Author groups the imams epistemologically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize the four major imams into schools based on their methodological reliance on hadith vs. reason.

Core Idea
The four imams are not uniform; Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad prioritize hadith, while Abu Hanifa emphasizes reason.

This passage presents a key distinction in usul al-fiqh: the division between the school of hadith (Malik, Shafi'i, Ahmad) and the school of opinion (Abu Hanifa). Understanding this grouping helps you see how different approaches to deduction shape legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the imams being compared
Locate the four names: Malik, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, and Abu Hanifa. These are the founders of major madhhabs.
2
Note the criterion used
The author groups them by 'methodological proximity' (manhaj al-istinbāṭ). Observe what is being compared: their methods of deducing rulings.
3
See how the author groups them
The text states that Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad form a 'mutually close methodology' (منهجًا متقاربًا). Recognize that they are treated as a group distinct from Abu Hanifa.
4
Understand the basis of grouping
The basis is that the three belong to the 'school of hadith' (مدرسة أهل الحديث). Understand that this school prioritizes textual evidence over human reasoning.
5
Contrast with Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa is distinguished by his methodology, which is implied to be the 'school of opinion' (مدرسة أهل الرأي). This school gives more room for reasoning and analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهكذا يتبين من مقارنة مناهج الأئمة المذكورة أعلاه أن الأئمة الثلاثة مالكًا والشافعي وأحمد يشكلون منهجًا متقاربًا فيما بينهم وإن اختلفوا في بعض مناهج الاستنباط وطرائقه على حين تميز الإمام أبو حنيفة عنهم في منهجه، ذلك أن الثلاثة الأوائل ينتمون في جملتهم إلى مدرسة أهل الحديث أو على الأقل كانوا قد...
Thus it becomes clear from the comparison of the methodologies of the aforementioned imams that the three imams—Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad—constitute a mutually close methodology, even though they differed in some methods of deduction and their ways, whereas Imam Abu Hanifa was distinguished from them in his methodology. That is because the first three belong, on the whole, to the school of the people of hadith, or at least they were...
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage provides a comparative analysis of the methodologies of four major imams: Malik, Shafi'i, Ahmad, and Abu Hanifa. The author concludes that Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad form a closely related group, despite minor differences in their specific methods of deduction. In contrast, Abu Hanifa stands apart due to his different methodological approach. The reason for this grouping is that the first three imams are fundamentally affiliated with the 'school of hadith' (ahl al-ḥadīth), which prioritizes textual evidence over human reason. Although the text is cut off, it implies that Abu Hanifa is more aligned with the 'school of opinion' (ahl al-ra'y). This observation highlights the classic division between hadith-based and rationalist approaches in Islamic jurisprudence.
