Key Disagreements in Usuli Dialectic
Author identifies the main points of disagreement in usul al-fiqh that fueled dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main areas of usuli disagreement that fueled dialectical debate in this period.

Core Idea
Debates in furu‘ are rooted in deeper disagreements over usul.

In this passage, the author describes how dialectic in usul al-fiqh intensified as debates moved from specific rulings (furu‘) to the foundational principles (usul) themselves. Understanding these key points of disagreement is essential for grasping the historical development of Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shift in debate focus
Read the first part of the passage to understand how the nature of dialectic changed. Note that earlier debates were about furu‘ (branches), but now they shifted to usul (principles).
2
Extract the three main areas of disagreement
The passage lists three numbered areas: the Sunnah, qiyas and istihsan, and ijma‘. For each, identify the specific point of contention (e.g., which types of hadith are authoritative).
3
Analyze the disagreements
For each area, consider the opposing views. For example, some scholars rejected mursal hadith and khabar al-wahid as evidence, while others accepted them. Similarly, some denied ijma‘ after the Companion era, while Malik relied on the practice of Madinah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثالث: استفحال الجدل الأصولي في هذا العهد. وفي هذا العهد أصبح الجدل حول المناهج الأصولية المعتمدة في استنباط الأحكام ظاهرا وبارزا، إذ بعد أن كان الجدل يدور حول الفروع، هاهو في العهد يمتد إلى الأصول المعتمدة ذاتها، لأنها هي القواعد التي يستند إليها في استنباط الفروع، فالاختلاف في الفروع والجدل حولها، هو أساسا اختلاف في الأصول وجدل حولها، لأن كل مجادل سيضطر إلى أن يبرز أدلته وقاعدته التي يستند إليها فيما ذهب إليه من رأي، فينتقل الجدل من الآراء إلى الأدلة والقواعد التي لم تكن كلها محل اتفاق بين جميع العلماء، بل كان الكثير منها محل اختلاف بينهم. وقد تركز الاختلاف في القواعد الأصولية في هذه المرحلة على القواعد الآتية أساسا: 1- السنة: حيث ظهر الاختلاف في الاحتجاج ببعض أنواع الحديث، كالأحاديث المرسلة وخبر الواحد. 2- القياس والاستحسان: فقد اشتد النزاع بين أهل الحديث وأهل الرأي في الأخذ بهما، وقد أدت قوة الخصومة بين الطرفين إلى تصدي كل واحد للدفاع عن مبدئه ونقض حجج الآخر. 3- الإجماع: فكان البعض في هذا العهد ينكر وجوده، لأن هذا يتوقف على معرفة شخصية المجتهدين في عصر واحد واعتراف الكافة لهم بذلك وأن ينقل عن كل منهم قول في المسألة التي فيها الفتوى وينقل ذلك القول عنهم جمع يؤمن كذبه أو خطؤه، وهذا في رأيهم لم يتحقق بعد عصر الصحابة، والبعض الآخر خط لنفسه طريقا آخر للإجماع كالإمام مالك رضي الله عنه، إذ جعل عمل أهل المدينة حجة. كما جرى الخلاف أيضا حول بعض أنواع الاستدلال ومذهب الصحابي.
The third demand: The intensification of usuli dialectic in this era. And in this era, the dialectic about the usuli methodologies adopted in deducing rulings became apparent and prominent, for after the dialectic used to revolve around the branches, here it extends in this era to the very principles relied upon, because they are the rules upon which the deduction of branches is based. Thus, disagreement in the branches and debate about them is essentially disagreement in the principles and debate about them, because every debater will be forced to present his evidence and the rule upon which he relies in the opinion he holds. So the debate shifts from opinions to the evidence and rules that were not all agreed upon by all scholars, but rather many of them were points of disagreement among them. And the disagreement in usuli rules in this stage concentrated on the following rules essentially: 1- The Sunnah: where disagreement appeared regarding using certain types of hadith as evidence, such as mursal hadith and khabar al-wahid. 2- Qiyas and Istihsan: the conflict intensified between the people of hadith and the people of opinion regarding adopting them, and the strength of the contention between the two parties led each to defend its principle and refute the proofs of the other. 3- Ijma‘: some in this era denied its existence, because it depends on knowing the identities of the mujtahids in a single era, general recognition of them, and that from each of them a statement on the matter concerning the fatwa is transmitted, and that statement is transmitted from them by a group whose lie or error cannot be believed, and this in their view was not realized after the era of the Companions. Others charted for themselves another path for ijma‘, such as Imam Malik, may Allah be pleased with him, making the practice of the people of Madinah a proof. Also, disagreement occurred regarding some types of inference and the madhhab of the Companion.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage marks the third section of the chapter, titled "The intensification of usuli dialectic in this era." The author observes a transition: earlier debates focused on specific legal branches (furu‘), but now they shift to the fundamental principles (usul) themselves. The reason given is that any debater must rely on his evidence and rules, so the debate naturally moves from particular opinions to the underlying principles, which were not universally agreed upon. The author then lists three main areas of disagreement: (1) the Sunnah, specifically the use of mursal hadith and solitary reports (khabar al-wahid) as evidence; (2) qiyas and istihsan, which was a point of contention between the hadith and opinion schools, leading to mutual refutation; and (3) ijma‘, where some denied its possibility after the Companion era due to practical difficulties, while others like Malik adopted alternative forms such as the practice of Madinah. Additionally, there was disagreement about other types of inference and the view of a Companion. This passage clearly demonstrates the dialectical nature of usul al-fiqh in the period and pinpoints the key issues that generated extensive jadal.

Key Terms
الجدل الأصولي
خبر الواحد
الإجماع

The Escalation of Jadal from Furūʿ to Uṣūl
Author describes the escalation of jadal into the principles of fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the central shift in jadal described by the author and explain why debate over furūʿ necessarily implies debate over uṣūl.

Core Idea
Disagreement on rulings forces disputants to defend their underlying principles.

In the early phases of Islamic legal theory, jadal often focused on specific rulings (furūʿ). However, as the passage explains, this era witnessed a crucial escalation: debate moved to the foundational principles (uṣūl) themselves. This lesson unpacks that shift and its logical necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two levels of debate
Read the passage and note the two levels: furūʿ (specific rulings) and uṣūl (foundational principles).
The author explicitly states that debate previously revolved around furūʿ but now extends to uṣūl.
2
Explain why the shift occurs
Find the author's reason: every debater must present the evidence and rule underlying his opinion, so debate naturally moves from opinions to principles.
3
List the three specific uṣūlī rules mentioned
Identify al-sunnah, al-qiyās/al-istiḥsān, and al-ijmāʿ as the main contested principles.
These are enumerated after the general statement.
4
Note additional areas of disagreement
The passage also mentions disagreement over types of inference and the doctrine of the Companion (madhhab al-ṣaḥābī).
These further illustrate the scope of uṣūlī jadal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثالث : استفحال الجدل الأصولي في هذا العهد
وفي هذا العهد أصبح الجدل حول المناهج الأصولية المعتمدة في استنباط الأحكام ظاهرًا وبارزا ، إذ بعد أن كان الجدل يدور حول الفروع، هاهو في العهد يمتد إلى الأصول المعتمدة ذاتها، لأنها هي القواعد التي يستند إليها في استنباط ،الفروع فالاختلاف في الفروع والجدل حولها، هو أساسا اختلاف في الأصول وجدل حولها، لأن كل مجادل سيضطر إلى أن يبرز أدلته وقاعدته التي يستند إليها فيما ذهب إليه من رأي، فينتقل الجدل من الآراء إلى الأدلة والقواعد التي لم تكن كلها محل اتفاق بين جميع العلماء، بل كان الكثير منها محل اختلاف بينهم .
وقد تركز الاختلاف في القواعد الأصولية في هذه المرحلة على القواعد الآتية أساسا :
-
1 - السنة : حيث ظهر الاختلاف في الاحتجاج ببعض أنواع الحديث، كالأحاديث المرسلة وخبر الواحد . 2 - القياس والاستحسان : فقد اشتد النزاع بين أهل الحديث وأهل الرأي في الأخذ بهما، وقد أدت قوة الخصومة بين الطرفين إلى تصدي كل واحد للدفاع عن مبدئه ونقض حجج الآخر.
3 - الإجماع فكان البعض في هذا العهد ينكر وجوده، لأن هذا يتوقف على معرفة شخصية المجتهدين في عصر واحد واعتراف الكافة لهم بذلك وأن ينقل عن كل منهم قول في المسألة التي فيها الفتوى وينقل ذلك القول عنهم جمع يؤمن كذبه أو خطؤه، وهذا في رأيهم لم يتحقق بعد عصر الصحابة، والبعض الآخر خط لنفسه طريقا آخر للإجماع كالإمام مالك رضي الله عنه، إذ جعل عمل أهل المدينة حجة .
كما جرى الخلاف أيضًا حول بعض أنواع الاستدلال ومذهب الصحابي، وتباينت منها مواقف الفقهاء (1)
Section Three: Escalation of Uṣūlī Dialectical Debate in This Era. In this era, debate over the methodological principles adopted in deriving rulings became apparent and prominent. After debate had revolved around branches (furūʿ), now in this era it extends to the very adopted principles (uṣūl), because they are the rules upon which the derivation of branches is based. Thus, disagreement in branches and debate about them is essentially disagreement in principles and debate about them, because every debater will be forced to present his evidences and the rule he relies on for his opinion, so the debate moves from opinions to evidences and rules that were not all agreed upon by all scholars, but rather many of them were subjects of disagreement among them. The disagreement in the uṣūlī rules in this phase centered essentially on the following rules: 1) The Sunnah: where disagreement arose about adducing some types of ḥadīth, such as the mursal aḥādīth and khabar al-wāḥid. 2) Qiyās (analogy) and Istihsān (juristic preference): the conflict intensified between the People of Ḥadīth and the People of Opinion regarding adopting them, and the strength of antagonism between the two sides led each to defend his principle and refute the arguments of the other. 3) Ijmāʿ (consensus): some in this era denied its existence because it depends on knowing the personalities of the mujtahids of one era, the recognition of all of them, and that the statement of each one in the issue of the fatwa be transmitted, and that statement be transmitted from them by a group whose lying or error is impossible, and this in their view was not realized after the era of the Companions. Others charted another path for consensus, like Imam Mālik (may Allah be pleased with him) who made the practice of the people of Medina a proof. Also disagreement occurred regarding some types of inference and the doctrine of the Companion, and the positions of the jurists varied regarding them (1).
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage describes a pivotal shift in the history of Islamic legal theory: dialectical debate (jadal) escalated from mere disagreement over specific legal rulings (furūʿ) to questioning the foundational principles (uṣūl) themselves. The author explains that this is natural because every debater must eventually invoke underlying evidence and rules. The text then enumerates three major points of contention: (1) The Sunnah—specifically the authenticity and probative value of mursal and solitary hadith; (2) Qiyās and Istihsān—the heated conflict between Ahl al-Ḥadīth and Ahl al-Raʾy, where each side defended its methodology; (3) Ijmāʿ—some denied its possibility after the Companions due to practical difficulties, while others (like Mālik) accepted alternative forms such as the practice of the Medinans. Additional disagreements are mentioned concerning types of inference and the doctrine of the Companions. The passage is structured as a historical and analytical overview, not as a syllogistic argument.

C) From fiqh to kalām

Key Takeaway
In this era, jadal escalated from specific rulings to the very principles of Islamic law, as every disputant must defend the evidential basis of his opinion.

The Three Contested Uṣūl: Sunnah, Qiyās, and Ijmāʿ
Author lists key areas of disagreement in uṣūlī rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the specific points of disagreement over the Sunnah, qiyās/istiḥsān, and ijmāʿ as described in the passage.

Core Idea
The validity of certain hadith types, the role of analogy, and the possibility of consensus were key battlegrounds.

The passage identifies three major areas where uṣūlī disagreement intensified: the probative value of mursal and solitary hadith, the legitimacy of qiyās and istiḥsān, and the definition and feasibility of ijmāʿ. This lesson explores each and their implications for legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Locate the discussion of al-sunnah
Find the part where the author discusses disputed types of hadith. Note the two types mentioned: mursal (مرسل) and khabar al-wāḥid (خبر الواحد).
These are specific categories that sparked debate about their authority in law.
2
Locate the discussion of qiyās and istiḥsān
Identify the two opposing camps: Ahl al-Ḥadīth and Ahl al-Raʾy. Understand that their conflict was about adopting qiyās and istiḥsān as valid methods.
The passage highlights the intensity and mutual refutation.
3
Locate the discussion of ijmāʿ
Read the arguments against ijmāʿ: the practical difficulty of ensuring all mujtahids agree and the impossibility of reliable transmission. Then note the alternative view of Imām Mālik: the practice of Medina as proof.
4
Note additional disagreements
The passage also mentions disagreement over 'some types of inference' and 'the doctrine of the Companion' (madhhab al-ṣaḥābī).
These further illustrate the scope of uṣūlī debate.
5
Reflect on the principle-branch connection
For each of the three areas, consider how a debate over a specific branch ruling would lead back to one of these contested principles.
For example, a ruling on forbidden drinks might implicate qiyās.

المطلب الثالث : استفحال الجدل الأصولي في هذا العهد
وفي هذا العهد أصبح الجدل حول المناهج الأصولية المعتمدة في استنباط الأحكام ظاهرًا وبارزا ، إذ بعد أن كان الجدل يدور حول الفروع، هاهو في العهد يمتد إلى الأصول المعتمدة ذاتها، لأنها هي القواعد التي يستند إليها في استنباط ،الفروع فالاختلاف في الفروع والجدل حولها، هو أساسا اختلاف في الأصول وجدل حولها، لأن كل مجادل سيضطر إلى أن يبرز أدلته وقاعدته التي يستند إليها فيما ذهب إليه من رأي، فينتقل الجدل من الآراء إلى الأدلة والقواعد التي لم تكن كلها محل اتفاق بين جميع العلماء، بل كان الكثير منها محل اختلاف بينهم .
وقد تركز الاختلاف في القواعد الأصولية في هذه المرحلة على القواعد الآتية أساسا :
-
1 - السنة : حيث ظهر الاختلاف في الاحتجاج ببعض أنواع الحديث، كالأحاديث المرسلة وخبر الواحد . 2 - القياس والاستحسان : فقد اشتد النزاع بين أهل الحديث وأهل الرأي في الأخذ بهما، وقد أدت قوة الخصومة بين الطرفين إلى تصدي كل واحد للدفاع عن مبدئه ونقض حجج الآخر.
3 - الإجماع فكان البعض في هذا العهد ينكر وجوده، لأن هذا يتوقف على معرفة شخصية المجتهدين في عصر واحد واعتراف الكافة لهم بذلك وأن ينقل عن كل منهم قول في المسألة التي فيها الفتوى وينقل ذلك القول عنهم جمع يؤمن كذبه أو خطؤه، وهذا في رأيهم لم يتحقق بعد عصر الصحابة، والبعض الآخر خط لنفسه طريقا آخر للإجماع كالإمام مالك رضي الله عنه، إذ جعل عمل أهل المدينة حجة .
كما جرى الخلاف أيضًا حول بعض أنواع الاستدلال ومذهب الصحابي، وتباينت منها مواقف الفقهاء (1)
Section Three: Escalation of Uṣūlī Dialectical Debate in This Era. In this era, debate over the methodological principles adopted in deriving rulings became apparent and prominent. After debate had revolved around branches (furūʿ), now in this era it extends to the very adopted principles (uṣūl), because they are the rules upon which the derivation of branches is based. Thus, disagreement in branches and debate about them is essentially disagreement in principles and debate about them, because every debater will be forced to present his evidences and the rule he relies on for his opinion, so the debate moves from opinions to evidences and rules that were not all agreed upon by all scholars, but rather many of them were subjects of disagreement among them. The disagreement in the uṣūlī rules in this phase centered essentially on the following rules: 1) The Sunnah: where disagreement arose about adducing some types of ḥadīth, such as the mursal aḥādīth and khabar al-wāḥid. 2) Qiyās (analogy) and Istihsān (juristic preference): the conflict intensified between the People of Ḥadīth and the People of Opinion regarding adopting them, and the strength of antagonism between the two sides led each to defend his principle and refute the arguments of the other. 3) Ijmāʿ (consensus): some in this era denied its existence because it depends on knowing the personalities of the mujtahids of one era, the recognition of all of them, and that the statement of each one in the issue of the fatwa be transmitted, and that statement be transmitted from them by a group whose lying or error is impossible, and this in their view was not realized after the era of the Companions. Others charted another path for consensus, like Imam Mālik (may Allah be pleased with him) who made the practice of the people of Medina a proof. Also disagreement occurred regarding some types of inference and the doctrine of the Companion, and the positions of the jurists varied regarding them (1).

Debate of Abu Hanifa with Ja'far al-Sadiq on Qiyas
Author narrates a historical debate between Abu Hanifa and Ja'far al-Sadiq attacking qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical exchange from uṣūl al-fiqh, identify the structure of a jadal argument, and evaluate the force of counterexamples against qiyās.

Core Idea
Ja'far al-Ṣādiq attacks qiyās using historical precedent and counterexamples that reveal the limits of analogical reasoning in divine law.

This passage presents a famous debate between two early scholars on the validity of qiyās (analogical reasoning) in Islamic law. Ja'far al-Ṣādiq, representing a position skeptical of qiyās, challenges Abū Ḥanīfah with a series of objections: first, linking qiyās to Iblīs, then highlighting apparent contradictions in the revealed law that defy analogical expectations. Understanding this debate is essential for grasping the early uṣūlī polemics around the sources of law.

Guided Path
1
Locate the thesis being attacked
Identify where Ja'far al-Ṣādiq characterizes Abū Ḥanīfah's method as 'measuring religion with opinion' (yaqīsu al-dīna bi-ra'yihi). This frames qiyās as a human innovation.
2
Unpack the first counterexample (Iblīs)
Ja'far claims that the first qiyās was made by Iblīs when he compared his creation from fire to Adam's from clay. This is a rhetorical move equating qiyās with arrogance and disobedience.
Consider whether this is a logical argument or a moral condemnation.
3
Analyze the second challenge: the shahāda phrase
Ja'far points out that saying 'Lā ilāha' alone is shirk, but the full 'Lā ilāha illā Allāh' is īmān. Thus a part cannot be judged like the whole, undermining the analogical move from part to whole.
This challenges the principle of compositionality in qiyās.
4
Examine the third and fourth examples
For murder (graver sin) only two witnesses are required, but for fornication (lesser sin) four are required. Similarly, menstruating women make up fasts but not prayers, though prayer is more important. These contradict what qiyās would predict.
These examples show that the revealed law does not always follow the expected correlation between gravity and legal consequences.
5
Evaluate the overall argumentative strategy
Ja'far uses specific textual anomalies to argue that human reasoning cannot fully comprehend divine legislation. He concludes that one should rely on revelation alone ('Allah said').
Consider whether this constitutes a refutation of all qiyās or only a warning against its unqualified use.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونسوق فيما يلي واحدة من المناظرات التي شهدها هذا العصر بين رجال المدرستين وهي من صميم الجدل في أصول الفقه إذ تدور حول حجية العمل بالقياس ومدى صحة الاستدلال به في استنباط الأحكام.
عن ابن شبرمة، قال : دخلت أنا وأبو حنيفة على جعفر بن محمد بن الحنفية، فسلمت عليه، وكنت له صديقا ثم أقبلت على جعفر، وقلت له : أمتع الله بك هذا رجل من أهل العراق وله فقه وعقل.
فقال له :جعفر : لعله الذي يقيس الدين برأيه؟ ثم قال : أهو النعمان؟
فقال أبو حنيفة : نعم، أصلحك الله.
فقال جعفر : اتق الله ولا تقس الدين برأيك فإن أول من قاس إبليس إذ أمره الله بالسجود لآدم ، فقال : قَالَ أَنَا خَيْرٌ مِّنْهُ خَلَقْنَنِي مِن نَّارٍ وَخَلَقْتَهُ مِن طِينٍ
ثم قال لأبي حنيفة: أخبرني عن كلمة أولها شرك وآخرها إيمان؟ قال أبو حنيفة : لا أدري، قال جعفر : هي «لا إله إلا الله»، فلو قال: لا إله، ثم أمسك ، كان كافرًا ، فهذه كلمة أولها شرك وآخرها إيمان . ثم قال له : ويحك، أيهما أعظم عند الله ، قتل النفس التي حرم الله أو الزنا؟ قال : بل قتل النفس، فقال جعفر : إن الله قد قبل في قتل النفس شاهدين ولم يقبل في الزنا إلا أربعة، فكيف يقوم لك قياس؟ . . ثم قال : أيهما أعظم عند الله، الصوم أو الصلاة؟ قال: بل الصلاة ، قال : فما بال المرأة إذا حاضت تقضي الصيام ولا تقضي الصلاة، اتق الله يا عبد الله ولا تقس، فإنا نقف غدا نحن وأنت بين يدي الله فنقول : قال الله
And we present in the following one of the debates that this era witnessed between the men of the two schools, which is of the essence of dialectical debate in uṣūl al-fiqh, as it revolves around the authority of acting by analogy (qiyās) and the validity of inference by it in deriving rulings. From Ibn Shubrumah: He said: I and Abū Ḥanīfah entered upon Jaʿfar ibn Muḥammad ibn al-Ḥanafiyyah. I greeted him, and I was his friend. Then I turned to Jaʿfar and said to him: May Allah make you happy; this is a man from Iraq possessing fiqh and intellect. Jaʿfar said to him: Perhaps he is the one who measures the religion with his opinion? Then he said: Is he al-Nuʿmān? Abū Ḥanīfah said: Yes, may Allah set you right. Jaʿfar said: Fear Allah and do not measure the religion with your opinion, for the first to make an analogy was Iblīs when Allah commanded him to prostrate to Adam, and he said: 'I am better than him; You created me from fire and created him from clay.' Then he said to Abū Ḥanīfah: Inform me about a word whose beginning is shirk and whose end is īmān. Abū Ḥanīfah said: I do not know. Jaʿfar said: It is 'Lā ilāha illā Allāh'; if he said 'Lā ilāha' and then stopped, he would be a disbeliever. So this is a word whose beginning is shirk and its end is īmān. Then he said to him: Woe to you, which is greater before Allah: killing a soul that Allah has forbidden or fornication? He said: Rather killing a soul. Jaʿfar said: Indeed, Allah has accepted two witnesses for killing a soul, but has accepted only four for fornication. How can your analogy stand? Then he said: Which is greater before Allah: fasting or prayer? He said: Rather prayer. He said: Then what is the matter that a woman, when she menstruates, makes up the fasts but does not make up the prayers? Fear Allah, O ʿAbdallāh, and do not make analogies, for tomorrow we and you will stand before Allah and say: Allah said...
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents a classic dialectical exchange (munāẓara) that goes to the heart of the dispute over qiyās. The narrative begins by situating the debate as an example of the intense jadal between the two schools (Ahl al-Ḥadīth and Ahl al-Raʾy). Jaʿfar al-Ṣādiq immediately identifies Abū Ḥanīfah as one who 'measures religion with his opinion', linking qiyās to the reasoning of Iblīs (Satan) — a powerful rhetorical move that equates human analogical reasoning in religious matters with the sin of pride. Then Jaʿfar poses three challenges: (1) The phrase 'Lā ilāha illā Allāh' — if one says only 'Lā ilāha' (the beginning, which is shirk) and stops, he is a disbeliever; yet the full phrase is the foundation of īmān. This shows that parts cannot be judged by the same rule as the whole. (2) Killing a soul is a graver sin than fornication, yet Allah requires only two witnesses for murder but four for fornication. If qiyās were valid, the greater crime would demand more witnesses, but the opposite holds. (3) Prayer is more important than fasting, yet a menstruating woman must make up fasts but not prayers. Again, the revealed law defies the expected analogical correspondence. Jaʿfar concludes that these exceptions prove that human reasoning cannot fully comprehend the divine legislation; therefore, one should not make analogies in religion. The commentary follows the narrative order, explaining each counterexample's logical force against qiyās.

Key Terms
القياس
الجدل
إبليس
شاهدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The case of menstruating woman's fasting

Key Takeaway
The debate shows that early opposition to qiyās was rooted in specific textual anomalies and the fear of human reasoning overriding divine revelation.

?

The Birth of Jadal Usuli with al-Shafi'i's al-Risala
Author describes the origin of jadal usuli and introduces al-Shafi'i's formative role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how applied jadal usuli emerged concurrently with the science of usul al-fiqh and identify al-Shafi'i's formative role.

Core Idea
Jadal usuli was born with usul al-fiqh itself, not added later.

This passage establishes that the practical application of jadal (dialectical debate) in usul al-fiqh began with the very first systematic work in the discipline: al-Shafi'i's al-Risala. The author argues that since usul al-fiqh issues are inherently debatable, the research method naturally incorporated dialectical elements. Before examining examples from al-Risala, the author provides a brief biography of al-Shafi'i to illustrate his unique preparation for this foundational role.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
What is the author's core argument about the origin of applied jadal usuli? Locate the sentence that explicitly states when and with what work it emerged.
The author connects jadal usuli to the beginnings of usul al-fiqh literature.
2
Trace the evidence
According to the passage, what evidence supports the claim that jadal usuli began with al-Risala? Note the reasoning: since usul issues are disputational, the research method must have incorporated dialectics from the start.
3
Understand the significance of al-Shafi'i's formation
How does the author use al-Shafi'i's background (memorization of Quran, study with the eloquent Hudhayl) to explain his role? Identify the specific details that highlight his dialectical competence.
The author calls this 'al-takwin al-jadali' (dialectical formation).
4
Distinguish the two levels
The passage distinguishes between jadal usuli in theory (as a discipline) and in its applied form. How does the author connect them? What does 'applied form' refer to?
Applied form means jadal as practiced in actual usul works, not just as a theoretical discipline.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول : مرحلة النشأة والتأسيس
نشأ الجدل الأصولي مع نشأة الدراسة والبحث في مسائل علم أصول الفقه، إذ ما دامت المسائل الأصولية ذاتها هي مسائل خلافية، فلا شك أن حركة البحث التي انصبت عليها تكون قد استصحبت معها ذلك الخلاف ودرست تلك المسائل وهي تضع في اعتبارها الخلاف الدائر حولها.
فالبحث في الجدل الأصولي - في صورته التطبيقية – إنما نشأ، أول ما نشأ، مع بدايات التأليف في علم أصول الفقه، وتحديدًا مع أول مؤلف في هذا العلم، وهو كتاب (الرسالة) للإمام محمد بن إدريس الشافعي رحمه الله. وقبل أن نتعرض إلى دراسة نماذج من صور الجدل الأصولي في هذا الكتاب، نقدم نبذة مختصرة تبرز لنا طبيعة التكوين الجدلي للإمام الشافعي، الذي أهله لأن يكون مؤسس البحث التطبيقي في الجدل الأصولي.
المطلب الأول : التكوين الجدلي للإمام الشافعي
ولد أبو عبد الله محمد بن إدريس بن العباس الشافعي سنة 150 هـ بغزة أعمال عسقلان وبعد سنتين من ميلاده حملته أمه إلى موطن آبائه مكة، حيث نشأ بها يتيما، وتربى وترعرع بجوار الكعبة المشرفة في حجر أمه، فاستظهر القرآن في صباه وهو ابن سبع سنين . ثم خرج إلى هذيل بالبادية، وكانوا من أفصح العرب، فحفظ كثيرًا من أشعارهم، وقد نقل عن الشافعي قوله : إنني خرجت من مكة فلازمت هذيلا
First Topic: The Stage of Emergence and Foundation. The jadal usuli emerged with the emergence of study and research in the issues of the science of usul al-fiqh. Since the usuli issues themselves are matters of disagreement, there is no doubt that the research movement that focused on them must have accompanied that disagreement and studied those issues while taking into account the disagreement surrounding them. Thus, research in jadal usuli—in its applied form—first emerged with the beginnings of authorship in the science of usul al-fiqh, specifically with the first work in this science, which is the book 'al-Risala' by Imam Muhammad ibn Idris al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him. Before we examine examples of forms of jadal usuli in this book, we present a brief overview that highlights the nature of the dialectical formation of Imam al-Shafi'i, which qualified him to be the founder of applied research in jadal usuli. First Sub-Topic: The Dialectical Formation of Imam al-Shafi'i. Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Idris ibn al-Abbas al-Shafi'i was born in the year 150 AH in Gaza, in the region of 'Asqalan. Two years after his birth, his mother carried him to his ancestors' homeland, Mecca, where he grew up an orphan, raised and nurtured near the Holy Kaaba under his mother's care. He memorized the Qur'an in his childhood at the age of seven. Then he went to Hudhayl in the desert, who were the most eloquent of the Arabs, and he memorized much of their poetry. It is reported from al-Shafi'i that he said: 'I left Mecca and stayed with Hudhayl.'
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is an introductory section from a modern academic work on jadal among usuliyyin. It defines the emergence of applied jadal usuli as coinciding with the beginnings of usul al-fiqh literature, specifically with al-Shafi'i's al-Risala. The author emphasizes that since usul al-fiqh issues are inherently disputational, the research method naturally incorporated dialectical elements. The passage then transitions to a biographical sketch of al-Shafi'i, highlighting his early life, his exposure to Arabic eloquence among the Hudhayl tribe, and his memorization of their poetry. This background prepares the reader to understand al-Shafi'i's dialectical competence. The passage does not present a logical argument but rather sets the historical context for the study of jadal. All examples are from Islamic history and sciences, specifically the life of Imam al-Shafi'i.

Key Terms
الجدل الأصولي
علم أصول الفقه
الرسالة
التكوين الجدلي

D) Al-Musnad by Imam Ahmad

Key Takeaway
Applied jadal usuli was not a later addition; it was present from the inception of usul al-fiqh literature, exemplified by al-Shafi'i's al-Risala.

Al-Shafi'i's Ethical Framework for Jadal
Author describes al-Shafi'i's attitude in debate, his poem, and the impact on his students.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ethical principles of jadal as exemplified by al-Shafi'i and explain how they facilitate the pursuit of truth in Islamic dialectic.

Core Idea
Al-Shafi'i's debate ethics prioritize truth over personal victory, transforming jadal into a collaborative search for truth.

This passage presents Imam al-Shafi'i not only as a master debater but as a paragon of ethical conduct in jadal. His famous statement and poem to his student al-Buwayti distill core virtues that define Islamic disputation: sincerity, humility, and a genuine love for the truth, regardless of who articulates it. Understanding these principles is essential for anyone engaging in scholarly debate within the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the motive behind al-Shafi'i's debating
Read the opening sentences. What does the author say al-Shafi'i was NOT seeking? What was his true aim? This sets the ethical foundation.
The key is that he was a 'seeker of the truth' (talib al-haqq), not a seeker of leadership or fame.
2
Analyze al-Shafi'i's famous quote on truth
Examine the reported statement: 'I do not care whether God makes the truth appear on my tongue or on his tongue.' This encapsulates a radical detachment from ego in debate.
This implies al-Shafi'i saw himself and his opponent as collaborators in manifesting truth, not adversaries.
3
Extract virtues and vices from the poem
Al-Shafi'i wrote a poem advising al-Buwayti. List the commended behaviors (calmness, forbearance, humility) and condemned behaviors (obstinacy, showing off, boasting).
The poem contrasts the ideal debater (sakin, halim) with the obstinate (lajuj) and boastful (mufakhir).
4
Assess the impact on students
Read the account of Muhammad ibn Abd al-Hakam. What did he learn from al-Shafi'i? How does this show the transmission of ethical method?
He credited al-Shafi'i with teaching him how to respond to opponents and the use of qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يكن الشافعي في مناظراته طالب زعامة أو حب ظهور، وإنما كان مناظرا حسن المناظرة، أمينًا فيها طالبًا للحق منها، وقد بلغ من ذلك أن أثرت عنه هذه الكلمة: ما ناظرت أحدًا قط إلا أحببت أن يوفق ويسدد ويعان، وتكون عليه رعاية الله وحفظه، وما ناظرت أحدا إلا ولم أبال بين الله الحق على لساني أو من لسانه. وقد روى الربيع بن سليمان أن الشافعي كتب أبياتا إلى أبي يعقوب البويطي حنا له على الإنصاف والاتصاف بالأخلاق الحميدة في المناظرة، والأبيات هي:
إذا كنت ذا فضل وعلم بما اختلف الأوائل والأواخر
فناظر من تناظر في سكون حليماً لا تلج ولا تكابر
يفيدك ما استفاد بلا امتنان من النكت اللطيفة والنوادر
وإياك اللجوج ومن يرائي بأني قد غلبت ومن يفاخر
فإن الشر في جنبات هذا قمين بالتقاطع والتدابر
وقد بلغ من تمكن الشافعي من الجدل والمناظرة أن تلاميذه تعلموا منه كيف يناظرون غيرهم، وكيف يردون عليهم اعتراضاتهم حتى قال محمد بن عبد الحكم أحد تلاميذه بمصر: لولا الشافعي ما عرفت كيف أرد على أحد، وبه عرفت ما عرفت وهو الذي علمني القياس رحمه الله، فكان صاحب سنة وأثر وفضل وخير، مع لسان فصيح طويل، وعقل صحيح رصين.
Al-Shafi'i was not, in his debates, a seeker of leadership or love of prominence. Rather, he was a debater with good debating skills, trustworthy in it, seeking the truth from it. This went to such an extent that this statement was reported from him: 'I have never debated anyone except that I love that he be granted success, guidance, and support, and that he be under the care and protection of God. And I have never debated anyone except that I did not care whether God makes the truth appear on my tongue or on his tongue.' Al-Rabi' ibn Sulayman reported that al-Shafi'i wrote verses to Abu Ya'qub al-Buwayti advising him on fairness and possessing praiseworthy ethics in debate. The verses are:
If you possess virtue and knowledge of what the early and later ones differed on,
Then debate with your opponent in calmness, forbearing, do not be obstinate nor overbearing.
He will benefit you with what he gains without gratitude, from subtle points and anecdotes.
Beware of the obstinate and those who show off, saying 'I have overcome' and those who boast.
For evil in these aspects is worthy of severance and turning away.
And the extent of al-Shafi'i's mastery of jadal and debate reached such that his students learned from him how to debate others and how to respond to their objections, until Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Hakam, one of his students in Egypt, said: 'Were it not for al-Shafi'i, I would not have known how to respond to anyone. Through him I came to know what I know, and he taught me qiyas, may God have mercy on him. He was a person of sunna, traceable tradition, virtue, and goodness, with a fluent, eloquent tongue and a sound, solid intellect.'
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a biographical description of Imam al-Shafi'i's character and approach to dialectical debate (jadal). The author emphasizes that al-Shafi'i was not motivated by personal ambition or love of fame in his debates. Instead, he was a skilled debater who sought the truth and maintained integrity. The famous quote attributed to him illustrates his sincere desire for truth to prevail, regardless of who speaks it. This is a high ethical standard in jadal. The author then narrates that al-Shafi'i wrote a poem to his student al-Buwayti, advising him on proper conduct in debate: calmness, forbearance, avoiding obstinacy and boastfulness. The poem warns that evil conduct leads to broken relations. The passage concludes by noting the impact of al-Shafi'i's debating skills on his students, particularly Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Hakam, who credited al-Shafi'i with teaching him how to respond to opponents and how to use qiyas (analogical reasoning). This highlights the educational transmission of dialectical method in the Islamic scholarly tradition.

Al-Shafi'i transformed jadal into a truth-seeking collaboration by prioritizing ethical conduct over ego, a model that deeply influenced Islamic dialectic.

Dialectic -- Jadal

Dissecting a Jadal Exchange: Imam al-Shafi'i on the Arabic Qur'an
Al-Shafi'i presents a jadal exchange defending the Arabic-only Qur'an, then modern author analyzes its dialectical structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a jadal exchange (da'wa, i'tirad, jawab) in a text from Imam al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Jadal is a disciplined dialectical method: claim, objection, response, evidence — not mere arguing.

Imam al-Shafi'i's argument for the Arabic-only Qur'an is a classic example of jadal. In this lesson, we will break down his dialectical exchange to see how claims are supported, objections are anticipated, and responses are grounded in revelation. This will train you to recognize and construct sound dialectical arguments in Islamic debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim (da'wa)
Find al-Shafi'i's main assertion. He claims that the Arabs are the source for understanding the Arabic language and the Qur'an, and that others learn from them.
The claim is stated in the first paragraph: '...he is from the people of their language.'
2
Find the first objection and response
Look for the first hypothetical 'in qala qa'il' (if someone says). The objection is that non-Arabs can pronounce Arabic words. Al-Shafi'i responds that they learned from Arabs and are followers.
Objection: 'We find non-Arabs who pronounce something from the language of the Arabs.' Response: 'That includes what I described...'
3
Find the second objection and response
The second objection asks for proof that the Qur'an is purely Arabic. Al-Shafi'i responds by citing multiple Qur'anic verses that describe the Qur'an as Arabic.
Objection: 'What is the proof that God's book is purely in the language of the Arabs?' Response: Qur'anic evidence, e.g., Surat Ibrahim verse 4.
4
Analyze the third objection and response
A third objection arises: Muhammad was sent to all people, so perhaps he was sent in all languages. Al-Shafi'i argues that languages are diverse so one must be followed, and Arabic is the language of the Prophet, so it is the one to follow.
Objection: 'The messengers before Muhammad were sent to their own people...' Response: 'The most deserving of people of merit in language is the one whose language is the language of the Prophet.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيه إلا من اتبعها في تعلمه منها ومن قبله منها فهو من أهل لسانها . وإنما صار غيرهم من غير أهله بتركه، فإذا صار إليه صار أهله . وعلم أكثر اللسان في أكثر العرب أعم من علم أكثر السنن في العلماء .
فإن قال قائل : فقد نجد من العجم من ينطق بالشيء من لسان العرب؟ فذلك يحتمل ما وصفت من تعلمه منهم، فإن لم يكن من تعلمه منهم فلا يوجد ينطق إلا بالقليل منه ومن نطق بقليل منه فهو تبع للعرب فيه . ولا ننكر إذ كان اللفظ قيل - تعلمًا أو نطق به موضوعا - : أن يوافق لسان العجم أو بعضها قليلا من لسان العرب، كما يتفق القليل من ألسنة العجم المتباينة في أكثر كلامها مع تنائي ديارها واختلاف لسانها وبعد الأواصر بينها وبين من وافقت بعض لسانه منها .
فإن قال قائل : ما الحجة في أن كتاب الله محض بلسان العرب، لا يخلطه فيه غيره؟
فالحجة فيه كتاب الله : ﴿وَمَا أَرْسَلْنَا مِن رَّسُولٍ إِلَّا بِلِسَانِ قَوْمِهِ ... (1) 
فإن قال قائل : فإن الرسل قبل محمد كانوا يرسلون إلى قومهم خاصة، وأن محمدا بعث إلى الناس كافة فقد يحتمل أن يكون بعث بلسان قومه خاصة، ويكون على الناس كافة أن يتعلموا لسانه وما أطاقوا منه ويحتمل أن يكون بعث بألسنتهم، فهل من دليل على أنه بعث بلسان قومه خاصة دون ألسنة العجم؟ فإذا كانت الألسنة مختلفة بما لا يفهمه بعضهم عن بعض، فلابد أن يكون بعضهم تبعا لبعض وأن يكون الفضل في اللسان المتبع على التابع . وأولى الناس بالفضل في اللسان من لسانه لسان النبي ، ولا يجوز – والله أعلم - أن يكون أهل لسانه أتباعا لأهل لسان غير السانه في حرف واحد، بل كل لسان لسانه وكل أهل دين قبله فعليهم اتباع دينه تبع
وقد بين الله ذلك في غير آية من كتابه : .
قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّهُ لَتَنزِيلُ رَبِّ الْعَالَمِينَ نَزَلَ بِهِ الرُّوحُ الأَمِينُ على قلبك لتكون من الْمُنذِرِينَ بِلِسَانٍ عربي مبين﴾ (1) , وقال : ﴿ وَكَذَلِكَ أَنزَلْنَاهُ حُكْمًا عَرَبِيًّا ﴾ (2) , وقال : وَكَذَلِكَ أَوْحَيْنَا إِلَيْكَ قُرَّانًا عَرَبِيًّا لِتُنذِرَ أُمَّ الْقُرَى وَمَنْ حَوْلَهَا (3) , وقال : حم وَالْكِتَبِ الْمُبِينِ إِنَّا جَعَلْنَهُ قُرَّانًا عَرَبِيًّا لَعَلَّكُمْ تَعْقِلُونَ (4) , وقال : قُرْآنًا عَرَبِيًّا غَيْرَ ذِي عِوَجٍ لَعَلَّهُمْ يَتَّقُونَ (5) .
قال الشافعي : فأقام حجته بأن كتابه عربي في كل آية ذكرناها، ثم أكد بأن نفى عنه جل ثناؤه - كل لسان غير لسان العرب، في آيتين من كتابه : فقال تبارك وتعالى: ﴿وَلَقَدْ نَعْلَمُ أَنَّهُمْ يَقُولُونَ إِنَّمَا يُعَلِّمُهُ بَشَرُ لِسَانُ الَّذِي يُلْحِدُونَ إِلَيْهِ أَعْجَمِيٌّ وَهَذَا لِسَانُ عَرَبِيٌّ مُّبِينٌ ﴾ (6) . وقال: ﴿وَلَوْ جَعَلْنَهُ قُرَانًا أَعْجَبًا لَقَالُوا لَوْلَا فُصِّلَتْ وَايَتُهُ وَاعْجَمِيٌّ وَعَرَبِيٌّ ﴾ (7) .
فنحن إذا ألقينا نظرة تحليلية على هذا النص أمكننا أن نلاحظ أنه ينطوي على كافة أسس وأركان العملية الجدلية . فهناك أولا تقرير لدعوى علمية من قبل الإمام الشافعي، تتعلق باعتبار العرب المصدر في فهم الخطاب العربي والعلم به، دون غيرهم. ثم هناك اعتراض مفترض على هذه الدعوى باعتبارها غير عامة، إذ أن بعض غير العرب ينطقون باللفظ العربي مثل العرب تماما . وهناك بعد ذلك جواب على هذا الاعتراض، بأن من كان مثل العرب في ذلك، فهو إنما تعلم ذلك منهم، وبالتالي فلا تسقط العمومية في الدعوى. يلي بعد ذلك اعتراض مفترض ثان، ولكن هذه المرة بالتساؤل عن دليل الدعوى والحجة التي تستند إليها والإجابة عن السؤال جاءت بعرض نصوص القرآن.
Except that whoever follows them in learning from them and before them, he is from the people of their language. And others become not from its people by abandoning it, so if he comes to it he becomes its people. And knowledge of most of the language among most Arabs is more general than knowledge of most of the Sunnah among scholars. If someone says: We find non-Arabs who pronounce something from the language of the Arabs? That includes what I described of their learning from them; if he did not learn it from them, he cannot be found pronouncing except a little of it, and whoever pronounces a little of it is a follower of the Arabs in it. And we do not deny, since the utterance was said — by learning or pronounced as a topic — that it may coincide with the language of the non-Arabs or some of them a little from the language of the Arabs, just as the little from the diverse non-Arab languages agree in most of their speech despite the distance of their lands and the difference of their languages and the remoteness of ties between them and the one with whom some of his language agreed. If someone says: What is the proof that God's book is purely in the language of the Arabs, nothing else mixing into it? The proof is God's book: {And We did not send any messenger except in the tongue of his people...} (1) If someone says: The messengers before Muhammad were sent to their own people specifically, but Muhammad was sent to all people. It is possible that he was sent in the tongue of his people specifically, and all people must learn his language and what they can of it. And it is possible that he was sent in their languages. Is there evidence that he was sent in the tongue of his people specifically, not the languages of the non-Arabs? If languages differ such that some do not understand others, then necessarily some are followers of others, and the merit is with the followed language over the follower. And the most deserving of people of merit in language is the one whose language is the language of the Prophet. It is not permissible — and God knows best — for the people of his language to be followers of the people of a language other than his in a single letter. Rather, every language is his language, and every people of a religion before him must follow his religion as followers. And God clarified that in more than one verse of His book. God Almighty said: {And indeed it is a revelation of the Lord of the worlds, brought down by the trustworthy Spirit upon your heart, that you may be among the warners, in a clear Arabic tongue} (1), and He said: {Thus We have sent it down as an Arabic judgment} (2), and He said: {Thus We revealed to you an Arabic Qur'an that you may warn the Mother of Cities and those around it} (3), and He said: {Ha Mim, by the clear Book, indeed We made it an Arabic Qur'an that you may understand} (4), and He said: {An Arabic Qur'an, without any crookedness, that they may fear} (5). Al-Shafi'i said: So he established his proof that His book is Arabic in every verse we mentioned, then he emphasized by negating from Him — exalted and venerable — every language other than the language of the Arabs, in two verses of His book: He said, blessed and exalted: {And We certainly know that they say, 'It is only a human being who teaches him.' The tongue of the one they refer to is foreign (a'jami), and this is a clear Arabic tongue} (6). And He said: {And if We had made it a foreign (a'jami) Qur'an, they would have said, 'Why are its verses not explained in detail? A foreign (a'jami) and an Arabic?'} (7). So when we cast an analytical glance at this text, we can notice that it encompasses all the foundations and pillars of the dialectical process. First, there is a statement of a scientific claim by Imam al-Shafi'i, concerning considering the Arabs the source in understanding the Arabic discourse and knowledge of it, not others. Then there is a hypothetical objection to this claim as not general, since some non-Arabs pronounce the Arabic word exactly like Arabs. Then after that there is a response to this objection, that whoever is like the Arabs in that, he only learned it from them, thus the generality of the claim does not fall. Following that is a second hypothetical objection, but this time by asking about the proof of the claim and the evidence upon which it relies, and the answer to the question came by presenting Quranic texts.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a composite lesson on jadal (dialectical exchange) as exemplified by Imam al-Shafi'i and analyzed by the modern author. It begins with al-Shafi'i's original text, which itself contains a structured jadal argument. The passage first establishes that true knowledge of the Arabic language comes from the Arabs themselves; non-Arabs who speak Arabic do so only by learning from Arabs. Then a hypothetical first objection is raised: non-Arabs can be found pronouncing Arabic words. The response is that such non-Arabs learned from Arabs, so they are followers, not independent sources; furthermore, any genuine Arabic speech by non-Arabs is limited and exceptional. A second objection follows: what proof is there that the Qur'an is purely Arabic with no foreign elements? The proof is from the Qur'an itself, citing verses that describe it as Arabic. A third objection is then anticipated: if earlier messengers were sent only to their own people, Muhammad might have been sent in his people's language (Arabic) alone, but since he was sent to all nations, perhaps he was sent in all languages. The response argues that languages are diverse, so one must be followed, and since Muhammad's language is that of the Prophet, it is the one that must be followed; evidence from multiple Qur'anic verses confirms the Qur'an is Arabic. Finally, al-Shafi'i summarizes his argument and cites two additional verses that refute any claim of foreign influence. The modern author then provides an analytical commentary, identifying the structure: a claim (al-Shafi'i's assertion about Arab primacy), a hypothetical first objection (non-Arabs can be like Arabs), a response (they learned from Arabs), a second hypothetical objection (no proof?), and a response (Qur'anic verses). This lesson demonstrates the complete anatomy of a dialectical exchange: claim, objection, response, further objection, further response, and evidence.

Key Terms
جَدَل (jadal): Dialectical argumentation following structured claim-objection-response pattern, as exemplified in this passage. [Note: In classical logic, jadal often referred to a specific art (one of the five arts); here it is used in the context of usul al-fiqh debates.]
دَعْوَى (da'wa): A claim or thesis put forward by the proponent in a dialectical exchange. [Note: In legal contexts, da'wa can mean lawsuit or claim; here it retains a general sense of an asserted position.]
اعتراض (i'tirad): A hypothetical or actual objection raised against the proponent's claim. [Note: No significant shift; common in jadal and munazara.]
جواب (jawab): The response given by the proponent to refute the objection and defend the claim. [Note: In logic, jawab can also mean answer in a syllogism; here specifically a dialectical response.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the jadal exchange presented, what is the second hypothetical objection that al-Shafi'i addresses?
A) That non-Arabs cannot learn Arabic.
B) That the Qur'an might contain foreign words.
C) That Muhammad was sent to all people and therefore might have been sent in all languages.
D) That the Arabs are not the source of the Arabic language.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Shafi'i demonstrates jadal by anticipating objections and grounding his responses in Qur'anic proofs, showing that dialectics is a disciplined tool for defending Islamic truths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Construct a jadal exchange on the topic 'The Qur'an cannot be translated without loss of meaning.' Write a short dialogue with a claim, two hypothetical objections, and responses supported by relevant Qur'anic verses or sound reasoning.
?
2. Re-read al-Shafi'i's text and identify the third objection and response yourself. Write a sentence summarizing the objection and the core of the response.

Dialectic -- Jadal
beginner

Analyzing a Dialectical Exchange: Al-Shafi'i's Jadal in al-Risala
Analysis of a dialectical exchange from al-Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the claim, objection, and response in a jadal exchange from a classical usul al-fiqh text.

Core Idea
Dialectic is a structured three-step process: claim, objection, answer.

In this lesson, we examine an actual dialectical exchange from al-Shafi'i's al-Risala. The author shows that al-Shafi'i's argument about Arabic being the language of the Qur'an contains all the pillars of jadal. You will learn to recognize each component and understand how the proponent defends his claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim (da'wa)
Locate al-Shafi'i's central thesis: that the Arabs are the unique source for understanding the Arabic discourse (the Qur'an) and that no other group has authority in this matter.
The claim is stated at the beginning of the analysis: 'تقرير لدعوى علمية من قبل الإمام الشافعي، تتعلق باعتبار العرب المصدر في فهم الخطاب العربي والعلم به، دون غيرهم.'
2
Identify the first objection (i'tirad)
Find the hypothetical challenge to the claim: that some non-Arabs can pronounce Arabic exactly like Arabs, which would make the claim not general.
The objection is: 'ثم هناك اعتراض مفترض على هذه الدعوى باعتبارها غير عامة، إذ أن بعض غير العرب ينطقون باللفظ العربي مثل العرب تماما.'
3
Identify the response (jawab)
See how al-Shafi'i answers: those non-Arabs learned Arabic from Arabs, so the generality of the claim remains intact.
The response: 'بأن من كان مثل العرب في ذلك، فهو إنما تعلم ذلك منهم، وبالتالي فلا تسقط العمومية في الدعوى.'
4
Identify the second round of objection and response
Notice a second hypothetical objection: asking for the proof of the claim. The answer is provided by quoting Qur'anic verses that confirm Arabic as the language of revelation.
The second objection: 'اعتراض مفترض ثان، ولكن هذه المرة بالتساؤل عن دليل الدعوى والحجة التي تستند إليها.' The response: 'عرض نصوص من القرآن تثبتها.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشافعي : فأقام حجته بأن كتابه عربي في كل آية ذكرناها، ثم أكد بأن نفى عنه جل ثناؤه - كل لسان غير لسان العرب، في آيتين من كتابه : فقال تبارك وتعالى: وَلَقَدْ نَعْلَمُ أَنَّهُمْ يَقُولُونَ إِنَّمَا يُعَلِّمُهُ بَشَرُ لِسَانُ الَّذِي يُلْحِدُونَ إِلَيْهِ أَعْجَمِيٌّ وَهَذَا لِسَانٌ عَرَبِيٌّ مُبِينٌ) ( 6 ) . وقال: وَلَوْ جَعَلْنَاهُ قُرْآنًا أَعْجَمِيًّا لَقَالُوا لَوْلَا فُصِّلَتْ آيَاتُهُ أَأَعْجَمِيٌّ وَعَرَبِيٌّ) (7) (8). فنحن إذا ألقينا نظرة تحليلية على هذا النص أمكننا أن نلاحظ أنه ينطوي على كافة أسس وأركان العملية الجدلية . فهناك أولا تقرير لدعوى علمية من قبل الإمام الشافعي، تتعلق باعتبار العرب المصدر في فهم الخطاب العربي والعلم به، دون غيرهم. ثم هناك اعتراض مفترض على هذه الدعوى باعتبارها غير عامة، إذ أن بعض غير العرب ينطقون باللفظ العربي مثل العرب تماما . وهناك بعد ذلك جواب على هذا الاعتراض، بأن من كان مثل العرب في ذلك، فهو إنما تعلم ذلك منهم، وبالتالي فلا تسقط العمومية في الدعوى. يلي بعد ذلك اعتراض مفترض ثان، ولكن هذه المرة بالتساؤل عن دليل الدعوى والحجة التي تستند إليها والإجابة عن السؤال جاءت بعرض نصوص من القرآن تثبتها.
Al-Shafi'i said: So he established his argument that His book is Arabic in every verse we mentioned, then he emphasized by His - exalted be His praise - denying every language other than the language of the Arabs, in two verses of His book: He said, Blessed and Exalted: 'And indeed We know that they say, "Only a human being teaches him." The tongue of the one they incline to is foreign, and this is a clear Arabic tongue.' (6) And He said: 'And if We had made it a foreign Quran, they would have said, "Why are its verses not explained in detail? Is it foreign and Arabic?"' (7) (8). So when we cast an analytical look at this text, we can notice that it includes all the foundations and pillars of the dialectical process. There is first a statement of a scientific claim by Imam al-Shafi'i, concerning the consideration of the Arabs as the source in understanding the Arabic discourse and knowledge of it, to the exclusion of others. Then there is a hypothetical objection to this claim considering it not general, since some non-Arabs pronounce the Arabic word exactly like the Arabs. Then there is after that a response to this objection, that whoever is like the Arabs in that, he only learned it from them, and thus the generality in the claim does not fall away. Then follows a second hypothetical objection, but this time by asking about the proof of the claim and the evidence upon which it relies, and the answer to the question came by presenting texts from the Qur'an that prove it.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of a dialectical (jadal) exchange found in al-Shafi'i's al-Risala. The author first presents al-Shafi'i's argument that the Qur'an is Arabic and that God denied any language other than Arabic in two verses. Then the author analyzes this as a complete dialectical process. He identifies four components: (1) a claim: that Arabs are the authority in understanding Arabic discourse; (2) a first objection: some non-Arabs speak Arabic like Arabs, so the claim is not general; (3) a response: those non-Arabs learned Arabic from Arabs, so the generality remains; (4) a second objection: asking for the proof of the claim; and (5) a response: providing Qur'anic texts. This shows how dialectic involves claim, objection, and answer.

Key Terms
جدل
دعوى
اعتراض
جواب

A) The Qur'an is not entirely Arabic.

C) The claim is not supported by any proof.
D) The claim is contradicted by other verses.

Key Takeaway
Dialectic is not mere argument; it is a structured process with a claim, a specific objection, and a direct response, as seen in al-Shafi'i's defense of Arabic as the language of the Qur'an.

Three Manifestations of Dialectic in al-Risala
Author cites Mustafa Abd al-Raziq's analysis of al-Risala's dialectical features.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three manifestations of philosophical thinking in al-Risala according to Mustafa Abd al-Raziq and explain how they reflect dialectical methodology.

Core Idea
Al-Shafi'i's al-Risala integrates logic, definitions, and theological discussion in a dialectical method.

Mustafa Abd al-Raziq, a prominent Egyptian scholar, observed that al-Shafi'i's al-Risala contains a strong dialectical character. In this passage, he lists three manifestations of philosophical thinking in the work. These show how usul al-fiqh can employ logical reasoning while remaining grounded in revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three manifestations
Look for the three 'minha' phrases in Abd al-Raziq's quote. Each introduces a distinct manifestation of philosophical thinking.
The manifestations are: (1) logical definitions and divisions, (2) dialectical dialogue, (3) theological topics.
2
Analyze the first manifestation
Note the logical tendency to first lay down definitions and limits, then proceed to division with examples and citation. al-Shafi'i compares different definitions and chooses the best after scrutiny.
This shows a systematic approach to defining key terms in usul al-fiqh.
3
Analyze the second manifestation
Observe the style of dialectical dialogue saturated with forms and meanings of logic. It involves precise investigation, subtle understanding, and good handling of inference and refutation, while still relying on transmission.
This makes the dialogue appear philosophical despite its religious foundation.
4
Analyze the third manifestation
Identify the allusions to topics that border on theology, such as discussions of knowledge, truth in apparent and hidden, status of the mujtahid, difference between Qur'an and Sunnah, causes of rulings, and ordering of sources by strength.
These topics show the intersection of usul al-fiqh with kalam.
5
Connect to usul al-fiqh
Understand that these manifestations demonstrate how al-Risala integrates logical reasoning and dialectical method with legal theory, making it a foundational work in usul al-fiqh.
This integration is key to the development of Islamic legal methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس هذا النص الذي ذكرناه هو المثال الوحيد للجدل الأصولي في أول مؤلف في هذا العلم، وإنما هو نموذج من نماذج كثيرة نجدها مبثوثة في ثنايا الكتاب (1) هذا، وقد لاحظ الشيخ مصطفى عبد الرازق (2) هذا الطابع الجدلي الذي يغلب على (الرسالة)، فقال يصف منهج الإمام الشافعي في تأليفها : «إذا كنا نلمح في الرسالة نشأة التفكير الفلسفي في الإسلام من ناحية العناية بضبط الفروع والجزئيات بقواعد كلية وإن لم تغفل جانب الفقه . . فإننا نلمح للتفكير الفلسفي في الرسالة مظاهر أخرى: منها ؛ هذا الاتجاه المنطقي إلى وضع الحدود والتعاريف أولا، ثم الأخذ في التقسيم مع التمثيل والاستشهاد لكل قسم وقد يعرض الشافعي لسرد التعريفات المختلفة ليقارن بينها وينتهي به التمحيص إلى تخير ما يرتضيه منها . ومنها ؛ أسلوبه في الحوار الجدلي المشبع بصور المنطق ومعانيه، حتى لتكاد تحسبه - لما فيه من دقة البحث ولطف الفهم وحسن التصرف في الاستدلال والنقض ومراعاة النظام المنطقي - حوارًا فلسفيًا، على رغم اعتماده على النقل أولا وبالذات واتصاله بأمور شرعية خالصة . . ومنها ؛ الإيماء إلى مباحث من علم الأصول تكاد تهجم على الإلهيات أو علم الكلام كالبحث في العلم وأن هناك حقا في الظاهر والباطن وحقا في الظاهر دون الباطن ، وأن المجتهد مصيب أو مخطئ معذور، والفرق بين القرآن والسنة، وعلل الأحكام، وترتيب الأصول بحسب قوتها وضعفها، وقد استدل الشافعي
And this text we mentioned is not the only example of dialectic in usul in the first authored work in this science, but it is a model from many models we find scattered in the folds of the book (1). Moreover, Sheikh Mustafa Abd al-Raziq (2) noted this dialectical character that prevails in al-Risala, and he said describing Imam al-Shafi'i's method in composing it: 'If we perceive in al-Risala the inception of philosophical thinking in Islam from the aspect of attention to subjugating branches and particulars under universal rules, although it does not neglect the side of fiqh... then we perceive for philosophical thinking in al-Risala other manifestations: among them: this logical tendency to first lay down definitions and limits, then proceed to division with examples and citation for each division, and al-Shafi'i may present different definitions to compare between them and end up, after scrutiny, choosing what he approves. Among them: his style in dialectical dialogue saturated with forms and meanings of logic, so that you almost consider it - due to its precision of investigation, subtlety of understanding, good handling in inference and refutation, and observance of logical order - a philosophical dialogue, despite its reliance on transmission first and foremost and its connection to purely religious matters. Among them: allusion to topics from usul that almost invade theology or kalam, such as the discussion of knowledge, that there is truth in the apparent and the hidden, and truth in the apparent without the hidden, and that the mujtahid is correct or mistaken but excused, the difference between the Qur'an and Sunnah, causes of rulings, and the ordering of sources according to their strength and weakness, and al-Shafi'i inferred...'
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is an assessment of the dialectical and logical character of al-Shafi'i's al-Risala. The author first notes that the earlier example is not unique; there are many such pieces in the book. Then he cites Mustafa Abd al-Raziq's observation of the dialectical nature of al-Risala. Abd al-Raziq lists three manifestations of philosophical thinking: (1) the logical tendency to define terms first, then divide and provide examples, comparing definitions; (2) the style of dialectical dialogue rich in logical forms, precise investigation, and proper handling of inference and refutation, making it seem like philosophical dialogue; (3) references to topics in usul that border on theology, such as discussions of knowledge, truth in apparent and hidden, the status of the mujtahid, distinctions between Qur'an and Sunnah, causes of rulings, and ordering of sources. This demonstrates that al-Risala integrates logic and dialectic with legal theory.

Mustafa Abd al-Raziq highlights that al-Shafi'i's al-Risala integrates logical definitions, dialectical dialogue, and theological discussions, showing that usul al-fiqh is a philosophical discipline grounded in revelation.

The Dialectical Impact of al-Shafi'i's Risala on Usul al-Fiqh
Author traces the historical development of jadal in usul al-fiqh after al-Shafi'i's Risala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the two scholarly responses to al-Shafi'i's Risala and identify the key works that emerged from each camp.

Core Idea
Al-Shafi'i's Risala sparked a dialectical divide that shaped early usul al-fiqh.

This passage traces the historical development of jadal in usul al-fiqh after al-Shafi'i's Risala. It shows how the Risala became a catalyst for scholarly engagement, leading to a split between ahl al-hadith (who accepted it) and ahl al-ra'y (who rejected it). The passage lists numerous works from both camps, illustrating the dialectical response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
Look for the explicit division of scholars into two camps mentioned in the passage. What are they called? The first group accepted the Risala and made it a basis for their method; the second rejected much of it and felt compelled to respond.
2
List works from ahl al-hadith
Find the authors and works that belong to the camp that accepted the Risala. For each author, note the titles mentioned, e.g., Ahmad ibn Hanbal's 'al-Nasikh wa al-Mansukh' and 'al-Sunna'.
The passage notes that al-Sunna is more theological than usuli.
3
List works from ahl al-ra'y
Identify the authors and works from the camp that rejected the Risala, especially the Hanafi scholars. Look for names like Isa ibn Aban, al-Maturidi, al-Karkhi, and al-Jassas, and their respective works.
Some works are written to refute al-Shafi'i, others to extract principles from Abu Hanifa.
4
Understand the dialectical dynamic
Recognize that the Risala did not only create followers but also opponents. The resulting debates contributed to the expansion of usul al-fiqh as a discipline. Consider how each camp's works reflect their stance toward the Risala.

The author discusses the historical development of jadal in usul al-fiqh, starting from al-Shafi'i's Risala. The scholars split into two groups: those who accepted it (ahl al-hadith) and those who rejected it (ahl al-ra'y). He then lists works by various scholars: Ahmad ibn Hanbal, Dawud al-Zahiri, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, Abu Bakr al-Barda'i, Isa ibn Aban, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, al-Karkhi, al-Jassas, and Abu al-Abbas ibn Surayj.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview. It opens by stating that the development of jadal in usul al-fiqh began in earnest with al-Shafi'i's Risala. The author notes that al-Shafi'i's dialectical method attracted many scholars, especially those interested in theology, and led to the expansion of usul al-fiqh. However, the Risala caused a split: one group (mostly ahl al-hadith) accepted it and used it as a basis for argumentation; the other group (mostly ahl al-ra'y) rejected much of it and felt compelled to refute it. The author then lists works written after the Risala, attributing to each scholar their contributions. For example, Ahmad ibn Hanbal wrote 'al-Nasikh wa al-Mansukh' and 'al-Sunna', though the latter is noted to be more theological than usuli. Dawud al-Zahiri wrote works on consensus and prohibition of taqlid. Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari wrote a work on the proof of qiyas. The Hanafi scholars, including al-Barda'i, Isa ibn Aban, al-Maturidi, al-Karkhi, and al-Jassas, wrote to refute al-Shafi'i and to extract principles from Abu Hanifa's fatwas. Finally, the Shafi'is, like ibn Surayj, wrote to defend their madhhab. This passage illustrates the scholarly engagement and dialectical debates that shaped early usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
الرسالة
الجدل
أصول الفقه

Key Takeaway
Al-Shafi'i's Risala generated a dialectical split that spurred the development of usul al-fiqh through both acceptance and refutation.

Critiquing the Development of Jadal: The Case of Usul al-Jassas
Author critiques Dr. Alwani's view and evaluates the development of jadal in usul al-fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate Dr. Alwani's claim about early jadal literature and identify the dialectical features in Usul al-Jassas.

Core Idea
Lost works hinder definitive judgment, but available evidence suggests real development in usuli dialectic.

This passage engages with a modern scholar's negative assessment of early jadal works. The author challenges the claim by pointing to the lost manuscript problem and uses Usul al-Jassas as a surviving example to demonstrate dialectical features. You will learn how to critically assess historical claims and recognize dialectical methodology in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify Alwani's claim and the author's response
Dr. Alwani stated that early jadal efforts do not represent real development because they merely revolve around al-Shafi'i's Risala. The author responds by noting the difficulty of such an assertion given that most works are lost or inaccessible.
2
Examine the author's methodology for evaluating lost works
The author uses the titles of lost works as indirect evidence, suggesting that they were inter-madhhab debates on usuli issues, which indicates development. However, he acknowledges the limitation that without the texts themselves, a definitive conclusion is impossible.
3
Analyze the dialectical features of Usul al-Jassas
The author describes how al-Jassas first clarifies the topic, then presents the opinions of jurists (especially Hanafis), then corrects and prefers, and refutes opposing arguments objectively. This methodology is dialectical.
The influence of al-Shafi'i's Risala is noted, but the Hanafi focus on furu' distinguishes it.
4
Assess the author's conclusion about its importance
The author concludes that Usul al-Jassas integrates Hanafi usul in knowledge, methodology, and organization, and is the first comprehensive Hanafi work in its field. This supports the claim of real development despite Alwani's skepticism.

يقول الدكتور طه جابر العلواني : وهذه الجهود كلها لا يمكن اعتبارها تطورًا حقيقيًا في هذا العلم فإن أكثرها يدور حول الرسالة نقضا أو تأييدًا أو شرحًا لا يكاد يخرج عن ذلك»(1) ، والحقيقة أنه من الصعب الجزم بمثل هذا الرأي ما دامت هذه الكتب قد فقدت أو أنها مازالت مخطوطة بعيدة عن التناول إلى اليوم. بل إننا - ومن خلال بعض عناوين هذه المؤلفات - نعتبر هذه الدراسات تمثل تطورًا فعليا في الجدل الأصولي، إذ أن أكثر هذه المؤلفات هي عبارة عن ردود ومناقشات ومجادلات بين أرباب المذاهب في قضايا أصولية بحتة، ولا شك أنها قد ساهمت في حينها في الدفع بالمنهج الجدلي في دراسة أصول الفقه خطوات إلى الأمام. ولكن، مع ذلك فإن فقدان هذه المؤلفات يبقى حاجزًا يمنع دون إصدار رأي قاطع في شان دورها في تطور الجدل الأصولي أو عدمه، فيما عدا كتاب (أصول الجصاص) الذي تم تحقيقه ونشره وهو يسير على طريقة الحنفية، التي تعتمد تقرير المسائل الأصولية انطلاقا من الفروع الفقهية، ومع ذلك، فإن هذا الكتاب لم يخل من أثر المنهج الجدلي، إذ أن مؤلفه قد تأثر نوعًا ما بمنهج الإمام الشافعي في (الرسالة)، ولذلك نجده يقيم منهجه في كل مسألة يدرسها أولا على تقرير الموضوع الذي عنون له إن احتاج إلى شرح وتقرير ، ثم يعرض آراء الفقهاء ومواقفهم في المسألة، ولا يفوته تدوين آراء الأحناف ما دام لهم رأي وموقف في الموضوع ، ثم يحدد موقفه من تلك الأقوال تصحيحا، أو ترجيحا، والاستدلال لذلك.. ويتم البحث عنده بذكر أدلة المخالفين ونقضها بطريقة موضوعية (2). ولعل هذا الطابع الجدلي إنما جاءه من جهة عنايته بنقد الكثير مما ذهب إليه الإمام الشافعي من آراء في كتابه (الرسالة) التي خالف فيها ما ذهب إليه علماء الأحناف. . . وتكمن أهمية هذا الكتاب في أن فيه يتكامل أصول الفقه الحنفي، علما ومنهجًا، وتبويبا . وإن المعلومات المتوافرة حاليًا بين يدي الباحثين لا تترك مجالا للتردد في القول بأنه أول عمل علمي متكامل في مجاله عند الأحناف»(1)
Dr. Taha Jabir al-Alwani says: 'All these efforts cannot be considered a real development in this science, for most of them revolve around the Risala, either refuting, supporting, or explaining it, hardly going beyond that.' (1) The truth is that it is difficult to assert such an opinion as long as these books have been lost or are still in manuscript form, inaccessible until today. Rather, we—through some titles of these works—consider these studies to represent an actual development in usuli dialectic, since most of these works consist of responses, discussions, and debates among the leaders of the schools purely on usuli issues, and there is no doubt that they contributed at their time to advancing the dialectical method in the study of usul al-fiqh. However, the loss of these works remains an obstacle preventing a definitive opinion regarding their role in the development of usuli dialectic or lack thereof, except for the book 'Usul al-Jassas' which has been edited and published, and it follows the Hanafi method, which relies on presenting usuli issues starting from jurisprudential branches. Nevertheless, this book is not devoid of the influence of the dialectical method, for its author was somewhat influenced by the method of Imam al-Shafi'i in 'al-Risala', and therefore we find him establishing his methodology in each issue he studies first by presenting the topic he titled, if it needs explanation and elaboration, then presenting the opinions and positions of the jurists on the issue, and he does not fail to record the opinions of the Hanafis as long as they have an opinion and position on the subject, then he determines his stance on those statements by correcting or preferring, and the reasoning for that... And his research proceeds by mentioning the proofs of the opponents and refuting them in an objective manner (2). Perhaps this dialectical character came to him from his attention to criticizing much of what Imam al-Shafi'i held in his book 'al-Risala', in which he opposed what the Hanafi scholars held. . . And the importance of this book lies in the fact that it integrates Hanafi usul al-fiqh, in terms of knowledge, methodology, and organization. And the information currently available to researchers leaves no room for hesitation in saying that it is the first comprehensive scholarly work in its field among the Hanafis.' (1)
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a critical evaluation of early jadal literature in usul al-fiqh. The author begins by citing Dr. Taha Jabir al-Alwani's claim that early works were not real development. The author then challenges this view, arguing that without access to the lost manuscripts, such a judgment is difficult. Despite this, the author acknowledges that the available titles indicate real development, as they consist of inter-madhhab debates on usuli issues. However, the loss of most works prevents a definitive conclusion, except for 'Usul al-Jassas'. The author then describes the method of al-Jassas, noting its dialectical character due to its refutation of al-Shafi'i's views and its objective presentation of arguments. Finally, the author highlights the importance of the book as a comprehensive Hanafi usul work.

Key Terms
الجدل الأصولي
المنهج الجدلي
أصول الجصاص

Evaluating Priority Claims in Jadal: Al-Ash'ari vs. Al-Qaffal
Author identifies the pioneers in theoretical jadal composition, al-Qaffal and al-Ash'ari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to critically evaluate a claim about the first author of a theoretical jadal work by identifying the evidence and counter-evidence provided in the passage.

Core Idea
The first to write theoretical jadal was not who many thought.

This passage teaches us to be careful with historical claims. Al-Shirazi claimed al-Qaffal was first, but the author shows al-Ash'ari preceded him. We will learn how to check such claims in Islamic intellectual history.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed priority
Al-Shirazi states al-Qaffal was the first to compose in jadal. Highlight that claim in the text.
2
Find the corrective evidence
The author points out al-Ash'ari wrote a book on adab al-jadal and died earlier (324 AH vs 365 AH). Note the dates and titles.
al-Ash'ari is known for kalam, but his jadal work is earlier.
3
Assess the correction's strength
Does the author only offer one counterexample? Is it sufficient? The author says 'what appears from surveying' suggests al-Ash'ari preceded, but does not provide exhaustive proof.
The author's tone is careful; he says 'whoever preceded al-Shashi' but al-Ash'ari is given as an example.
4
Understand the significance
This correction is important because it shifts the origin of theoretical jadal from a Shafi'i jurist to the founder of the Ash'ari school, linking jadal to kalam.

المطلب الخامس : التأليف النظري في الجدل الأصولي خلال هذه المرحلة
قد
إذا كانت هذه المرحلة، وهي التي سميناها بمرحلة النشأة والتأسيس. شهدت تركيز المؤلفات في الجدل الأصولي على ما كان منها في صورته التطبيقية، فإنها مع ذلك قد شهدت ظهور البذور الأولى للتأليف في الجدل الأصولي في صورته النظرية . وقد ذهب الإمام أبو إسحاق الشيرازي (2) إلى أن أول من ألف في الجدل هو الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن علي بن إسماعيل، الفقيه الشافعي المعروف بالقفال الكبير (3) (ت: 365 هـ ) .
لكن الذي يبدو من استقراء إنتاج الأصوليين في هذا الميدان أن هناك من العلماء من سبق الشاشي إلى هذا، وإن كان هو أول الشافعية سبقا إلى التأليف في الجدل.
فقد سبقه مثلا الإمام أبو الحسن الأشعري (4) (ت : 324هـ)، الذي صنف كتابا في أدب الجدل ( 5 ) .
The Fifth Section: Theoretical Composition in Usuli Dialectic during This Stage Although this stage, which we called the stage of emergence and foundation, witnessed a focus on usuli dialectic works in their applied form, it nevertheless saw the first seeds of composition in usuli dialectic in its theoretical form. Imam Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi (2) held that the first to compose in dialectic was Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn Ali ibn Isma'il, the Shafi'i jurist known as al-Qaffal al-Kabir (3) (d. 365 AH). However, what appears from surveying the output of usuli scholars in this field is that there were scholars who preceded al-Shashi to this, even though he was the first of the Shafi'is to precede in composing in dialectic. For example, Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (4) (d. 324 AH) preceded him and authored a book on the etiquette of dialectic (5).
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a historical classification of the first works on theoretical jadal. The author begins by noting the stage of emergence and foundation, where applied works were dominant but theoretical seeds appeared. He cites al-Shirazi's claim that al-Qaffal was first. He then corrects this by pointing out that al-Ash'ari actually preceded him, writing a book on the etiquette of dialectic. Thus al-Ash'ari is the true pioneer among usuli scholars, while al-Qaffal was first among the Shafi'is.
