Critique of Outcome-Based Definitions of Jadal
Al-Juwayni critiques the definition of jadal as realizing truth and refuting falsehood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to criticize an outcome-based definition of jadal by identifying why process matters over outcome.

Core Idea
Jadal is defined by its method, not its result.

In this passage, al-Juwayni examines a common definition of jadal: that it is 'realizing truth and refuting falsehood.' He argues that this definition is flawed because it focuses on the outcome rather than the nature of the exchange. As we will see, even a false disputant engaged in debate is still considered a mujadil. Understanding this critique is essential for grasping the procedural essence of jadal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposed definition
Find the Arabic phrase for the definition: تحقيق الحق وتزهيق الباطل. Note that it is outcome-based: it defines jadal by its result (truth/falsehood).
2
Understand al-Juwayni's objection
He says this expression contains no meaning of disputation (المناظرة) because it could apply to a single person acting alone, whereas jadal inherently requires two opposing parties.
Disputation (munazara) implies an exchange, not a solitary investigation.
3
Analyze the counterexamples
Al-Juwayni gives two cases: (a) a person who does not realize truth but is still called a disputant, and (b) a person who is entirely false in his views yet still called a disputant and munazir. These show the definition is both too broad and too narrow.
4
Derive the lesson
Jadal is defined by the process of opposition, not by epistemic outcome. The term 'jadal' applies regardless of whether truth is achieved or falsehood refuted.
This aligns with the usuli focus on the method of debate (adab al-bahth).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال : حده أنه تحقيق الحق، وتزهيق الباطل. وهذا اعتزاز بعبارة ليس فيها معنى المناظرة لانفراد الواحد بتحقيق الحق وتزهيق الباطل، وقد لا يحق الحق بنظره، ولا يزهق الباطل، ويسمى مجادلا، وكذلك المبطل الذاهب في جميع نظره عن الحق، يسمى مجادلاً ومناظرا، وإن لم يوجد منه تزهيق الباطل وتحقيق الحق .
And among them is one who said: its definition is realizing truth and refuting falsehood. This is a pride in expression that contains no meaning of disputation, because it allows one person alone to realize truth and refute falsehood, and he may not realize the truth by his view nor refute falsehood, yet he is called a disputant; similarly, the false one who goes astray in all his views from the truth is called a disputant and a public debater, even if there is no refutation of falsehood or realization of truth from him.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents another definition of jadal: 'realizing truth and refuting falsehood.' Al-Juwayni critiques it as a mere boastful expression that misses the essence of disputation. He points out that a person may be called a disputant (mujadil) even if he does not actually achieve truth or refute falsehood. Similarly, one who is entirely false in his views is still called a disputant and a munazir. Therefore, jadal cannot be defined by its outcome (truth/falsehood) but rather by its process of opposition.

Key Terms
تحقيق الحق وتزهيق الباطل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Jadal is defined by the act of disputation, not by its success in attaining truth.

Critiquing the Definition of Jadal
Al-Juwayni critiques a cooperative definition of jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in a proposed definition of jadal and apply the same critical method to other dialectical definitions.

Core Idea
Jadal is adversarial, not cooperative – a definition must capture opposition.

A correct definition must capture the essential nature of a thing. Al-Juwayni here examines a definition that fails to do so. By learning his method, you will strengthen your ability to critique definitions in the Islamic disciplines, especially in jadal and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
What is being defined? In this passage, it is 'jadal' (dialectic).
2
Extract the proposed definition
The definition offered is 'shared investigation between two' (nazar mushtarak bayn ithnayn). Note its components: 'shared' and 'investigation'.
3
Test the definition against the nature of jadal
Jadal is fundamentally adversarial: it involves opposition and dispute. Does this definition reflect that? No – it suggests cooperation and agreement.
4
Identify the flaw
The definition implies that the two parties cooperate and agree, and that each individually investigates. This contradicts the adversarial essence of jadal.
5
Formulate a corrected definition
A better definition would highlight opposition, e.g., 'a disputational investigation between two parties aiming to establish the truth'.
This step is extrapolated from al-Juwayni's critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال : هو نظر مشترك بين اثنين . وهذا باطل لأنهما يشتركان على التعاون والتوافق فيه وكل واحد على الانفراد ينظران فيه .
And among them is one who said: it is a shared investigation between two. This is false because they would be sharing in cooperation and agreement, and each one individually investigates in it.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents a third definition: jadal is a 'shared investigation between two persons.' Al-Juwayni rejects this as false because such a definition implies cooperation and agreement, whereas jadal is characterized by opposition and conflict. Moreover, each participant investigates individually, not jointly. Thus this definition fails to capture the adversarial nature of dialectical exchange.

Key Terms
نظر مشترك بين اثنين

Why Jadal is Not Cooperative Truth-Seeking
Al-Juwayni critiques the definition of jadal as seeking a ruling by thought with the opponent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw in the definition of jadal as 'seeking a ruling by thought with the opponent' and explain why it fails to distinguish jadal from collaborative investigation.

Core Idea
Jadal requires opposition, not cooperation—a definition that conflates the two misses the essence of disputation.

Al-Juwayni encounters a fourth definition of jadal: it is 'seeking a ruling by thought with the opponent.' At first glance, this may seem plausible, but he rejects it decisively. The problem is that this definition describes a cooperative search for truth, whereas jadal is fundamentally adversarial. In this lesson, we will dissect his critique and understand why the element of opposition is essential to the concept of jadal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under attack
Locate the phrase 'طلب الحكم بالفكر مع الخصم' in the Arabic. This is the proposed definition: seeking a ruling by thought with the opponent. Note the word 'مع' (with) as it signals cooperation.
2
Understand al-Juwayni's objection
Al-Juwayni says each one with his companion ('كل واحد منهما مع صاحبه') seeks the truth. This is not disputation ('لا بالمناظرة') but rather a cooperative or agreeable manner ('طريق المعاونة والموافقة'). The definition reduces jadal to a joint inquiry.
The key is that 'مع' implies partnership, not opposition.
3
Recognize the essential feature of jadal
Jadal (or munazara) is defined elsewhere as an adversarial process where each party defends their position against the other. The definition here fails to capture that adversarial essence. If both are merely seeking truth together, they are not disputants ('لا يكونان مناظرين').
Al-Juwayni uses the term 'مناظرين' to emphasize the required opposition.
4
Conclude why the definition is inadequate
The definition is too broad: it encompasses collaborative study sessions, teacher-student interactions, or any joint search for truth. Therefore, it cannot serve as a proper definition of jadal, which must include conflict and disagreement.
A valid definition must distinguish jadal from other forms of inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال : هو طلب الحكم بالفكر مع الخصم. وهذا أيضًا لا يصح لأن كل واحد منهما مع صاحبه يطلب الحق، لا بالمناظرة، أو على طريق المعاونة والموافقة، ولا يكونان مناظرين.
And among them is one who said: it is seeking a ruling by thought with the opponent. This also is not correct because each of them with his companion seeks the truth, not through disputation, or in the manner of cooperation and agreement, and they are not disputants.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth definition: jadal is 'seeking a ruling by thought with the opponent.' Al-Juwayni deems it incorrect because it describes a cooperative search for truth, not the adversarial process of disputation. If both parties are seeking truth together cooperatively, they are not engaged in jadal (munazara), which requires opposition and conflict. Therefore, this definition fails to distinguish jadal from collaborative investigation.

Key Terms
طلب الحكم بالفكر مع الخصم (talab al-hukm bi al-fikr ma'a al-khasm)
مناظرة (munazara)
معاونة (mu'aawana)
موافقة (muwafaqa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it requires a ruling
B) Because it describes cooperative truth-seeking, not disputation
C) Because it involves thought
D) Because it is too vague

Key Takeaway
Jadal must involve opposition; a definition that turns it into a cooperative search for truth fails to capture its essential adversarial character.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Al-Juwayni's Definition of Jadal: Means Beyond Speech
Al-Juwayni's own definition of jadal with two observations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three means of jadal in al-Juwayni's definition and explain how it expands dialectic beyond verbal exchange.

Core Idea
Jadal is not limited to words—it includes signs and indications.

After critiquing earlier definitions, Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni presents his own definition of jadal (dialectic). It reframes the discipline not merely as verbal debate, but as any adversarial manifestation of conflicting views—whether by speech, sign, or implication. This lesson unpacks that definition and its two key observations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and core action
Al-Juwayni begins with 'izhar al-mutanazi'ayn muqtada nazaratihima 'ala al-tadafu' wa al-tanafi' — the two disputants manifesting the implication of their views as opposition and contradiction. The action is 'izhar' (manifestation), not necessarily spoken argument.
2
Extract the means of manifestation
He specifies the means: 'bi al-'ibara aw ma yaqum maqamaha min al-ishara wa al-dalala' — by speech, or what stands in its place of sign and indication. This tripartite division (speech, sign, indication) is the expansion.
3
Identify the two observations
The author notes: (1) Like other definitions, it is general and extensive in scope. (2) It uniquely adds two non-verbal means—ishara (sign) and dalala (indication)—beyond mere 'ibara (speech).
4
Reflect on the implication of the expansion
Consider how including non-verbal means changes the practice of jadal. In a scholarly debate, a raised eyebrow or a silent nod could count as a dialectical move, requiring the opponent to respond appropriately.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتعريف الصحيح للجدل في رأي إمام الحرمين الجويني، هو ما سجله عقب نقده لهذه التعريفات التي ساقها، وهو قوله : والصحيح أن يقال : إظهار المتنازعين مقتضى نظرتهما على التدافع ،والتنافي بالعبارة، أو ما يقوم مقامها من الإشارة والدلالة» (2) هذا التعريف يمكن أن نلاحظ عليه ما يلي: - أنه – مثل تعريفات غيره – عام، ممتد المساحة . - أنه يضيف وسيلتين أخراوين للجدل وهما الإشارة والدلالة.
The correct definition of jadal in the view of Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni is what he recorded after his critique of these definitions that he brought, and it is his statement: The correct way is to say: the manifestation by the two disputants of the implication of their views upon opposition and contradiction by speech, or what stands in place of it from sign and indication. This definition, we can observe concerning it: - That it is, like other definitions, general, extensive in scope. - That it adds two other means of jadal, namely sign and indication.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents al-Juwayni's own definition of jadal after critiquing earlier ones. He defines jadal as 'the manifestation by the two disputants of the implication of their views upon opposition and contradiction by speech, or what stands in place of it from sign and indication.' This definition emphasizes that jadal is an adversarial display of conflicting views, not limited to spoken words but including gestures and signs. Two observations follow: first, like other definitions, it is general in scope; second, it introduces two additional means of jadal—sign (ishara) and indication (dalala)—beyond mere verbal expression.

Critique of Four Incorrect Definitions of Jadal
Author refutes four flawed definitions of jadal by counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute flawed definitions of jadal by providing counterexamples.

Core Idea
Jadal is defined by its adversarial process, not by its outcomes or cooperative intent.

What exactly is jadal (dialectic/disputation)? Several scholars have attempted to define it, but their definitions often miss the mark. In this passage, the author critiques four such definitions, using concrete counterexamples to show why each fails. By understanding these refutations, you will sharpen your ability to distinguish a proper definition from a defective one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four definitions
Locate the four definitions of jadal that the author critiques. They appear sequentially after the opening statement 'This is a mistake...'. List them in order: (1) silencing the opponent, (2) realizing truth and falsifying falsehood, (3) shared investigation between two, (4) seeking judgment through thought with an opponent.
The definitions are each introduced by 'ومنهم من قال' (among them are those who say).
2
Extract the author's counterexample for each
For each definition, find the specific scenario the author provides to refute it. For example, for the first definition, he gives the case of a person who is silenced but is still a disputant. For the second, he notes that a single person can realize truth without disputation, and a wrong person can still be called a disputant. For the third, he argues that shared investigation implies cooperation, not opposition. For the fourth, he says seeking judgment with an opponent implies cooperation, not jadal.
The counterexamples show the definitions are either too broad (including non-jadal) or too narrow (excluding actual jadal).
3
Explain why each counterexample refutes the definition
Analyze how each counterexample demonstrates the definition's insufficiency. For the first: silencing is not necessary for jadal because one can be a disputant without silencing anyone. For the second: realizing truth is not necessary (a false claimant is still a disputant) nor sufficient (a solitary truth-seeker is not engaged in disputation). For the third: jadal requires opposition, not cooperation. For the fourth: seeking judgment cooperatively contradicts the adversarial nature of jadal.
The author's method is to test definitions against clear cases of what is and is not jadal.
4
Distinguish between outcome-based and process-based definitions
Notice that the first two definitions focus on outcomes (silencing, truth/falsehood), while the last two focus on process (shared investigation, cooperative seeking). The author rejects both types because they miss the essential feature: jadal is an adversarial exchange where each party defends a position against the other, regardless of outcome or cooperation.
This distinction helps in formulating a correct definition: jadal is a structured disputation between two parties with opposing claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا خطأ، فإن من ينقطع في مكالمة خصمه كان مناظرا، وإن لم يدفع خصمه بحجة أو شبهة، وقد تبتدئ الخصم بحجة أو شبهة فيسكت وينقطع من تريد مناظرته، فلم يكن الدفع له مناظرة ولا المدفوع مناظرا للدافع. ومنهم من قال: حده أنه تحقيق الحق، وتزهيق الباطل. وهذا اعتزاز بعبارة ليس فيها معنى المناظرة لانفراد الواحد بتحقيق الحق وتزهيق الباطل، وقد لا يحق الحق بنظره، ولا يزهق الباطل، ويسمى مجادلا، وكذلك المبطل الذاهب في جميع نظره عن الحق، يسمى مجادلاً ومناظرا، وإن لم يوجد منه تزهيق الباطل وتحقيق الحق. ومنهم من قال: هو نظر مشترك بين اثنين. وهذا باطل لأنهما يشتركان على التعاون والتوافق فيه وكل واحد على الانفراد ينظران فيه. ومنهم من قال: هو طلب الحكم بالفكر مع الخصم. وهذا أيضًا لا يصح لأن كل واحد منهما مع صاحبه يطلب الحق، لا بالمناظرة، أو على طريق المعاونة والموافقة، ولا يكونان مناظرين.
This is a mistake, because one who is silenced in conversation with his opponent is a disputant (munazir), even if he did not refute his opponent with a proof or a sophism. And you may begin with a proof or sophism against an opponent, and he remains silent and is silenced, then the one you intend to dispute with him is not a disputant, nor is the silenced one a disputant for the one who silenced him. And among them are those who say: its definition is that it is the realization of truth and the falsification of falsehood. This is a boast with an expression that contains no meaning of disputation, because a single person can realize truth and falsify falsehood by himself, and he may not realize truth by his reasoning nor falsify falsehood, and he is called a dialectician (mujadil). Similarly, the falsehood-holder who goes astray in all his reasoning from truth is called a dialectician and a disputant, even if there is no falsification of falsehood and realization of truth from him. And among them are those who say: it is a shared investigation between two. This is false, because they share it in cooperation and agreement, and each of them investigates it individually. And among them are those who say: it is the seeking of judgment by thought with the opponent. This also is not valid, because each of them with his companion seeks truth, not through disputation, or by way of cooperation and agreement, and they are not disputants.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage criticizes four definitions of jadal (dialectic/disputation) proposed by various scholars. The author first addresses the definition that jadal is simply silencing one's opponent. He points out that a person who is silenced in a debate is still a disputant (munazir) even if he fails to refute his opponent. Conversely, one might begin with an argument and silence the opponent, but that does not make the silenced person a disputant for the silencer; the action of silencing does not define the relationship. The second definition claims jadal is the realization of truth and falsification of falsehood. The author objects that this definition is boastful and does not capture the essence of disputation, because a single person can independently realize truth or falsify falsehood without any exchange. Moreover, someone might claim to realize truth but actually be wrong, yet still be called a mujadil (dialectician). The third definition states it is a joint investigation between two people. This is rejected because joint investigation implies cooperation, but jadal involves opposition. Each person can investigate individually, contradicting the shared nature. The fourth definition says it is seeking a judgment through thought with an opponent. The author finds this invalid because seeking judgment with an opponent is done cooperatively, not adversarially. The key point is that none of these definitions properly capture the adversarial yet structured interaction that defines jadal. For example, in usul al-fiqh debates about the 'illa of a ruling, the adversarial yet truth-seeking nature is essential.

Key Terms
الجدل
المناظرة
تحقيق الحق
تزهيق الباطل

D) All of the above are invalid.

Key Takeaway
The correct definition of jadal must capture its adversarial nature, not its outcomes or cooperative aspects.

Al-Juwayni's Definition of Jadal and Its Linguistic Roots
Author presents Juwayni's definition and its linguistic justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of al-Juwayni's definition of jadal and explain its linguistic basis from the three Arabic roots.

Core Idea
Jadal is the adversarial display of views, linguistically rooted in 'ihkam' (strengthening).

Al-Juwayni, a leading Shafi'i usuli, offers a definition of jadal that is both precise and linguistically grounded. In this passage, he critiques earlier definitions and presents his own, then justifies it by tracing the word jadal to three Arabic roots: ihkam (strengthening), fatl (twisting), and darb (wrestling). Understanding this definition helps you see how classical scholars viewed dialectical debate as a structured, adversarial but principled activity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
Find what is being defined (jadal) and the essential features in Juwayni's statement: 'the two disputants' manifestation of the implications of their views upon mutual repulsion and opposition through speech, or what takes its place of gesture and indication.'
Note that he includes non-verbal means.
2
Extract the key components
The definition has three key parts: (1) two disputants, (2) manifesting opposing views, (3) using speech or equivalent gestures.
3
Observe the author's critique
The author notes that the definition is general and adds non-verbal means. This shows Juwayni's expansion of typical definitions.
The critique is not a rejection but an observation.
4
Explore the three linguistic roots
Juwayni considers jadal derived from ihkam (strengthening), fatl (twisting a rope), and darb (wrestling). Explain how each connects: each disputant strengthens his own argument, twists the opponent's view, or throws down the opponent's argument like a wrestler.
All return to the core idea of strengthening.
5
Understand the reduction to ihkam
Juwayni concludes that all roots ultimately refer to ihkam, meaning the disputants strengthen and clarify their positions. Munazara is based on this strengthening.
This shows his preference for a unified linguistic foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتعريف الصحيح للجدل في رأي إمام الحرمين الجويني، هو ما سجله عقب نقده لهذه التعريفات التي ساقها، وهو قوله : والصحيح أن يقال : إظهار المتنازعين مقتضى نظرتهما على التدافع والتنافي بالعبارة، أو ما يقوم مقامها من الإشارة والدلالة. هذا التعريف يمكن أن نلاحظ عليه ما يلي: - أنه – مثل تعريفات غيره – عام، ممتد المساحة . - أنه يضيف وسيلتين أخرتين للجدل وهما الإشارة والدلالة. والذي يلاحظ على تعريف إمام الحرمين - كبعض التعريفات السابقة - أنه يميل أكثر إلى الجانب اللغوي ويبرر إمام الحرمين هذا الميل بقوله : فإن قيل : وما وجه تنزيل حد العلماء للجدل على معناه في اللغة؟ قيل : إن قلنا : إنه في اللغة للإحكام فكان كل واحد من الخصمين إذا كان يكشف لصاحبه صحة كلامه بإحكامه وإسقاط كلام صاحبه، سميا متجادلين. وإن قلنا : إنه مأخوذ من الفتل كقولهم : حبل جديل، فيكون ذلك واقعا بين طاقيه الحبل، فقيل : يقع بين الخصمين جدال، لأن كل واحد منهما يفتل صاحبه عما يعتقده إلى ما هو صائر إليه . وإن قلنا : إنه في اللغة مأخوذ من الضرب بالأرض بالمصارعة ، يقال : جدلته فانجدل وتجدل، إذا ضربته على الجدالة، وهي الأرض المطمئنة الصلبة، المحكمة وجهها . . . فيكون كل من الخصمين يروم غلبة صاحبه بإسقاط كلامه بتقوية كلام نفسه عليه كالمتصارعين يروم كل إسقاط صاحبه بغلبته وقوته عليه . والصحيح أن الرجوع في جميعه إلى الإحكام في اللغة، فتكون المناظرة منزلة على معنى الإحكام في تبيين ما يصير إليه كل واحد من الخصمين، ثم يكون النزاع بين الخصمين مرة في الحكم، وأخرى في علة الحكم، والمناظرة بينهما في الأمرين صحيحة . والذي يبدو من كلام الجويني أنه لا يفرق بين مصطلحي الجدل والمناظرة ويسوقهما للدلالة على معنى واحد.
The correct definition of jadal in the opinion of Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni is what he recorded after his critique of the definitions he adduced: 'The correct thing to say is: the two disputants' manifestation of the implications of their views upon mutual repulsion and opposition through speech, or what takes its place of gesture and indication.' This definition can be observed upon it: - It is, like other definitions, general, extended in scope. - It adds two other means for jadal: gesture and indication. And what is observed upon the definition of Imam al-Haramayn - like some previous definitions - is that it leans more towards the linguistic side, and Imam al-Haramayn justifies this inclination by saying: If it is said: What is the basis for placing the scholars' definition of jadal on its linguistic meaning? It is said: If we say that in language it means strengthening (ihkam), then each of the two opponents, when he reveals to his companion the correctness of his speech by strengthening it and invalidating his companion's speech, they are called mutajadilayn (two disputants). And if we say that it is derived from twisting (al-fatl) as in their saying 'habl jadiil' (a twisted rope), then it occurs between the two strands of the rope; thus it is said that between the two opponents there occurs jidal, because each of them twists his companion away from what he believes towards what he is about to hold. And if we say that in language it is derived from striking the ground in wrestling; it is said: jadaltuhu fainjadala wa tajaddala, if you strike him on the jadalah, which is the firm, solid, leveled ground... thus each of the two opponents aims to overcome his companion by invalidating his speech by strengthening his own speech against him, like two wrestlers each aiming to throw his companion by overcoming him with his strength and power. The correct view is that all these return in language to ihkam; thus munazara is established on the meaning of ihkam in clarifying what each of the two opponents arrives at. Then the dispute between the opponents is sometimes over the ruling (hukm) and other times over the cause ('illa) of the ruling, and munazara between them concerning both matters is valid. And it appears from al-Juwayni's speech that he does not distinguish between the terms jadal and munazara and uses them to indicate the same meaning.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents and justifies al-Juwayni's definition of jadal. After critiquing other definitions, al-Juwayni proposes that jadal is the process where two disputants manifest the implications of their views in a mutually repulsive and oppositional manner, using speech or its equivalents like gesture and indication. The author then notes two observations: first, this definition is general and broad; second, it adds non-verbal means. The author then notes that al-Juwayni's definition leans heavily on linguistic analysis. Al-Juwayni himself justifies this by connecting the term jadal to three possible linguistic roots in Arabic: (1) from ihkam (strengthening/perfection), because each disputant strengthens his own argument and weakens his opponent's; (2) from fatl (twisting), like a twisted rope (habl jadiil), where each disputant twists the other away from his position; (3) from darb bil-ard (striking to the ground) in wrestling, where each tries to throw the other by overpowering his argument. Al-Juwayni concludes that all these return to the core meaning of ihkam (strengthening), so munazara is based on strengthening and clarifying each disputant's position. He also notes that the dispute can be over the ruling (hukm) itself or over the cause ('illa) of the ruling. Finally, the author observes that al-Juwayni treats jadal and munazara as synonyms. For example, in a fiqh debate about the prohibition of wine, the disputants might argue over whether the ruling (tahrim) applies to nabidh due to the 'illa of intoxication. This definition emphasizes the adversarial yet structured nature of dialectical exchange.

الإشارة والدلالة
الإحكام

الجدالة

Al-Ghazali's Definition of Jadal
Author presents al-Ghazali's definition of jadal as a dispute aimed at truth and falsehood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in al-Ghazali's definition of jadal and explain how it fulfills the purpose of truth-seeking.

Core Idea
Jadal is a contestation aimed at truth, not mere victory.

Al-Ghazali's definition of jadal is one of the most cited in the Islamic logical tradition. It captures both the adversarial form and the noble purpose. Let us dissect it to see how it balances structure and goal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined. In the passage, the term is 'jadal'.
2
Extract the genus
What broader category does al-Ghazali place jadal in? He calls it a 'munaza'a' (contestation/dispute). This is the genus.
In other contexts, munaza'a can imply a physical fight, but here it is intellectual.
3
Extract the differentia
What distinguishes jadal from other contestations? Two elements: the participants are 'mutafawidayn' (two interlocutors), and the goal is 'tahqiq al-haqq wa ibṭal al-batil' (realizing truth and invalidating falsehood).
The differentia ensures jadal is not aimless wrangling but a purposeful intellectual exchange.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
5 - تعريف أبي حامد الغزالي (ت: 505 هـ) : نقل الطوفي عن الإمام أبي حامد الغزالي ، قوله بأن «الجدل : منازعة بين متفاوضين لتحقيق الحق وإبطال الباطل»
5 - Definition of Abu Hamid al-Ghazali (d. 505 AH): al-Tufi narrated from Imam Abu Hamid al-Ghazali his statement that 'Jadal is a contestation between two interlocutors to realize truth and invalidate falsehood.'
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents a fifth definition of jadal attributed to Imam al-Ghazali. The definition is transmitted by al-Tufi. Al-Ghazali defines jadal as a munaza'a (contestation or dispute) between two interlocutors (mutafawidayn) with the explicit purpose of realizing truth (tahqiq al-haqq) and invalidating falsehood (ibṭal al-batil). This definition is clear and emphasizes the epistemological goal of dialectic: it is not mere argumentation but a structured debate aimed at distinguishing truth from falsehood. It avoids the pitfalls of earlier definitions by explicitly mentioning the adversarial nature (munaza'a) and the goal (truth and falsehood). For example, in a kalam debate about whether the world is created, two mutakallimun would engage in jadal to realize the truth of creation and invalidate the falsehood of eternity. The author notes that this definition underscores the fundamental objective of dialectical exchange.

Key Terms
المنازعة
المتفاوضين
تحقيق الحق
إبطال الباطل

D) Al-mujādilān (the two dialecticians)

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's definition establishes jadal as a truth-seeking contestation, not a mere verbal duel.

Critique of al-Juwayni's Definition of Jadal
Author critiques Juwayni's definition of jadal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate al-Juwayni's definition of jadal by identifying its reliance on linguistic roots and its conflation of jadal and munazara.

Core Idea
Juwayni's definition of jadal is a linguistic anchor, not a full logical tool.

Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni defined jadal by grounding it in Arabic lexicography. In this lesson, we examine why his definition leans on linguistic derivations and how it treats jadal and munazara as synonyms. This critique reveals the need for a more precise technical definition in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify Juwayni's definition
Find the part where the author presents Juwayni's definition and his justification. Note that Juwayni argues jadal is derived from al-ihkam (strengthening).
2
Extract the linguistic roots
List the three linguistic origins Juwayni proposes: al-ihkam, al-fatl (twisting), and al-musara'a (wrestling). Understand how each metaphor applies to disputation.
3
Assess the critique
The author notes that Juwayni leans too much on language and does not distinguish jadal from munazara. Ask: Why might this conflation be problematic for a logical definition?
In later Islamic logic, jadal and munazara are distinguished: jadal is the dialectical art, while munazara is a formal debate procedure.

- أنه يخصص الجدل بين متنازعين، مع أنه يمكن ألا يكون كذلك. والذي يلاحظ على تعريف إمام الحرمين - كبعض التعريفات السابقة - أنه يميل أكثر إلى الجانب اللغوي ويبرر إمام الحرمين هذا الميل بقوله : فإن قيل : وما وجه تنزيل حد العلماء للجدل على معناه في اللغة؟ قيل : إن قلنا : إنه في اللغة ،للإحكام فكان كل واحد من الخصمين إذا كان يكشف لصاحبه صحة كلامه بإحكامه وإسقاط كلام صاحبه، سميا متجادلين. وإن قلنا : إنه مأخوذ من الفتل كقولهم : حبل جديل، فيكون ذلك واقعا بين طاقيه الحبل، فقيل : يقع بين الخصمين جدال، لأن كل واحد منهما يفتل صاحبه عما يعتقده إلى ما هو صائر إليه . وإن قلنا : إنه في اللغة مأخوذ من الضرب بالأرض بالمصارعة ، يقال : جدلته فانجدل وتجدل، إذا ضربته على الجدالة، وهي الأرض المطمئنة الصلبة، المحكمة وجهها وجهها . . . فيكون كل من الخصمين يروم غلبة صاحبه بإسقاط كلامه بتقوية كلام نفسه عليه كالمتصارعين يروم كل إسقاط صاحبه بغلبته وقوته عليه . والصحيح أن الرجوع في جميعه إلى الإحكام في اللغة، فتكون المناظرة منزلة على معنى الإحكام في تبيين ما يصير إليه كل واحد من الخصمين، ثم يكون النزاع بين الخصمين مرة في الحكم، وأخرى في علة الحكم، والمناظرة بينهما في الأمرين صحيحة (1) . والذي يبدو من كلام الجويني أنه لا يفرق بين مصطلحي الجدل والمناظرة ويسوقهما للدلالة على معنى واحد . 5 - تعريف أبي حامد الغزالي (ت: 505 هـ) : نقل الطوفي عن الإمام أبي حامد الغزالي ، قوله بأن «الجدل : منازعة بين متفاوضين لتحقيق الحق وإبطال الباطل (3) , وهذا التعريف – كما هو ملاحظ - يؤكد على الهدف الأساس الذي ينبغي أن يراد من وراء الجدل، ألا وهو تحقيق الحق وإبطال الباطل، بغض النظر عمن يكون على حق ومن يكون على باطل، وكأن الإمام الغزالي يفترض في المتجادلين أن يكون كل منهما على استعداد لتقبل الحق واعتناقه، وليس فيهما من يريد تحقيق قوله وإن كان باطلا وإبطال قول خصمه وإن كان حقا .. وهذه ملاحظة هامة لم نلاحظها في التعريفات السابقة. لكن مع ذلك يبقى تعريف الغزالي كتعريفات غيره عاما شاملا، لا يختص بمجال علمي معين دون غيره من المجالات. 6 - تعريف ابن عقيل الحنبلي (ت: 513 هـ) : قال الإمام ابن عقيل : «الجدل : هو الفتل للخصم عن مذهب إلى مذهب بطريق الحجة، ولا يخلو الفتل للخصم عن مذهبه أن يكون بحجة أو شبهة أو شغب»(2) هذا التعريف يمكن أن نلاحظ عليه هو الآخر، ما يلي: - أنه – هو الآخر - تعريف عام وإن كان أقرب إلى ميدان العلوم الشرعية. - أنه يخصص الجدل - ليس بتردد الكلام بين الخصمين فحسب وإنما بحمل أحدهما للآخر على مذهبه بواسطة الحجة المقنعة والدليل الساطع، وهذه نتيجة الجدل، وليست فعل الجدل ذاته، إضافة إلى أنه لا يفترض في المجادل أن يتخلى حتما عن مذهبه (3) . - أنه يؤكد احتمال حمل الخصم على المذهب بغير دليل أو حجة شرعية، وإنما بواسطة شبهة أو شغب وهذا واقع فعلا في الجدل. وقد أورد ابن عقيل تعريفا آخر في كتابه الواضح قريبا من هذا التعريف، وهو قوله عن الجدل والجدل: نقل الخصم من مذهب إلى مذهب، وقيل: من مذهب إلى غيره بطريق الحجة(4)
That it specifies jadal between two disputants, although it is possible that it is not so. What is noted about the definition of Imam al-Haramayn – like some previous definitions – is that it leans more towards the linguistic aspect, and Imam al-Haramayn justifies this inclination by saying: If it is said: What is the basis for applying the scholars' definition of jadal to its meaning in language? He answers: If we say that in language it [is derived] from al-ihkam (strengthening), then each of the two opponents, when he reveals to his companion the correctness of his speech by strengthening it and undermining his companion's speech, they are called mutajadilayn (disputants). And if we say that it is derived from al-fatl (twisting), as in their saying: habl jadil (twisted rope), then that twisting occurs between the two strands of the rope. So it is said: jadal occurs between two opponents because each one twists his companion away from what he believes to what he is turning toward. And if we say that in language it is derived from striking the ground in wrestling; one says: jadaltuhu fa-injadala wa-tajaddala, if you strike him on al-jadala, which is the firm, compact, smooth ground, then each of the two opponents seeks to overcome his companion by undermining his speech by strengthening his own speech over it, like wrestlers each seeking to bring down his companion through domination and strength. The correct [view] is that reference in all of it is to al-ihkam in language. So munazara is applied to the meaning of al-ihkam in clarifying what each opponent arrives at. Then the dispute between the opponents is sometimes about the ruling (hukm) and other times about the cause ('illa) of the ruling, and munazara between them concerning both matters is valid (1). It appears from al-Juwayni's speech that he does not distinguish between the terms jadal and munazara and uses both to indicate a single meaning. 5 - Definition of Abu Hamid al-Ghazali (d. 505 AH): al-Tufi transmitted from Imam Abu Hamid al-Ghazali his statement that 'Jadal is a disputation (munaza'a) between two negotiators (mutafawidayn) to realize truth (haqq) and refute falsehood (batil) (3).' This definition – as is observed – emphasizes the fundamental aim that should be intended from jadal, namely realizing truth and refuting falsehood, regardless of who is upon truth and who is upon falsehood. It is as if Imam al-Ghazali assumes in the two disputants that each of them is prepared to accept truth and embrace it, and that neither of them desires to establish his own statement even if it is false and to refute his opponent's statement even if it is true. This is an important observation that we did not notice in the previous definitions. However, with all that, al-Ghazali's definition remains, like other definitions, general and comprehensive, not specific to a particular scientific field without others. 6 - Definition of Ibn 'Aqil al-Hanbali (d. 513 AH): Imam Ibn 'Aqil said: 'Jadal is the twisting (fatl) of the opponent from one madhhab to another by way of proof (hujja), and the twisting of the opponent from his madhhab cannot be without proof or doubtful proof (shubha) or clamor (shaghab) (2).' This definition – we can observe regarding it as well the following: - That it too is a general definition, although it is closer to the field of religious sciences (shar'iyya). - That it specifies jadal – not merely as the exchange of speech between opponents, but as one of them carrying the other to his madhhab by means of convincing proof and clear evidence. This is the result of jadal, not the act of jadal itself, in addition to the fact that it does not assume that the disputant necessarily abandons his madhhab (3). - That it asserts the possibility of carrying the opponent to a madhhab without a religious proof or evidence, but only by means of a doubtful proof or clamor, and this actually occurs in jadal. Ibn 'Aqil also gave another definition in his book al-Wadih, close to this definition, where he says about jadal: 'The transfer of the opponent from a madhhab to a madhhab, and it is said: from a madhhab to another by way of proof (4).'
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of several definitions of jadal (dialectics/disputation) as given by key figures in Islamic intellectual history. The author begins by critiquing a definition by Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), noting that it leans heavily on linguistic derivations. Al-Juwayni himself defends this by explaining possible linguistic roots: from al-ihkam (strengthening) referring to each disputant strengthening his own argument and undermining the other's; from al-fatl (twisting) like twisting a rope, where each disputant tries to turn the other from his position; or from wrestling (al-musara'a), where they strike each other to the ground. The author notes that al-Juwayni does not distinguish between jadal and munazara, treating them synonymously. Next, the author presents al-Ghazali's definition, which emphasizes the goal of realizing truth and refuting falsehood. This is praised for assuming good faith in the disputants, but criticized for being too general and not specific to any discipline. Finally, Ibn 'Aqil's definition is examined: jadal as the twisting of the opponent from one madhhab to another by proof, but also possibly by doubt or clamor. The author critiques it for confusing the result of jadal with its act, and for allowing illegitimate means. Another definition from Ibn 'Aqil is noted, which simply calls it a transfer by proof. Throughout, the author is concerned with precision and the distinctiveness of jadal as a science.

Key Terms
الجدل
المناظرة
الإحكام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which linguistic root does Juwayni ultimately prefer for jadal?
A) Al-fatl (twisting)
B) Al-musara'a (wrestling)
C) Al-ihkam (strengthening)
D) Al-jadala (ground)

Key Takeaway
Al-Juwayni's definition of jadal is grounded in linguistic metaphors and conflates jadal with munazara, making it too broad for a precise logical tool.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze a hypothetical debate: Two jurists dispute whether a certain analogy (qiyas) is valid. Identify whether this debate fits Juwayni's linguistic definition of jadal (as strengthening and twisting) and whether it would be classified as jadal or munazara according to later distinctions.

Dialectic -- Jadal
beginner

Al-Ghazali's Definition: Truth-Seeking in Jadal
Author critiques Ghazali's definition of jadal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate al-Ghazali's definition of jadal by identifying its emphasis on truth and its limitation as a general definition.

Core Idea
Ghazali's jadal is truth-seeking, but too generic for any science.

Al-Ghazali defined jadal as a disputation aimed at realizing truth and refuting falsehood. This definition is praised for its ethical orientation but criticized for being too broad. In this lesson, we compare Ghazali's definition to the previous ones and assess its utility for Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate Ghazali's definition
Find the sentence where al-Tufi transmits Ghazali's definition. Note the key phrase: 'to realize truth and refute falsehood.'
2
Identify the author's praise
The author commends Ghazali for emphasizing that jadal should aim at truth, not victory. This implies good faith in the disputants.
3
Identify the critique
The author states that Ghazali's definition remains 'general and comprehensive, not specific to a particular scientific field.' This means it applies to any discipline, but jadal in logic should have a more specific domain.
In Islamic logic, jadal is the fifth art of the Organon, dealing with probable premises and disputation. A specific definition should reflect this.

Commentary

Ibn 'Aqil's Definition: Twisting the Opponent by Proof or Doubt
Author critiques Ibn 'Aqil's definition of jadal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to critique Ibn 'Aqil's definition of jadal by identifying its confusion of act and result and its allowance of illegitimate means.

Core Idea
Ibn 'Aqil defines jadal as twisting the opponent by proof, but confuses the act with its outcome.

Ibn 'Aqil al-Hanbali defined jadal as the twisting of the opponent from one madhhab to another by proof, but also by doubtful proof or clamor. In this lesson, we analyze this definition and its critique: that it mistakes the result of jadal for its action, and that it permits non-proof-based means.

Guided Path
1
Extract Ibn 'Aqil's definition
Find the sentence: 'Jadal is the twisting of the opponent from one madhhab to another by way of proof (hujja).' Note that he also allows doubtful proof (shubha) and clamor (shaghab).
2
Identify the author's first critique
The author says the definition confuses the result of jadal (the opponent's change of position) with the act of jadal itself. Jadal is the process of argumentation, not necessarily the outcome.
This is a logical error: confusing effect with cause.
3
Identify the second critique
The definition acknowledges that jadal may use doubtful proofs or clamor, which are illegitimate means. The author notes this as a realistic but problematic aspect.
In Islamic logic, proper jadal should rely on true or probable premises, not sophistry.
4
Compare with the alternative definition
Ibn 'Aqil's other definition in al-Wadih simply says 'transfer from a madhhab to another by proof.' Compare which is more precise.

- أنه يخصص الجدل بين متنازعين، مع أنه يمكن ألا يكون كذلك. والذي يلاحظ على تعريف إمام الحرمين - كبعض التعريفات السابقة - أنه يميل أكثر إلى الجانب اللغوي ويبرر إمام الحرمين هذا الميل بقوله : فإن قيل : وما وجه تنزيل حد العلماء للجدل على معناه في اللغة؟ قيل : إن قلنا : إنه في اللغة ،للإحكام فكان كل واحد من الخصمين إذا كان يكشف لصاحبه صحة كلامه بإحكامه وإسقاط كلام صاحبه، سميا متجادلين. وإن قلنا : إنه مأخوذ من الفتل كقولهم : حبل جديل، فيكون ذلك واقعا بين طاقيه الحبل، فقيل : يقع بين الخصمين جدال، لأن كل واحد منهما يفتل صاحبه عما يعتقده إلى ما هو صائر إليه . وإن قلنا : إنه في اللغة مأخوذ من الضرب بالأرض بالمصارعة ، يقال : جدلته فانجدل وتجدل، إذا ضربته على الجدالة، وهي الأرض المطمئنة الصلبة، المحكمة وجهها وجهها . . . فيكون كل من الخصمين يروم غلبة صاحبه بإسقاط كلامه بتقوية كلام نفسه عليه كالمتصارعين يروم كل إسقاط صاحبه بغلبته وقوته عليه . والصحيح أن الرجوع في جميعه إلى الإحكام في اللغة، فتكون المناظرة منزلة على معنى الإحكام في تبيين ما يصير إليه كل واحد من الخصمين، ثم يكون النزاع بين الخصمين مرة في الحكم، وأخرى في علة الحكم، والمناظرة بينهما في الأمرين صحيحة (1) . والذي يبدو من كلام الجويني أنه لا يفرق بين مصطلحي الجدل والمناظرة ويسوقهما للدلالة على معنى واحد . 5 - تعريف أبي حامد الغزالي (ت: 505 هـ) : نقل الطوفي عن الإمام أبي حامد الغزالي ، قوله بأن «الجدل : منازعة بين متفاوضين لتحقيق الحق وإبطال الباطل (3) , وهذا التعريف – كما هو ملاحظ - يؤكد على الهدف الأساس الذي ينبغي أن يراد من وراء الجدل، ألا وهو تحقيق الحق وإبطال الباطل، بغض النظر عمن يكون على حق ومن يكون على باطل، وكأن الإمام الغزالي يفترض في المتجادلين أن يكون كل منهما على استعداد لتقبل الحق واعتناقه، وليس فيهما من يريد تحقيق قوله وإن كان باطلا وإبطال قول خصمه وإن كان حقا .. وهذه ملاحظة هامة لم نلاحظها في التعريفات السابقة. لكن مع ذلك يبقى تعريف الغزالي كتعريفات غيره عاما شاملا، لا يختص بمجال علمي معين دون غيره من المجالات. 6 - تعريف ابن عقيل الحنبلي (ت: 513 هـ) : قال الإمام ابن عقيل : «الجدل : هو الفتل للخصم عن مذهب إلى مذهب بطريق الحجة، ولا يخلو الفتل للخصم عن مذهبه أن يكون بحجة أو شبهة أو شغب»(2) هذا التعريف يمكن أن نلاحظ عليه هو الآخر، ما يلي: - أنه – هو الآخر - تعريف عام وإن كان أقرب إلى ميدان العلوم الشرعية. - أنه يخصص الجدل - ليس بتردد الكلام بين الخصمين فحسب وإنما بحمل أحدهما للآخر على مذهبه بواسطة الحجة المقنعة والدليل الساطع، وهذه نتيجة الجدل، وليست فعل الجدل ذاته، إضافة إلى أنه لا يفترض في المجادل أن يتخلى حتما عن مذهبه (3) . - أنه يؤكد احتمال حمل الخصم على المذهب بغير دليل أو حجة شرعية، وإنما بواسطة شبهة أو شغب وهذا واقع فعلا في الجدل. وقد أورد ابن عقيل تعريفا آخر في كتابه الواضح قريبا من هذا التعريف، وهو قوله عن الجدل والجدل: نقل الخصم من مذهب إلى مذهب، وقيل: من مذهب إلى غيره بطريق الحجة(4)
That it specifies jadal between two disputants, although it is possible that it is not so. What is noted about the definition of Imam al-Haramayn – like some previous definitions – is that it leans more towards the linguistic aspect, and Imam al-Haramayn justifies this inclination by saying: If it is said: What is the basis for applying the scholars' definition of jadal to its meaning in language? He answers: If we say that in language it [is derived] from al-ihkam (strengthening), then each of the two opponents, when he reveals to his companion the correctness of his speech by strengthening it and undermining his companion's speech, they are called mutajadilayn (disputants). And if we say that it is derived from al-fatl (twisting), as in their saying: habl jadil (twisted rope), then that twisting occurs between the two strands of the rope. So it is said: jadal occurs between two opponents because each one twists his companion away from what he believes to what he is turning toward. And if we say that in language it is derived from striking the ground in wrestling; one says: jadaltuhu fa-injadala wa-tajaddala, if you strike him on al-jadala, which is the firm, compact, smooth ground, then each of the two opponents seeks to overcome his companion by undermining his speech by strengthening his own speech over it, like wrestlers each seeking to bring down his companion through domination and strength. The correct [view] is that reference in all of it is to al-ihkam in language. So munazara is applied to the meaning of al-ihkam in clarifying what each opponent arrives at. Then the dispute between the opponents is sometimes about the ruling (hukm) and other times about the cause ('illa) of the ruling, and munazara between them concerning both matters is valid (1). It appears from al-Juwayni's speech that he does not distinguish between the terms jadal and munazara and uses both to indicate a single meaning. 5 - Definition of Abu Hamid al-Ghazali (d. 505 AH): al-Tufi transmitted from Imam Abu Hamid al-Ghazali his statement that 'Jadal is a disputation (munaza'a) between two negotiators (mutafawidayn) to realize truth (haqq) and refute falsehood (batil) (3).' This definition – as is observed – emphasizes the fundamental aim that should be intended from jadal, namely realizing truth and refuting falsehood, regardless of who is upon truth and who is upon falsehood. It is as if Imam al-Ghazali assumes in the two disputants that each of them is prepared to accept truth and embrace it, and that neither of them desires to establish his own statement even if it is false and to refute his opponent's statement even if it is true. This is an important observation that we did not notice in the previous definitions. However, with all that, al-Ghazali's definition remains, like other definitions, general and comprehensive, not specific to a particular scientific field without others. 6 - Definition of Ibn 'Aqil al-Hanbali (d. 513 AH): Imam Ibn 'Aqil said: 'Jadal is the twisting (fatl) of the opponent from one madhhab to another by way of proof (hujja), and the twisting of the opponent from his madhhab cannot be without proof or doubtful proof (shubha) or clamor (shaghab) (2).' This definition – we can observe regarding it as well the following: - That it too is a general definition, although it is closer to the field of religious sciences (shar'iyya). - That it specifies jadal – not merely as the exchange of speech between opponents, but as one of them carrying the other to his madhhab by means of convincing proof and clear evidence. This is the result of jadal, not the act of jadal itself, in addition to the fact that it does not assume that the disputant necessarily abandons his madhhab (3). - That it asserts the possibility of carrying the opponent to a madhhab without a religious proof or evidence, but only by means of a doubtful proof or clamor, and this actually occurs in jadal. Ibn 'Aqil also gave another definition in his book al-Wadih, close to this definition, where he says about jadal: 'The transfer of the opponent from a madhhab to a madhhab, and it is said: from a madhhab to another by way of proof (4).'
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of several definitions of jadal (dialectics/disputation) as given by key figures in Islamic intellectual history. The author begins by critiquing a definition by Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), noting that it leans heavily on linguistic derivations. Al-Juwayni himself defends this by explaining possible linguistic roots: from al-ihkam (strengthening) referring to each disputant strengthening his own argument and undermining the other's; from al-fatl (twisting) like twisting a rope, where each disputant tries to turn the other from his position; or from wrestling (al-musara'a), where they strike each other to the ground. The author notes that al-Juwayni does not distinguish between jadal and munazara, treating them synonymously. Next, the author presents al-Ghazali's definition, which emphasizes the goal of realizing truth and refuting falsehood. This is praised for assuming good faith in the disputants, but criticized for being too general and not specific to any discipline. Finally, Ibn 'Aqil's definition is examined: jadal as the twisting of the opponent from one madhhab to another by proof, but also possibly by doubt or clamor. The author critiques it for confusing the result of jadal with its act, and for allowing illegitimate means. Another definition from Ibn 'Aqil is noted, which simply calls it a transfer by proof. Throughout, the author is concerned with precision and the distinctiveness of jadal as a science.

الفتل
الحجة
الشبهة
الشغب

What is the main logical error in Ibn 'Aqil's definition according to the author?
A) It is too specific to religious sciences.
B) It confuses the result of jadal with the act itself.
C) It ignores the role of proof.
D) It assumes the opponent always abandons his madhhab.

Key Takeaway
Ibn 'Aqil's definition mistakenly describes jadal by its outcome (changing the opponent's view) and allows dubious means, making it imprecise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imagine a debate in usul al-fiqh where one party uses a fallacious argument (shubha) to persuade the other. According to Ibn 'Aqil's definition, is this jadal? According to the author's critique, should this be considered jadal? Explain your reasoning.

Dialectic -- Jadal
beginner
5 min
Ibn 'Aqil's Definition of Jadal
Ibn 'Aqil's definition of jadal with its threefold means

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the three means of twisting the opponent in Ibn 'Aqil's definition of jadal and distinguish between them.

Core Idea
Jadal is not limited to valid proofs; it includes specious argument and sophistry.

Ibn 'Aqil defines jadal as an effort to twist the opponent from his madhhab using proof, but he immediately clarifies that this twisting can also occur through specious arguments or pure sophistry. This broader definition captures the reality that many debates are not conducted with sound evidence alone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core action
Find the key verb that Ibn 'Aqil uses to describe jadal. He says 'al-fatl' (twisting/turning). What is being twisted? The opponent from his madhhab.
Fat l is a physical metaphor for persuasive effort.
2
Identify the goal
What is the intended outcome of the fatl? It is to move the opponent from one madhhab to another. This is the target of jadal.
The goal is a change of position, not necessarily conversion.
3
Identify the three means
Ibn 'Aqil states that the fatl is not free from being by hujjah (valid proof), shubhah (specious argument), or shaghb (sophistry). List these three.
These are mutually exclusive categories covering all possibilities.
4
Distinguish between the three means
How does each differ? Hujjah is a valid shar'i proof; shubhah resembles proof but is fallacious; shaghb is contentious without pretense of proof.
The distinction is based on validity and intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام ابن عقيل : «الجدل : هو الفتل للخصم عن مذهب إلى مذهب بطريق الحجة، ولا يخلو الفتل للخصم عن مذهبه أن يكون بحجة أو شبهة أو شغب».

هذا التعريف يمكن أن نلاحظ عليه هو الآخر، ما يلي:

- أنه – هو الآخر - تعريف عام وإن كان أقرب إلى ميدان العلوم الشرعية.

- أنه يخصص الجدل - ليس بتردد الكلام بين الخصمين فحسب وإنما بحمل أحدهما للآخر على مذهبه بواسطة الحجة المقنعة والدليل الساطع، وهذه نتيجة الجدل، وليست فعل الجدل ذاته، إضافة إلى أنه لا يفترض في المجادل أن يتخلى حتما عن مذهبه.

- أنه يؤكد احتمال حمل الخصم على المذهب بغير دليل أو حجة شرعية، وإنما بواسطة شبهة أو شغب وهذا واقع فعلا في الجدل.

وقد أورد ابن عقيل تعريفا آخر في كتابه الواضح قريبا من هذا التعريف، وهو قوله عن الجدل والجدل: نقل الخصم من مذهب إلى مذهب، وقيل: من مذهب إلى غيره بطريق الحجة.

وابن عقيل في هذا الكلام كأنه فصل بين تعريفين : أحدهما له ومفاده محاولة حمل الخصم من مذهبه الذي يعتنقه إلى مذهب المجادل، بغض النظر عن الطريقة التي يحمل بها على ذلك. والثاني لغيره، ومفاده حمل الخصم على اعتناق المذهب بطريق الحجة، وابن عقيل في هذا كأنه يناقض ما سبق أن صدر منه في التعريف السابق الذي أكد فيه على ضرورة أن يكون الحمل للخصم على المذهب بطريق الحجة والدليل، والله تعالى أعلم .
Imam Ibn 'Aqil said: 'Jadal is the twisting of the opponent from a madhhab to a madhhab by way of proof, and the twisting of the opponent from his madhhab is not free from being by a proof, a specious argument, or sophistry.'

This definition can be observed, as well, in the following: that it is a general definition, although it is closer to the field of the shar'i sciences. That it specifies jadal not merely by the repetition of speech between the two opponents, but by causing one of them to adopt the other's madhhab by means of a convincing proof and a brilliant evidence, and this is the result of jadal, not the act of jadal itself, in addition to that it does not assume that the disputant will inevitably abandon his madhhab. That it confirms the possibility of causing the opponent to adopt the madhhab by other than a shar'i proof or evidence, but rather by means of a specious argument or sophistry, and this actually occurs in jadal.

And Ibn 'Aqil mentioned another definition in his book al-Wadih near to this definition, which is his statement about jadal and jadal: the transfer of the opponent from a madhhab to a madhhab, and it is said: from a madhhab to another by way of proof.

And Ibn 'Aqil, in this speech, appears to distinguish between two definitions: one of them is his, and its meaning is an attempt to move the opponent from his madhhab which he holds to the madhhab of the disputant, regardless of the method by which he is moved to that. And the second is from someone else, and its meaning is to move the opponent to adopt the madhhab by way of proof. And Ibn 'Aqil in this appears to contradict what was previously issued from him in the previous definition in which he emphasized the necessity that the moving of the opponent to the madhhab be by way of proof and evidence. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of jadal (dialectical argument) by Imam Ibn 'Aqil al-Hanbali and then provides a critical analysis of that definition. The author first quotes Ibn 'Aqil's definition: jadal is the twisting of the opponent from one school of thought to another using a proof, and that the process of twisting is not free from being done by a valid proof, a specious argument, or outright sophistry. This definition is notable for including fallacious means (shubhah and shaghab) alongside valid proofs. The author then lists three observations on this definition: firstly, it is a general definition though it leans towards the field of Islamic legal theory; secondly, it frames jadal not as mere back-and-forth speech but as an active attempt to convert the opponent, though that conversion is the result, not the act itself—it also clarifies that the disputant is not assumed to fully abandon his own position; thirdly, it acknowledges that the opponent may be moved by non-shar'i means such as specious arguments, which is a realistic description of actual jadal. After this, the author mentions another definition by Ibn 'Aqil from his book al-Wadih, where jadal is defined simply as transferring the opponent from one madhhab to another by way of proof, with some variant saying 'from a madhhab to another.' The author then notes that Ibn 'Aqil appears to have distinguished between two definitions: one of his own, which allows any method of transfer, and another that seems to belong to others, which emphasizes proof. This reveals an apparent contradiction in Ibn 'Aqil's own work, as the first definition required proof but the second includes non-proof methods. The passage is an expansion-probe, clarifying the nuances in defining the art of dialectic.

Key Terms
الجدل
الفتل
الحجة
الشبهة
الشغب

Checkpoint
According to Ibn 'Aqil's definition, which of the following can be used in jadal to twist the opponent from his madhhab?
A) Only a valid shar'i proof (hujjah)
B) Only a valid proof or a specious argument (shubhah), but not sophistry (shaghb)
C) A valid proof, a specious argument, or sophistry
D) Only a convincing speech without any logical structure

Key Takeaway
Ibn 'Aqil's definition of jadal includes valid proof, specious argument, and sophistry, reflecting the reality that debate is not always conducted with sound evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify in a real jadal example which of the three means (hujjah, shubhah, shaghb) is being used. For instance, in the debate over qiyas in usul al-fiqh, the Mu'tazilites used a rational argument ('aql) to support qiyas—is that a hujjah or shubhah? Justify your answer.
?
In Ibn 'Aqil's definition, what is the role of 'al-madhhab'? Why does he specify a change of madhhab?

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate

Contradiction in Ibn 'Aqil's Definitions of Jadal
Analysis of the two definitions of jadal and the apparent contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the two definitions of jadal attributed to Ibn 'Aqil and identify the apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
Ibn 'Aqil appears to contradict himself when he also defines jadal as transfer by proof only.

After presenting his first definition, Ibn 'Aqil mentions another definition from his book al-Wadih, where jadal is simply transferring the opponent from one madhhab to another by proof. The author of our passage raises a quandary: Ibn 'Aqil's own definition includes non-proof means, but the second definition restricts to proof. Let's analyze this tension.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Locate both definitions in the text. The first is 'al-fatl ... bi-hujjah aw shubhah aw shaghb'. The second is 'naql al-khasm min madhhab ila madhhab bi-tariq al-hujjah'.
The second definition is from al-Wadih and omits shubhah and shaghb.
2
Compare the means
What is common? Both involve moving from one madhhab to another. What differs? The first allows three means; the second allows only hujjah.
This is the core difference.
3
Consider the author's observation
The author suggests Ibn 'Aqil distinguishes between his own view (any method) and the view of others (proof only). Does this resolve the contradiction?
The author is not entirely sure, noting 'kannahu fasl bayna ta'rifini'.
4
Evaluate the contradiction
Is it a logical contradiction, or could the two definitions serve different purposes? One is descriptive (actual practice) and the other prescriptive (ideal jadal).
This helps reconcile the apparent inconsistency.

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of jadal (dialectical argument) by Imam Ibn 'Aqil al-Hanbali and then provides a critical analysis of that definition. The author first quotes Ibn 'Aqil's definition: jadal is the twisting of the opponent from one school of thought to another using a proof, and that the process of twisting is not free from being done by a valid proof, a specious argument, or outright sophistry. The author then lists three observations on this definition: firstly, it is a general definition though it leans towards the field of Islamic legal theory; secondly, it frames jadal not as mere back-and-forth speech but as an active attempt to convert the opponent, though that conversion is the result, not the act itself—it also clarifies that the disputant is not assumed to fully abandon his own position; thirdly, it acknowledges that the opponent may be moved by non-shar'i means such as specious arguments, which is a realistic description of actual jadal. After this, the author mentions another definition by Ibn 'Aqil from his book al-Wadih, where jadal is defined simply as transferring the opponent from one madhhab to another by way of proof, with some variant saying 'from a madhhab to another.' The author then notes that Ibn 'Aqil appears to have distinguished between two definitions: one of his own, which allows any method of transfer, and another that seems to belong to others, which emphasizes proof. This reveals an apparent contradiction in Ibn 'Aqil's own work, as the first definition required proof but the second includes non-proof methods. The passage is an expansion-probe, clarifying the nuances in defining the art of dialectic.

Key Terms
الجدل
الحجة

A) He claims the first definition is his own and the second is from others
B) He claims both are his own and they are identical
C) He claims the second definition is a correction of the first

Key Takeaway
Understanding a scholar's definitions requires attention to context and possible evolution of thought; apparent contradictions may reflect different purposes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find another example in the Islamic intellectual tradition where a scholar gives two definitions of the same term that appear contradictory. Explain how later commentators resolved the tension (e.g., definitions of 'ijtihad' by al-Shafi'i or 'ijma').

In Ibn 'Aqil's case, which definition do you think aligns better with the actual practice of jadal in Islamic history? Support your answer with evidence.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
7 min
Al-Tufi's Dual Definition of Jadal
Al-Tufi defines jadal both as an artful law and as the act of replying the opponent with proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-Tufi's theoretical and practical definitions of jadal and identify the key components of each definition.

Core Idea
Jadal is both a law of investigation and an act of refutation – a double nature.

Al-Tufi offers a unique definition of jadal that reflects its maturation in usul al-fiqh. Unlike earlier scholars who treated jadal purely as a practical disputation, al-Tufi presents it both as a theoretical 'artificial law' (qanun sina'i) that governs investigation, and as the concrete act of repelling an opponent from his opinion by proof. This lesson unpacks these two dimensions and the nuance about the opponent's possible advocacy role.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of jadal
Locate in the Arabic where al-Tufi first gives the technical definition (rasm) as 'an artificial law that knows the states of investigations from error and correctness' and then his own preferred definition as 'repulsion of the opponent from his opinion to another by proof.' These represent the theoretical and practical aspects respectively.
The first definition emphasizes the regulative function; the second emphasizes the act of refutation.
2
Analyse the theoretical definition
Break down the first definition: what is the genus (artificial law) and what is the differentia (that identifies error/correctness and repels doubt)? Note that it positions jadal as a meta-discipline for evaluating investigations.
'Artificial law' (qanun sina'i) is a term borrowed from logic, indicating a structured, artful system.
3
Analyse the practical definition
Examine the second definition: the act of repelling (radd) the opponent from his opinion to another by proof (hujja). Why does al-Tufi say 'to another' and not necessarily to the opponent's own view? This introduces a key subtlety.
The opponent may be arguing on behalf of someone else; the aim is refutation, not conversion.
4
Compare with earlier definitions
Al-Tufi's definition is said to be different from previous ones because it presents jadal as a 'standing science' (ilm qa'im) and 'intellectual laws' (qawanin fikriyya), not merely practical behavior. Identify how this shift reflects the development of usul al-fiqh.
The commentator attributes this change to the maturation of usul al-fiqh as a theoretical discipline.
5
Evaluate the completeness of the definition
Check whether al-Tufi's definition covers all essential aspects of jadal: does it include both the theoretical framework and the practical application? Does it exclude anything that should not be jadal? Consider the role of intention and the possibility of defending another's view.
Al-Tufi's inclusion of the possibility of defending another's madhhab shows attention to real scholarly practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الطوفي : أما رسم الجدل في الاصطلاح، فقيل : هو قانون صناعي يعرف أحوال المباحث من الخطأ والصواب على وجه يدفع عن نفس الناظر والمناظر الشك والارتياب . قلت [ القائل هو الطوفي]: ولك أن تقول فيه : إنه رد الخصم عن رأيه إلى غيره بالحجة، أو يقال : علم أو آلة يتوصل بها إلى قتل الخصم عن رأيه إلى غيره بالدليل ؛ وإنما قلنا عن رأيه إلى غيره، ولم نقل إلى رأي خصمه المناظر له، لأن الخصم قد يناظر عن مذهب غيره إعانة لذلك الغير كالحنبلي ينصر مذهب بعض الطوائف الثلاثة وقد يكون مقصوده إفساد مذهب الخصم لا تصحيح مذهبه هو، أي مذهب كان ويقابل به مذهب خصمه وبه يحل مقصوده . الملاحظ على تعريف الطوفي هذا أنه : فلا جرم يرجح يعرف الجدل باعتباره علما قائما و باعتباره قوانين فكرية ينضبط بها المتناظرون والمختلفون في أفكارهم حين يدافع كل منهم عن فكرته؛ وهذا بخلاف كل التعريفات السابقة التي قدمت الجدل على أنه سلوك عملي يجري بين المختلفين حين يدافع كل منهم عن فكرته أو رأيه .. ولا شك أن للتطور الفكري في دراسة أصول الفقه دوره في هذا المنهج في تعريف الجدل.
Al-Tufi said: As for the description of jadal in terminology, it is said: it is an artificial law that knows the states of investigations from error and correctness in a manner that repels from the self of the viewer and the disputant doubt and suspicion. I [the speaker is al-Tufi] said: And you can say about it: it is the repulsion of the opponent from his opinion to another by proof. Or it is said: a science or instrument by which one reaches the killing of the opponent from his opinion to another by evidence. And we only said 'from his opinion to another' and we did not say 'to the opinion of his opponent the disputant' because the opponent may argue for the madhhab of another, assisting that other, like the Hanbali who supports the madhhab of some of the three schools, and his intention may be to corrupt the madhhab of the opponent, not to correct his own madhhab, whichever madhhab it may be, and he meets the madhhab of his opponent with it and thereby achieves his purpose. The observation on this definition of al-Tufi is that: without doubt he is inclined to define jadal as a standing science and as intellectual laws by which the disputants and those who differ in their ideas are disciplined when each of them defends his idea. And this is contrary to all the previous definitions that presented jadal as a practical behavior that occurs between those who differ when each defends his idea or opinion. And there is no doubt that intellectual development in the study of usul al-fiqh has its role in this method of defining jadal.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of jadal by Jalal al-Din al-Tufi and then provides an analysis. The text begins with a technical description (rasm) of jadal in terminology: it is an 'artificial law' (qanun sina'i) that identifies the states of investigations in terms of error and correctness, in a way that frees the mind of the seeker and the disputant from doubt and suspicion. Al-Tufi then offers his own formulation: that jadal is the repulsion of the opponent from his opinion to another by proof. He specifies that he says 'from his opinion to another' rather than 'to the opinion of his opponent the disputant,' because the opponent may be arguing on behalf of another school (like a Hanbali defending a Shafi'i view) and his aim might be to corrupt the opponent's position rather than to correct his own. This nuance addresses situations where the disputant is not personally committed to the view he advocates. After presenting the definition, the author observes that al-Tufi's definition is distinct because it presents jadal both as a theoretical science (an established body of knowledge) and as a set of intellectual laws that govern disputation, rather than a mere practical behavior. This contrasts with earlier definitions that treated jadal simply as a practical exchange. The author ties this shift to the development of usul al-fiqh, implying that the theoretical approach emerged from studying the method of legal argumentation. The passage is an expansion-probe that clarifies how al-Tufi's definition differs from earlier ones and introduces a formal dimension to jadal.

Key Terms
الجدل
القانون الصناعي
الرد
قتل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does al-Tufi say 'repulsion from his opinion to another' instead of 'to the opinion of his opponent'?
A) Because the opponent may not have a clear opinion
B) Because the disputant may be arguing on behalf of someone else, and his goal may be to refute rather than to convert the opponent to his own view
C) Because the aim is always to reach a compromise
D) Because the opponent's opinion is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
Al-Tufi's definition of jadal is innovative because it combines a theoretical regulative aspect (artificial law) with a practical act of refutation, reflecting the maturation of usul al-fiqh.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
